Wilkerson v. Department of Health & Soc. Servs., Div. of Family & Youth Servs.


Supreme Court No. S-8652, No. 5216


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


993 P.2d 1018; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 166



December 17, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Superior Court No. 3AN-97-5466 CI. Sigurd E. Murphy, Judge pro tem.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alaska, Third Judicial Department, Anchorage, that he had waived his constitutional challenges to Alaska Admin. Code tit. 7, § 50.210(c)(5), because his brief, alleging due process and equal protection violations, was inadequate.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that Alaska Admin. Code tit. 7, § 50.210(c)(5), which denied foster care licenses to those who had been charged with or convicted of certain crimes, arbitrarily distinguished between applicants who had been charged with crimes but not convicted and those who had not been charged, in violation of Alaska Const. art. I, § 1. The court first noted that appellant's interest in the license was merely economic, so that it was only entitled to minimal protection. The court then found that the regulation's distinction bore a substantial relationship to the state's compelling interest in protecting foster children, because it provided an efficient means of protecting the children from the risk of being placed with someone who had actually committed a crime but had not been convicted. For the same reasons, the court also found that the regulation did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that appellant's brief was adequate, but that the regulation was constitutional, because it efficiently furthered the state's compelling interest in the welfare of its children by efficiently eliminating foster care applicants who posed a potential risk.

 

2 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Halter v. State


Supreme Court No. S-8245, No. 5198


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


990 P.2d 1035; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 145



November 5, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, Sen K. Tan, Judge. Superior Court No. 3KO-94-708 CI.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, which upheld appellee's determination that appellant's failure to adequately chart prescriptions amounted to professional incompetence under Alaska Stat. § 08.64.326(a)(8)(A).


OVERVIEW: Appellee had sanctioned appellant under Alaska Stat. § 08.64.326(a)(8)(A) for violating standards of professional competence. The basis of appellee's decision was that appellant's failure to adequately chart prescriptions for controlled substances amounted to professional incompetence. Appellant contended that Alaska Stat. § 08.64.326(a)(8)(A) was vague as applied to him because there were no specific regulations about record-keeping standards to provide notice of what was required. On appeal, the court held that the statute was not vague as applied to appellant since the term "incompetence or unskillfulness" was not so uncertain as to require further rulemaking. Since testimony was presented which indicated that appellant's record-keeping was inadequate, there was substantial evidence on the record to support appellee's finding that appellant was professionally incompetent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision upholding appellee's determination since the professional competence statute was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to appellant and because there was substantial evidence on the record which supported appellee's determination.

 

3 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Legislative Council v. Knowles


Supreme Court Nos. S-8143, S-8144, No. 5185


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


988 P.2d 604; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 132



October 1, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-96-1276 CI.



DISPOSITION: VACATED the superior court's order declaring C.S.S.B. 162 invalid and REMANDED for entry of an order of dismissal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff governor and defendants legislative council and legislators cross appealed Superior Court, First Judicial District, Juneau (Alaska), rulings dismissing defendants' dismissal motion made pursuant to Alaska Const. art. III, § 16, and granting plaintiff's motion for summary judgment in action arising from defendants' override of plaintiff's veto of a bill.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff governor sued defendants, legislative council and legislators, claiming that defendants' override of a bill vetoed by the plaintiff was untimely and did not affect his veto. The trial court agreed, declaring the override vote invalid and the veto effective. Although a substantially identical law passed in the subsequent legislative session, the court found that the public interest exception to the mootness doctrine applied and reversed, holding that, although formally filed in plaintiff governor's name against the defendants, the suit was in substance brought in the name of the state against the legislature. Because Alaska Const. art. III, § 16 expressly forbade such actions, and because the substance of the suit infringed upon the legislature's constitutional domain in precisely the manner that the Constitution's drafters intended to prohibit, the court vacated the plaintiff's summary judgment and directed dismissal of the action.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of plaintiff vacated and case remanded for dismissal because the action, although filed by plaintiff governor, was an unconstitutional suit against defendants, legislative council and legislators.

 

4 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Kilpper v. Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Div.


Supreme Court No. S-7696, No. 5138


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


983 P.2d 729; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 84



July 2, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Rene J. Gonzalez, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-9414 CI.



DISPOSITION: Case REMANDED for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted him relief from prospective liability for child support of a child of which he acknowledged paternity but later proved he did not father, but it found him liable for child support arrearages from the time of the child's birth through disestablishment of paternity.


OVERVIEW: Appellant acknowledged paternity of a child that he did not father. He did not live with the mother or child, and the mother began to receive public assistance. The Child Support Enforcement Division filed a motion requesting entry of judgment against appellant for child support arrearages. Appellant filed a motion to disestablish paternity, which the court granted prospectively. Appellant claimed that he did not understand that by acknowledging paternity he could be liable for child support and would not have acknowledged it had he known. The court found that he was nevertheless liable for the arrearages. The supreme court reversed the judgment holding that the trial court erred in not holding a hearing on appellant's equitable defense to the entry of the judgment for arrearages.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment finding appellant liable for child support arrearages and remanded the case for hearing on appellant's equitable defense to the claimed arrearages because the statute entitled him to a hearing on any defense raised in his response to the Child Support Enforcement Division request for entry of judgment.

 

5 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Henash v. Ipalook


Supreme Court Nos. S-8248, S-8268, No. 5119


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


985 P.2d 442; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 64; 138 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P58,666



May 21, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied November 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 7093.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Jay Hodges, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-95-2745 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED in part, VACATED in part and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties cross-appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which awarded unpaid overtime pay and liquidated damages pursuant to appellee employee's claim under the Alaska Wage and Hour Act, Alaska Stat. § 23.10.110, and which ruled in favor of appellee on appellant employer's counterclaim for breach of fiduciary duty.


OVERVIEW: Appellee filed a claim for unpaid overtime and liquidated damages against appellant former employer, pursuant to the Alaska Wage and Hour Act, Alaska Stat. § 23.10.110, alleging that her position was improperly classified as exempt from overtime. Appellant counterclaimed, alleging that appellee had breached her fiduciary duty to appellant during her prior tenure as president. The trial court held that appellee's position was not exempt from overtime and that she had not breached her fiduciary duty. The trial court awarded unpaid overtime and an equal amount in liquidated damages. On cross-appeals, appellant challenged the constitutionality of § 23.10.110, and appellee challenged the amount of the award. The court affirmed judgment for appellee, but reversed the damages award. The court held that the mandatory award of liquidated damages under § 23.10.110 did not violate substantive due process, procedural due process, or equal protection. The court vacated the damages award and remanded for the trial court to re-calculate appellee's overtime compensation rate and to determine the applicability of Alaska Stat. § 09.30.070(b), limiting the award of prejudgment interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's findings that appellee did not breach her fiduciary duties and rejected appellant's arguments that the award of mandatory liquidated damages under the Alaska Wage and Hour Act was unconstitutional. The court vacated and remanded the damages award for re-calculation of appellee's overtime compensation rate and for a determination of whether a statutory limitation on prejudgment interest applied to this action.

 

6 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Abbott v. State


Supreme Court No. S-7390, No. 5115


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


979 P.2d 994; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 59; 1999 AMC 2212



May 14, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-94-1187 CI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, granting summary judgment to appellees and awarding attorney's fees to appellee in appellant's suit for benefits under Federal Employers' Liability Act, 45 U.S.C.S. §§ 51-60.


OVERVIEW: Appellant suffered burns while working as a cook aboard appellee's ship. She received workers' compensation benefits in accordance with the collective bargaining agreement (CBA) between appellee and her union. A recent case prevented enforcement of the CBA in effect when appellant was injured. Appellant claimed that she first learned of the possibility of pursuing traditional maritime remedies when she received an adjuster's letter. A decision dismissing appellant's suit under Federal Employers' Liability Act, 45 U.S.C.S. §§ 51-60, and awarding attorney's fees to appellee was reversed and remanded. Federal law applied in deciding questions as to the tolling of the limitation provision. Appellant, by exercising reasonable diligence, could not have discovered essential information bearing on her claim until the adjuster's letter arrived. Appellant's workers' compensation injury report gave notice of her injury and claims. The suspension approach and the reasonable time approach gave appellant the entire three-year limitations period in which to file her complaint for her traditional maritime remedies. The limitations period started running when appellant got the letter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision dismissing the suit and awarding attorney's fees because the statutes of limitations were equitably tolled and appellant's maritime claims were not time barred.

 

7 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Ferguson v. Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Div. ex rel. P.G.


Supreme Court No. S-8123, No. 5110


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


977 P.2d 95; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 55



April 30, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial District, Kotzebue, Richard H. Erlich, Judge. Superior Court No. 2KB-91-00039 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial Circuit, which vacated the paternity judgment against him, but refused to extinguish accrued child support arrearages in response to appellant's motion for relief pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 60(b)(5).


OVERVIEW: Appellant admitted he was the father of a child, and the trial court accordingly entered a paternity judgment against him. Six years later, genetic testing excluded appellant as the father. Seeking relief under Alaska R. Civ. P. 60(b)(5), appellant filed a motion to vacate the judgment of paternity, arguing that the judgment should no longer have prospective application. The trial court vacated the paternity judgment, but refused to extinguish accrued child support arrearages. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that appellant was not entitled to retroactive relief under Rule 60(b)(5). The court concluded that Rule 60(b)(5) differentiated between prospective assessment for child support payment in the future and prospective collection of arrearages that resulted from a failure to pay past-due support. The court noted that an alternative distinction would give child support obligors incentive not to pay child support and not to resolve paternity disputes promptly. The court further concluded that the state would not be unjustly enriched by collecting arrearages which had accrued before appellant's paternity was disestablished.


OUTCOME: Because appellant sought and was granted relief under a rule allowing relief from prospective application of the paternity judgment against him, the court affirmed the trial court judgment requiring appellant to pay accrued child support arrearages.

 

8 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Alaska Civil Liberties Union


Supreme Court No. S-8778, No. 5108


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


978 P.2d 597; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 52



April 16, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied February 22, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1015.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Michael L. Wolverton, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-97-5289 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which held Alaska's 1996 campaign finance reform statute, Senate Bill 191, to be unconstitutional after finding it to have violated rights of free speech and association by restricting campaign contributions and expenditures for state and local elections.


OVERVIEW: The Alaska legislature reformed Alaska's campaign financing statutes in 1996 by enacting Senate Bill 191 (Act). Appellee, the Alaska Civil Liberties Union, sued the state, seeking a judgment that would declare that parts of the Act violated rights of free speech and association by restricting campaign contributions and expenditures for state and local elections. The court below accepted appellee's arguments and held the Act unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that appellant had a legitimate interest in preventing corruption or the appearance of corruption in state election campaigns. The court also found that most of the challenged provisions were narrowly tailored to achieve that interest. The judgment of the lower court was reversed as to several of the Act's provisions, including the ban on independent expenditures by non-group entities; the ban on contributions by corporations; the limits on individual contributions; and the restriction on contributions by nonresidents. However, the judgment of the lower court was affirmed to the extent that it related to the invalidity of the Act's pre-election year and legislative session contribution bans.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding the state's finance reform statute unconstitutional was reversed as to a majority of the statute's provisions because appellant was found to have had a legitimate interest in preventing corruption or the appearance of corruption in state elections. The lower court's judgment was affirmed as it related to the invalidity of pre-election year and legislative session contribution bans.

 

9 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Vinzant v. Elam


Supreme Court No. S-8564, No. 5094


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


977 P.2d 84; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 38



March 19, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Harold M. Brown, Judge. Superior Court No. 3KN-95-33 CI.



DISPOSITION: VACATED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, which modified a child custody order after a show cause hearing. Appellant alleged that the modification violated his right to due process.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father and appellee mother were divorced and the trial court granted joint legal custody but gave primary physical custody to appellee. The parties thereafter agreed to transfer the children's primary physical custody to appellant. The trial court approved the change. Thereafter, appellee moved to enforce the visitation order, claiming that appellant failed to make the children available for visitation. The trial court issued a show cause order and scheduled a contempt hearing, at which it awarded sole legal and primary physical custody to appellee. Appellant sought review. The court vacated the custody modification order and remanded, finding that appellant's due process rights were violated because neither party had been put on notice that a change in custody was at issue at the show cause hearing. The court found that the Alaska Constitution required that parties in a custody proceeding receive adequate notice and a hearing that granted them the opportunity to present the quantum of evidence needed to make an informed and principled determination. The court also found that the trial court failed to consider the statutory factors for modification of custody.


OUTCOME: The modification of custody order was vacated and the matter remanded, on the grounds that appellant's rights of due process were violated by the lack of notice that his custody rights were at issue at the show cause hearing that preceded entry of the modification order. The trial court did not address the statutory factors that were potentially relevant to the children's best interests.

 

10 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Beluga Mining Co. v. Department of Natural Resources


Supreme Court No. S-8256, No. 5082


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


973 P.2d 570; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 22



February 19, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, John Reese, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-96-2686 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mining company sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, that dismissed its suit against appellee State for economic losses resulting from the State's failure to issue mining leases on land held by a public trust.


OVERVIEW: Appellant held mining claims located on lands held by a public trust but was unable to mine its claims due to a preliminary injunction entered in litigation over appellee State's handling of trust lands. Appellant sought judicial review after the lower court dismissed its suit for economic losses and argued that the State unconstitutionally took its rights to mine the land, depriving it of private property without just compensation. The court disagreed and held that the takings doctrine was not implicated because the State did not deprive appellant of any property right. The court reasoned that appellant had no right to mine the land because its rights were prospective and contingent on the existing claims of the public trust. The court also held there was no per se taking because there was no physical invasion of appellant's property nor was there any denial of all economically feasible use of the property since the injunction only delayed appellant's ability to obtain permission to mine the property. In addition, the court found there was no contract between appellant and the State and therefore no breach of contract. The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's suit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's suit for economic losses after holding appellant's rights to mine the land were prospective and contingent and appellee State was not guilty of an unconstitutional taking of private property.

 

11 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Church v. Department of Revenue


Supreme Court No. S-7606, No. 5079


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


973 P.2d 1125; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 20



February 12, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Homer, Charles K. Cranston, Judge. Superior Court No. 3HO-95-8 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Homer, which affirmed an agency decision that denied appellant's application for a permanent fund dividend under Alaska Stat. § 43.23.095(8) and Alaska Admin. Code tit. 15, 23.163(c) (1997), for a year in which he was absent from the state for more than 180 days.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was absent from the state for more than 180 days in one year to care for his infirm mother. Holding that Alaska Admin. Code tit. 15, § 23.163(c) (1997) was not arbitrary or capricious, and that its application to appellant was not unconstitutional, the court affirmed the judgment. Reviewing the agency's determination independently, the court held that § 23.163 did not circumvent the intent of Alaska Stat. § 43.23.015(a). The court noted that the regulation was authorized by § 43.23.015(a), and was consistent with the purpose of Alaska Stat. § 43.23.095(8), which was to limit payment of dividends to permanent residents, and to ease the administrative burden of attempting to determine what treatment level was sufficient to merit eligibility for a permanent fund dividend (PFD). The court held that § 23.163(c) as applied to appellant did not violate procedural due process. Noting the absence of a fact dispute, the court held that appellant was not entitled to an agency hearing. The court also held that § 43.23.095(8) and § 23.163(c) did not violate appellant's rights to substantive due process, equal protection of the laws, or his right to travel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against appellant. The court held that the regulation imposing residency duration qualifications on the right to receive permanent fund dividends (PFDs) was consistent with the enabling statute and the legislature's intention to limit PFDs to residents and ease the administrative burden of determining who was entitled to the dividends. The court also held that the regulation was not unconstitutional.

 

12 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. UPS


Supreme Court No. S-8376, No. 5078


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


975 P.2d 684; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 18



February 5, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Sigurd E. Murphy, Judge, pro tem. Superior Court No. 3AN-96-8706 CI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska affirming a decision of the workers' compensation board to deviate from Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220 and lower appellant's temporary total disability benefits and to dismiss appellant's claims for attorney fees and costs.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee was injured on the job and sought workers' compensation benefits from appellee employer. She had been a full-time employee, but became a part-time employee working double shifts shortly before her accident because her former employer declared bankruptcy and terminated her. After her accident, she double-shifted only occasionally. The workers' compensation board deviated from Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220 because it believed it would be unfair to appellee to classify appellant as full-time. The superior court affirmed the board's decision, but the court reversed the superior court and the board. The board was correct to apply the version of § 23.30.220(a) in effect at the time of appellant's injury. The board erred, however, when it refused to consider appellant's double-shifting to determine her earning potential. Thus, the board lacked substantial evidence for its decision to deviate from the formula contained in § 23.30.220. The court remanded so that the board could award appellant compensation calculated by the statutory formula, attorney fees, and costs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment because appellee did not meet its burden of showing that the statutory formula for determining appellant's temporary total disability benefits inadequately predicted appellant's probable wages had she not been hurt on the job, and the court remanded with instructions for the board to award appellant compensation under the statutory formula and to award her attorney fees and costs.

 

13 of 606 DOCUMENTS


R.J.M. v. Department of Health & Soc. Servs.


Supreme Court No. S-8525, Supreme Court No. S-8526, No. 5072


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


973 P.2d 79; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 14



January 29, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court Nos. 4 FA- 91- 182 CP, 4FA-96-170 CP.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which found them unwilling to care for their son and terminated their parental rights under the child in need of aid statute, Alaska Stat. § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A).


OVERVIEW: In consolidated cases, the court first affirmed the termination of appellant mother's parental rights pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A). The court found that the trial court's ruling that appellant mother was unwilling to care for her child was not clearly erroneous. The court noted that appellant mother's mental illness, and her refusal to obtain treatment for it, was the principal evidence to support this finding, on the rationale that a parent objectively willing to provide care would have been willing to address the condition which precluded the provision of care. The court next affirmed the termination of appellant father's parental rights. The court found evidence of appellant father's unwillingness, including absenteeism, emotional neglect, and a refusal to accept physical custody of his child after a foster placement, so that the trial court's finding was not clearly erroneous. The court also held that appellant father's "willingness by declaration only" to be inadequate in light of his deeds. The court held that the trial court did not err in declining to hear new evidence on a prior remand, and held that § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A) was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the termination of appellant parents' parental rights. The court held that appellant mother's unwillingness to seek treatment for her mental illness demonstrated an unwillingness to care for her child. The court also held that appellant father's absenteeism, emotional neglect, and refusal to accept custody of the child after a foster placement adequately supported the termination of his rights.

 

14 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Brooks v. Wright


Supreme Court No. S-8676, No. 5066, Supreme Court No. S-8685


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


971 P.2d 1025; 1999 Alas. LEXIS 6



January 15, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Ralph R. Beistline, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-97-879 CI. Superior Court No. 4FA-97-2337 CI.



DISPOSITION: REMANDED AND VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, that granted summary judgment to appellees, concerned citizens and community organizations who had sought to remove an initiative prohibiting use of snares to trap wolves from the general election ballot, decertified the wolf snare ballot initiative, and enjoined placement of the initiative on the general election ballot.


OVERVIEW: A ballot initiative was certified that would, if passed, criminalize both the use of snares to trap wolves and the possession, sale, or purchase of wolf pelts known to have been taken by snare. Appellees, concerned citizens and community organizations, filed suit in the trial court and sought to remove the initiative prohibiting use of snares to trap wolves from the general election ballot contending that it was not constitutional. The trial court granted the appellees' motion for summary judgment and enjoinment of the ballot measure and decertified the wolf snare ballot initiative. The state filed an appeal which was expedited. The court, applying a de novo standard of review, examined the constitutionality of using the ballot initiative process to prohibit wolf snare traps. The court reversed the trial court's order of summary judgment against the state and its injunction, and placed the initiative back on the general election ballot because the prohibition of wolf snare traps was an appropriate subject for a ballot initiative given the Alaska Constitution's mandate for the state to manage its natural resources.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order of summary judgment against the state and placed the wolf snare initiative back on the general election ballot because the prohibition of wolf snare traps was an appropriate subject for a ballot initiative. The court also concluded that the legislature did not have exclusive law-making powers over natural resources issues because of the state's constitutionally mandated management role over wildlife.

 

15 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Sonneman v. State


Supreme Court No. S-7851, No. 5058


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


969 P.2d 632; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 176



December 24, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-96-636 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of the order of the Superior Court, First Judicial District, Juneau (Alaska), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, state, lieutenant governor, and division of elections director, in appellant's action which challenged the constitutionality of an amendment to a candidate ballot position statute.


OVERVIEW: Appellant candidate challenged the constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 15.15.030(6), an amendment that required candidates' names be placed randomly on election ballots. The lower court granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, state, lieutenant governor, and division of elections director. On appeal, the court affirmed the order of the lower court that granted summary judgment to appellees. The court found that appellant had taxpayer standing to challenge the constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 15.15.030(6). The court reasoned that even if positional bias existed, the statute did not impermissibly burden the right to vote because it was a reasonable and nondiscriminatory restriction which was designed to promote appellee state's important interests in government economy and prevention of voter confusion. The court held that the statute did not violate the "will of the people" or Alaska Const. art. I, § 5.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court that granted summary judgment to appellees, state, lieutenant governor, and division of elections director, in appellant candidate's challenge of a ballot placement statute. The court found that appellant had citizen-taxpayer standing to challenge the statute, the statute did not impermissibly burden the right to vote, and it did not violate the "will of the people" or the Alaska Constitution.

 

16 of 606 DOCUMENTS


In re K.A.H.


Supreme Court No. S-7761, No. 5049


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


967 P.2d 91; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 166



November 27, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied October 4, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 5005.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, Joan M. Woodward, Judge. Superior Court Nos. 3KO-93-45/55 PR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged the order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, which denied appellant's request for reimbursement from settlement funds for money advanced to his client for living expenses pursuant to Alaska R. Prof. Conduct 1.8e.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney represented client in a wrongful death action. In connection with this litigation, appellant advanced his client money for living expenses. When the action settled, appellant requested reimbursement of the loans from the settlement fund. The lower court denied the request, holding that Alaska R. Prof. Conduct 1.8e prohibited counsel from financially assisting a client except for advancement of court fees and litigation expenses. Appellant challenged this ruling, contending that Alaska R. Prof. Conduct 1.8e permitted loans for living expenses, or that it unconstitutionally impeded court access and should be changed, that Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.2 required clarification, and that he deserved attorney's fees. The court affirmed the lower court's order, concluding that the rule prohibited counsel's loan to his client and that it did not impede court access because it allowed assistance with court costs and litigation expenses. Appeal was an improper procedure for changing the rule. Appellant waived his challenge to Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.2 by inadequate briefing. Appellant was not a prevailing party entitled to attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of appellant attorney's request because a rule of professional conduct prohibited counsel from loaning his client money for living expenses and this rule did not unconstitutionally infringe upon court access. Moreover, appellant waived his challenge to settlement procedures by failing to brief the issue, and appellant was not entitled to attorney's fees because he was not a prevailing party.

 

17 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Brady v. State


Supreme Court No. S-7916, No. 5037


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


965 P.2d 1; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 154



October 9, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied March 22, 1999, Reported at: 1999 U.S. LEXIS 2020.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Peter A. Michalski, Judge. Superior Court Nos. 3AN-94-2951 CI / 3AN-94-2807 CI (Consolidated).



DISPOSITION: We AFFIRM the judgment in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicants, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted two summary judgments in favor of appellees, state and its officials, dismissing all of appellants' claims in their consolidated suits. Appellants filed their action after their applications for negotiated timber sale were denied.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicants, filed actions against appellees, state and its officials, for denying appellants' application to buy the right to harvest dead and dying trees. Appellants had hoped to show that harvesting could help stanch a beetle epidemic that was decimating forests. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of appellees. The court held that there was no legally enforceable contract because when appellee state promised to entertain applications, it promised at most to negotiate. Appellee state never objectively manifested an intent to be bound. Moreover, appellee officials' various comments and silences could not, as a matter of law, have manifested unequivocal acceptance. Because there was no promise to sell, and appellee state negotiated in good faith, the court held that the rule of promissory estoppel did not apply. Appellants' quantum meruit claim was rejected because appellants' offered to help prepare a site-specific forest land-use plan, and never said, until submitting an invoice, that payment was expected. The court found that appellants volunteered to do the work in order to gain a business advantage.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of two summary judgment in favor of appellees, state and its officials, dismissing all of appellant applicants' claims. The court held that there was no legally enforceable contract for negotiated timber sale. The court observed that appellee state never objectively manifested an intent to be bound, and appellee officials' comments and silences could not have manifested unequivocal acceptance.

Reid v. Williams


Supreme Court No. S-7839, No. 5034


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


964 P.2d 453; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 150



October 2, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-94-5234 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient sought review of the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage (Alaska), which applied Alaska Stat. § 09.55.548(b) to subtract appellant's medical expenses paid by his insurer from the jury's medical expenses award and which denied appellant an award of enhanced attorney fees in appellant's medical malpractice action against appellee treating doctor.


OVERVIEW: Appellant patient brought an action against appellee treating doctor for medical malpractice and prevailed at trial. The superior court applied Alaska Stat. § 09.55.548(b) to subtract appellant's medical expenses paid by his insurer from the jury's medical expenses award. The superior court concluded that it was a reasonable legislative response to a perceived medical malpractice insurance crisis. Appellant contended that § 09.55.548(b) violated his substantive due process and equal protection rights. Appellant also argued that he should have been awarded enhanced attorney fees. The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court and held that § 09.55.548(b) did not violate appellant's substantive due process rights or his equal protection rights because the statute was reasonably related to the stated purpose of reducing the cost of medical malpractice insurance. This reduction would serve a legitimate public purpose. The court concluded that appellant failed to show that appellee had acted in bad faith or reprehensible conduct and, therefore, appellant was not entitled to an award of enhanced attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court, which applied an Alaska statute to subtract appellant's medical expenses paid by his insurer from the jury's medical expenses award and to deny appellant patient an award for enhanced attorney fees in appellant's medical malpractice action. The court held that the subtraction of appellant's medical expenses was appropriate because the statute served a legitimate public purpose.

Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Div. v. Beans


Supreme Court No. S-8322, No. 5030


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


965 P.2d 725; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 145



September 4, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Dan A. Hensley, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-97-3329 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED only to the extent that we find AS 25.27.246(i) to be ineffective to prevent a litigant from seeking judicial relief based on inability to pay. Otherwise, we REVERSED and REMANDED for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee obligor father and held that Alaska Stat. § 25.27.246, which provided for the suspension of delinquent child support obligors' driver's licenses, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state notified appellee obligor father that his driver's license was to be suspended, pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 25.27.246, unless he complied with a child support payment order. Appellee filed suit and contended that § 25.27.246 was unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of appellee. On appeal, the court affirmed only the part of the judgment that held that Alaska Stat. § 25.27.246(i) did not prevent appellee from seeking judicial relief based on inability to pay. The court reversed and remanded and held that the statute clearly articulated a legitimate public policy for targeting the driver's licenses of delinquent obligors because appellant needed to collect child support from all obligors. The sanction of losing a license was rational, and as soon as appellee began to comply with a payment agreement, then appellant was to release his license. Appellee was not denied due process or equal protection of the laws because appellant could not have required payments beyond his best efforts. There was no right to a jury trial because appellee was permitted to have a judge review his claims.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee obligor father, and remanded to the trial court. The court held that the statute, which allowed appellant state to suspend delinquent child support obligors' driver's licenses, was constitutional, because it did not require payment beyond appellee's best efforts.

Titus v. State


Supreme Court No. S-8059, No. 5021


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


963 P.2d 258; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 138



August 14, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Court of Appeals No. A-5639. Superior Court No. 4FA-S93-2713 CR.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which reversed the superior court's ruling that granted petitioner a new trial after he had been convicted of first-degree sexual assault. Petitioner alleged jury misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of first-degree sexual assault. Petitioner moved for an evidentiary hearing to investigate potential jury misconduct. At the hearing, members of his jury testified that they had discussed matters not presented at trial, including the fact that petitioner might have been drinking the night of the alleged rape. The superior court granted petitioner a new trial, but the court of appeals reversed. It ruled that Alaska R. Evid. 606(b) barred the superior court from accepting the jurors' testimony about their deliberations because pre-existing juror knowledge did not qualify for the rule's extraneous prejudicial information exception. The court reversed the court of appeals and concluded that pre-existing juror knowledge about the facts of petitioner's alleged crime did constitute extraneous prejudicial information within the meaning of Rule 606(b). However, because it was unclear if the comments made in the jury room were based on actual knowledge about the facts and circumstances of the alleged crime, the court remanded to the superior court for further findings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals, which reversed the superior court's ruling that granted petitioner a new trial in his conviction for first-degree sexual assault. The court held that petitioner's grant of a new trial was appropriate because pre-existing juror knowledge about the facts of petitioner's alleged crime constituted extraneous prejudicial information within the meaning of Alaska's rules of evidence.

 

21 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Kenai Peninsula Borough v. Arndt


Supreme Court No. S-7776, No. 4989


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


958 P.2d 1101; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 99



May 22, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Jonathan H. Link, Judge. Superior Court No. 3KN-92-406 CI.

This Opinion Reissued by the Court for Withdrawn Opinion of May 8, 1998, Previously Reported at: 1998 Alas. LEXIS 93.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxing authority sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, which reversed the district court's entry of summary judgment, holding respondent tax obligor liable for taxes accrued on a ship after he sold it, and holding that the due process and commerce clauses required reduction of the tax to reflect the vessel's post-assessment sale and its departure from borough waters.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxing authority sought to collect a full year's tax, which respondent tax obligor refused to pay, on his locally-harbored ship, which he owned on the first day of the tax year, the assessment date, but sold a few months later. The superior court, relying on U.S. Const. amend. XIV, cl. 1 and art. I, § 8, cl. 3, ordered apportionment of the tax to reflect the portion of the year during which respondent owned the ship. The court held that personal property tax did not "accrue" day by day. The system of property taxation determined the principal attributes of tax status, value, situs, and ownership, as of January 1 each year; once tax status was determined, the property tax itself became fixed each year as of that date. The tax "accrued" in full each year on January 1. Apportionment of respondent's tax for the ship was not necessary to avoid an unconstitutional taking of his property or to prevent the possibility of multiple taxation because post-assessment changes were taken into account the next tax year, and the retrospective property tax approach was applied uniformly throughout the state and nationwide.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment and remanded the case to the district court to reenter judgment for petitioner taxing authority and holding respondent tax obligor liable for a full year's taxes on a ship after he had sold it. The court held that the ship's tax status became fixed for the full tax year on the assessment date. Apportionment of respondent's tax was not necessary to avoid an unconstitutional taking or to prevent multiple taxation.

Sielak v. State


Supreme Court No. S-8215, No. 4985


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


958 P.2d 438; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 87



May 15, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Niesje J. Steinkruger, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-96-759 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which ordered him to pay child support. Appellant claimed that the child was not his and sought a determination of the issue by appellee, the Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Division.


OVERVIEW: After two years of marriage, appellant father separated from his wife. His wife moved in with another man and more than a year later gave birth to a child. Because appellant was still married to his wife, appellant's name appeared on the child's birth certificate. Appellee, the Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Division, issued an order that appellant was to pay support for the child and six years later upon review of the order, appellee modified its order and again ordered appellant to pay support for the child. Upon hearing, appellee affirmed its support determination and held that it had no jurisdiction to disestablish paternity and that support was required unless a court disestablished appellant as the child's father. That decision was affirmed by the lower court and the court affirmed. The court held that Alaska Stat. § 25.27.166 did not change the legal consequences of actions taken by appellant before it was passed because he was not in the class of fathers who could have taken advantage of the new authority authorized by it. Further, the court held that appellee lacked statutory authority to disestablish paternity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that held that appellee, the Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Division, was without jurisdiction to disestablish paternity. The court remanded the case to the superior court with directions to conduct a paternity adjudication to determine whether appellant father was the father of the child for whom he was ordered to pay child support.

Grober v. Department of Revenue, Child Support Enforcement Div. ex rel. C.J.W.


Supreme Court No. S-7353, No. 4981


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


956 P.2d 1230; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 91



May 8, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] This Opinion Substituted by the Court for Withdrawn Opinion of March 6, 1998, Previously Reported at: 1998 Alas. LEXIS 26.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-92-1419 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged denial of his motion to dismiss paternity action brought by appellees, child support division and minor child, by the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks (Alaska), after the trial court stayed a standing order for blood tests under Alaska R. Civ. P. 35 and Alaska Stat. § 25.20.050(e). Appellant argued that the standing order was unconstitutional and that the action was time barred.


OVERVIEW: When her minor child began questioning about his father, mother of appellee, minor child, requested help from appellee, child support division, which instituted a paternity action against appellant father. Appellant requested a stay of the standing order for blood tests under Alaska R. Civ. P. 35 and Alaska Stat. § 25.20.050(e), which was granted until the trial court denied his motion to dismiss. Appellant challenged. The court held that because appellee, minor child, was under the age of majority when the cause of action arose, the statute of limitations was tolled during his minority even though appellant, child support division, could have brought the action on his behalf. Appellant's due process rights were not violated by the standing order, because mother's affidavit alleging paternity was a showing of reasonable cause to order blood testing. The trial court did not err in its calculation of the probability of paternity based on the scientific evidence presented. Therefore, because appellant had not met his burden to rebut the presumption of paternity under § 25.20.050(d) with clear and convincing evidence, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant father's motion to dismiss paternity action brought by appellees, child support division and minor child. The court held that appellant had not met his burden to rebut with clear and convincing evidence, the statutory presumption of paternity that arose as a result of scientific evidence from blood tests.

Hildebrandt v. City of Fairbanks


Supreme Court Nos. S-7484/7554, No. 4980


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


957 P.2d 974; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 84



May 1, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Jay Hodges, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-90-701 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured motorist sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks (Alaska), which granted summary judgment to defendant city. Plaintiff claimed that defendant was held liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 for failing to train its police officers in pursuit driving. Defendant cross-appealed that the trial court erred in using improper standards to determine liability.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff injured motorist was hurt when a police car collided with his vehicle in an intersection during a high speed chase of another car. Plaintiff brought suit against defendant city for damages, and, after a remand for a determination of whether the officer's conduct violated plaintiff's rights under U.S. Const. XIV, the trial court granted summary judgment on defendant's motion, finding that defendant was be held liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 for failing to train its police officers in pursuit driving. Defendant cross-appealed that the trial court erred by using improper standards to judge liability. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that § 1983 was inapplicable to plaintiff's complaint. The court held that absent a constitutional violation by the police officer of plaintiff's substantive due process rights, defendant was not liable under § 1983. Even though the police officer's conduct was negligent, the record did not permit a conclusion that it shocked the conscience. Because the officer's conduct did not shock the conscience, plaintiff did not suffer a Fourteenth Amendment violation. The court held it unnecessary to rule on the cross-appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendant city, in plaintiff injured motorist's claim for damages from a collision with a police car on a high speed chase of another car. The court held that absent a Fourteenth Amendment violation by the police officer of plaintiff's substantive due process rights, defendant was not liable under a federal civil right code. The court did not rule on defendant's cross appeal.

Department of Revenue v. OSG Bulk Ships


Supreme Court No. S-7498, No. 4951


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


961 P.2d 399; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 24



February 20, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, Donald D. Hopwood, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-7815 CI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Alaska Department of Revenue challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, that reversed appellant's decision to assess additional income taxes against appellee taxpayer on income derived from domestic and foreign shipping operations and investments under the Alaska Net Income Tax Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 43.20.011-43.20.350.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Alaska Department of Revenue assessed additional income taxes against appellee taxpayer on income derived from domestic and foreign shipping operations and investments. The trial court reversed appellant's decision. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's judgment to the extent that it was based on its holdings that a federal exemption, I.R.C. § 883(a)(1), applied to appellee and that appellant exceeded its statutory authority when it promulgated Alaska Admin. Code tit. 15, § 20.110(d). The court also reversed the holding that appellee's income was nonbusiness income. The court remanded for a redetermination of whether appellee's income was operational or investment income. The court found that appellee's out-of-state investment income could be apportioned for state taxation only if either the investment itself constituted part of appellee's unitary business, where unitariness was indicated by functional integration, centralization of management, and economies of scale, or the investment was short term and the income was used to fund the unitary business, such that the investment was analogous to a bank account for the unitary business.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment that reversed the decision of appellant Alaska Department of Revenue to assess additional income taxes on income derived from appellee taxpayer's domestic and foreign shipping operations. The court directed that the out-of-state investment income could be apportioned if the investment constituted part of a unitary business or it was short term and the income was used to fund the unitary business.

Department of Military & Veterans Affairs, Alaska Nat'l Guard v. Bowen


Supreme Court Nos. S-6822/6842, No. 4946


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


953 P.2d 888; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 21



February 13, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Bowen v. State, 2007 Alas. LEXIS 57 (Alaska, May 16, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-5009 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED superior court's determinations that Alaska courts have jurisdiction of this dispute. AFFIRMED superior court's order reversing DMVA's decision to terminate Bowen's employment without first giving him a pretermination hearing, and AFFIRMED superior court's determination that a trial de novo is not required. Concluded Bowen had a liberty interest in his reputation under Alaska Constitution, REVERSED that portion of superior court's opinion which held to contrary. REMANDED matter to superior court for remand to DMVA for a pretermination hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee guardsman and appellants, the state, the Department of Military and Veterans Affairs (DMVA), and the Alaska National Guard, challenged decisions of the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage (Alaska), which determined that state courts had jurisdiction, reversed appellant DMVA decision that terminated appellee without having given him a hearing, and found that appellee had no liberty interest in his reputation.


OVERVIEW: Appellee guardsman was terminated from his employment with appellant Alaska National Guard for misconduct. After a hearing before the lower court, all parties appealed. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court found that Alaska courts did have jurisdiction to hear the dispute. State courts had jurisdiction to decide cases involving the discharge of members of the National Guard who were state employees, and federal law did not pre-empt the state courts' rights. The court agreed that appellee was a state employee, but because no statute or regulation had been adopted that applied the Alaska Personnel Act, Alaska Stat. § 39.25.010 et seq., to the termination of a National guardsman, that statute did not apply. The court stated that appellee did have a property interest in full severance pay and a liberty interest in his reputation and was entitled to a full adversarial hearing before being terminated. Appellee's discharge from the Alaska National Guard noted that he was discharged for misconduct, and this sufficiently stigmatized him so that constitutional due process protection was triggered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decisions of the lower court. The court agreed that Alaska courts did have jurisdiction to hear the dispute because appellee guardsman was a state employee and the Alaska Personnel Act did not apply to him. The court concluded that appellee had a property interest in full severance pay and a liberty interest in his reputation, and he was entitled to an adversarial hearing before termination.

McGhee v. State


Supreme Court No. S-7817, No. 4937


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


951 P.2d 1215; 1998 Alas. LEXIS 10



January 23, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Elaine M. Andrews, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-S96-56 CI.



DISPOSITION: DMV's denial of reconsideration is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which affirmed an agency decision not to reduce the period of defendant's driver's license revocation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought judicial review of an administrative decision that denied his request to reduce the period for which his driver's license had been revoked. Defendant was arrested for a third time in 10 years for driving while intoxicated (DWI), and his license was revoked for three years, pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 28.35.030(o)(4)(A). On appeal, the court affirmed, and held that defendant was not entitled to a reduction in the revocation period. The court found that defendant's two prior convictions were final as the result of defendant's failure to timely appeal. Also, even though one of the prior convictions was vacated on procedural grounds after the date of the instant offense, defendant immediately pleaded no contest to the same charge. The change in the conviction date did not change the fact that two convictions for DWI had been entered against defendant prior to his most recent arrest. Moreover, enforcement of the original revocation period posed no threat of actual injustice as there was no question that defendant was guilty of the prior charges. Finally, the three-year revocation accurately reflected defendant's potential danger as a driver to the public.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which upheld the period of an administrative revocation of defendant's driver's license. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a reduction in the revocation period because two prior convictions were final orders, and a change in conviction date for one prior offense did not change that fact that two convictions had been entered against defendant prior to the most recent arrest.

Smith v. State


Supreme Court No, S-6613, No. 4909


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


948 P.2d 473; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 164



November 21, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone and Mark C. Rowland, Judges. Court of Appeals No. A-4054. Superior Court No. 3AN-S90-1097 CR.

Withdrawal Order of November 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Alas. LEXIS 163.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned the court to review the decision of the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the trial court's decision on remand that the evidence seized during the search of defendant's home was admissible because the state had sufficient evidence from independent, legal sources to determine defendant's address in the absence of the statement defendant made after the state violated his right to make a phone call.


OVERVIEW: Defendant purchased drugs from an undercover officer. When defendant requested to make a phone call, the officer said he could do so at the jail. Defendant then provided his home address. The officer obtained a search warrant for the residence by telling the magistrate that defendant told her the location and that she confirmed the address from his driver's license, which actually had a different address. The officer later testified she meant to identify the vehicle license as the confirmation source. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress and defendant was convicted. The court held that the "independent source" exception did not apply because the officer did not actually use the available independent source in applying for the warrant. The court adopted the "inevitable discovery" exception, and remanded the case for consideration of that exception. If the state could show, by clear and convincing evidence, that the illegally obtained evidence would have been discovered through predictable investigative processes, such evidence need not be suppressed as long as the police did not knowingly or intentionally violate defendant's rights in obtaining that evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court, which affirmed the trial court's decision that the evidence was admissible under the independent source exception to the exclusionary rule. The court remanded the case for consideration of the inevitable discovery exception under the test the court adopted.

Valley Hosp. Ass'n v. Mat-Su Coalition for Choice


Supreme Court No. S-7417, No. 4906


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


948 P.2d 963; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 165



November 21, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Palmer, Dana Fabe, Judge. Superior Court No. 3PA-92-01207 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital association challenged an order from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which granted summary judgment for appellee coalition, declared the hospital's policy limiting abortion unenforceable, permanently enjoined enforcement of the policy, and granted attorney fees to the coalition.


OVERVIEW: The hospital association, a nonprofit corporation, enacted a policy whereby it prevented elective abortions from being performed at its hospital. The coalition challenged the policy. On appeal of the lower court's granting of a permanent injunction preventing enforcement of the policy, the court ruled that the hospital was "quasi-public" because it received funds from the state and participated in its certificate of need program. Therefore, the court ruled that under Alaska Const. art. I, § 22, the hospital, as a state actor, could not interfere with a woman's right to an elective abortion in the absence of a compelling state interest that was narrowly tailored. The court found that the association had not met such a test since it advanced no medical, safety, or other public welfare interest for its policy. The court also ruled that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in awarding the coalition $ 110,000 in attorney fees because it found that the association was not a public interest litigant. The court also held that Alaska Stat. § 18.16.010(b), which allowed hospitals to refuse abortions, was unconstitutional to the extent that it applied to quasi-public institutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the lower court that declared the hospital's abortion-limiting policy unenforceable and issued a permanent injunction against its enforcement. The court also upheld the lower court's award of attorney fees to the coalition.

State v. Hazelwood


Supreme Court No. S-7602, No. 4891


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


946 P.2d 875; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 145; 138 Oil & Gas Rep. 245



October 3, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on appeal from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge. Court of Appeals No. A-3452. Superior Court Nos. 3AN-S89-7217 Cr. and 3AN-S89-7218 Cr.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state challenged an order from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which reversed the trial court's order that convicted respondent captain of violating Alaska Stat. § 46.03.790(a) after finding that he had negligently discharged eleven million gallons of petroleum onto a reef.


OVERVIEW: Eleven million gallons of petroleum were discharged onto a reef after the captain ran his ship aground. The jury found that the captain had negligently discharged oil onto the land, and he was convicted under Alaska Stat. § 46.03.740 of a class B misdemeanor. The trial court ruled that the conviction could be predicated upon a finding of ordinary negligence. On appeal of the court of appeals reversal of that decision, the court ruled that the conviction was proper. The court found the captain was not denied due process under Alaska Const. art. I, § 7 because reasonable deterrence was the basic principle of the due process balance between individual and societal interests and society could reasonably expect the captain to conform his or her conduct to the law. The court held that the negligence standard was constitutionally permissible because it approximated what the due process guarantee aimed at: an assurance that criminal penalties would be imposed only when the conduct at issue was something society could reasonably expect to deter. The court also disagreed with the captain's contention that § 46.03.740 itself incorporated a criminal negligence mens rea standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the holding of the court of appeals, finding that the trial court had not erroneously applied the ordinary negligence standard to the criminal statute. The court remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of any unresolved issues.

 

32 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Hertz v. Storer


Supreme Court No. S-7511, No. 4871


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


943 P.2d 725; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 122



August 22, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Hertz v. Storer, 522 U.S. 1059, 118 S. Ct. 717, 139 L. Ed. 2d 657, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 234 (1998)
Related proceeding at Hertz v. State, 2010 Alas. LEXIS 101 (Alaska, Sept. 22, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Milton M. Souter, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-94-11415 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which affirmed the decision of appellee, the Director of the Department of Revenue, Permanent Fund Dividend Division for the State of Alaska, denying the inmate a Permanent Fund Dividend (PFD) for 1993 because he was an incarcerated felon.


OVERVIEW: The inmate did not dispute that he was ineligible for a PFD under the terms of Alaska Stat. § 43.23.005(d), but he challenged the statute as unconstitutional. The inmate's argument that § 43.23.005(d) was an ex post facto law had already been rejected by the Alaska Supreme Court in a previous action to which the inmate was a party, and because the Alaska Supreme Court had held that denial of a PFD under § 43.23.005(d) was not a criminal punishment for purposes of the ex post facto clause, the inmate's argument that he was being subjected to increased punishment and, thus, double jeopardy was invalid. The inmate's claim that he was entitled to compensation for a taking of his interest in the wealth produced by state land was unavailing because at most, the inmate was entitled to procedural due process in the denial of his PFD and he could not contend that he had been denied such due process. The court also held that the superior court properly held that the inmate was not subject to fee awards because he had an economic interest in the form of dividend payments that he stood to collect if his action succeeded and, thus, he did not quality as a public interest litigant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court.

 

33 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Municipality of Anchorage v. Gallion


Supreme Court Nos. S-7331/S-7591, No. 4869


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


944 P.2d 436; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 120



August 15, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Anchorage Police & Fire Ret. Sys. v. Gallion, 2003 Alas. LEXIS 21 (Alaska, Mar. 14, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Dana Fabe, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-94-8592 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order from the Superior Court (Alaska), which granted summary judgment to a class of firefighters and police officers (APFRS), after determining that the city's reduction of APFRS's benefits was made in violation of Alaska Const. art. XII. § 7.


OVERVIEW: The city claimed that its decision to reduce benefits for the APFRS was not violative of the Alaska Constitution because it had not diminished or impaired the benefits, as they had not yet accrued. The APFRS claimed that reduction of their benefit scheme violated the state constitution because their benefits had already accrued, and vested with them. The court agreed with the APFRS. The court found that the members had a vested interest in the surpluses generated by their plans and that using the new actuarial tables for calculating benefits violated their constitutional rights. The court reasoned that the members were entitled to have the level of rights and benefits preserved in substance without any modification downwards. The court also upheld the award of attorney fees, however it disagreed with the way the award was calculated. Hence, the court remanded for a redetermination of the attorney fees award.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, but remanded for a redetermination of the amount of the attorney fee award to APFRS.

 

34 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Thoma v. Hickel


Supreme Court No. S-6273, No. 4863


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


947 P.2d 816; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 121



August 15, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, J. Justin Ripley, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-93-692 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED in part, REVERSED in part and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizen sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which ruled that appellee governor was protected by the doctrine of public executive immunity and granted him summary judgment in an action involving an ethics complaint, filed pursuant to federal and state law.


OVERVIEW: The citizen filed an action against the governor alleging that the governor was in concert with others to engage in a smear campaign against him. The citizen alleged that the campaign improperly used the criminal justice system records, and that the campaign was conducted in retaliation for the citizen's protected political activity seeking the removal of the governor. The superior court ruled that the governor was protected by the doctrine of public executive immunity. The citizen claimed that people had a constitutional right to be free from acts of retaliation by officials made in response to speech or other activities protected by the U.S. Const. amend. I. The court stated that making public officials civilly liable for retaliatory speech would, in essence, convert the U.S. Const. amend. I model of an interchange into a one-way street. This would be fundamentally inconsistent with the values protected by U.S. Const. amend. I. Therefore, the citizen did not have a valid claim of retaliation. However, the court held that the citizen had a claim based on the violation of state regulations concerning the use of his criminal record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the decision of the superior court when it granted summary judgment to the governor in the citizen's action involving an ethics complaint. The judgment was affirmed as to federal claims and a state constitutional claim, reversed regarding a state regulation claim, and remanded for further proceedings.

Law Offices of Vincent Vitale, P.C. v. Tabbytite


Supreme Court Nos. S-7351/7542, No. 4852


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


942 P.2d 1141; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 108



July 25, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Milton M. Souter, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-9653 CI.



DISPOSITION: The dismissal of this case for lack of subject matter jurisdiction is REVERSED. This case is REMANDED for (1) entry of a personal judgment in favor of Vitale, (2) entry of judgment awarding the interpled funds to Tabbytite, (3) determination of the prevailing party and an award of attorney's fees to such party, and (4) such other and further relief as may be appropriate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant law firm and attorney sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska that dismissed appellee city's interpleader action for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The action arose out of the federal district court's judgment that awarded appellee landowner damages in a condemnation action.


OVERVIEW: A road that became public was illegally built on the landowner's allotment that was protected by 25 U.S.C.S. § 410. More than two decades later, the land on which the road was located was formally taken in condemnation proceedings and a money judgment in the landowner's favor was entered. Prior to his termination as counsel to the landowner in the condemnation action, the attorney filed an attorney's lien seeking security for payment of his fees from litigation proceeds. The municipality, fearing it would be unable to pay the condemnation award, filed an interpleader in the superior court. Subsequently, the superior court dismissed the action, reasoning that it was divested of subject matter jurisdiction by the application of 25 U.S.C.S.§ 410. In reversing and remanding, the court found that Alaska Bar R. 40(t) required the superior court to confirm an award and reduce it to judgment upon the application of a party unless either party timely sought to vacate, correct or modify it. The court also found that the superior court had jurisdiction to decide the application of 25 U.S.C.S. § 410 to the proceeds in question.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision that dismissed the city's interpleader action that arose out of the condemnation action. The court directed entry of a personal judgment in favor of the attorney, entry of judgment awarding the interpled funds to the landowner, determination of the prevailing party and the corresponding award of attorney fees, and such other relief as might be appropriate.
