Mathis v. Sauser


Supreme Court No. S-6773, No. 4849


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


942 P.2d 1117; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 99



July 18, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, John Reese, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-9451 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED in part, REVERSED in part, REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate appealed from the order of the Superior Court of the Third Judicial District, Anchorage (Alaska) granting summary judgment to defendant prison officials in inmate's action challenging the constitutionality of a state prison policy preventing inmates from possessing computer printers in their cells.


OVERVIEW: State prison officials promulgated a policy preventing inmates in state correctional facilities from possessing computer printers in their cells. The inmate filed a civil rights action against prison officials seeking damages and declaratory and injunctive relief. The superior court granted summary judgment to the prison officials. On appeal, the court affirmed the superior court's judgment in part, ruling that the prison officials were protected from civil claims for damages by qualified immunity. The court ruled that the policy had been properly promulgated and that there was no evidence that the policy had been selectively enforced so as to deny the inmate equal protection of the laws. However, the court reversed the superior court's judgment regarding the inmate's claims for declaratory and injunctive relief based upon denial of access to the courts. The court ruled that a genuine fact issue existed regarding the constitutionality of the policy because the record showed that the policy was specifically intended to interfere with inmates' access to the courts. The court remanded the case for further proceedings on the inmate's claims for declaratory and injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order granting summary to defendants on the inmate's claims except for his claim for the denial of access to the courts, which the court reversed. The court remanded the case to the superior court for further proceedings regarding the declaratory and injunctive relief aspects of the inmate's remaining claim.

State v. Arnariak

SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


941 P.2d 154; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 93



June 27, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the District Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Dillingham, Milton M. Souter, Judge. Court of Appeals Nos. A-5397/5398. Trial Court Nos. 3DI-93-429/3DI-93-427 Cr.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the district court's grant of defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint charging them with entering a state island without a permit and charging one of them with the unlawful discharge of a firearm on the island, both in violation of Alaska Admin. Code tit. 5, § 92.066.

The appellate court reversed the district court, holding that the regulation was not preempted by the MMPA. The court found that the legislative history, the purpose of MMPA, and the rule that statutes should be construed to avoid an unconstitutional result persuasively indicated that MMPA's preemption was not so broad as to prevent the state from limiting access to, or the discharge of firearms on, state wildlife refuges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the court of appeals, which affirmed the district court's dismissal of the complaint against defendants. The court remanded the case with directions to reverse the decision of the district court and to remand this case to the district court for further proceedings.

Alaska Ctr. for the Env't v. State


Supreme Court No. S-5776, No. 4830


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


940 P.2d 916; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 82



June 13, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-82-2208 CI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant intervenors sought review of a judgment from the Fairbanks Superior Court (Alaska), which refused to award the intervenors attorney's fees pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 82 after the intervenors successfully challenged the approval of appellee State's settlement proposal. The trial court held that the intervenors were not prevailing parties because the settlement would have been disapproved without intervention.


OVERVIEW: When the State removed lands from a public trust, plaintiffs filed an action, alleging a breached of duty. After the State was ordered to reconstitute the trust, plaintiffs enjoined the State from transferring trust lands and filed a notice of lis pendens. When the State proposed a settlement, plaintiffs moved to modify the injunction and to release the lis pendens. Thereafter, the intervenors moved to intervene, opposing the settlement and plaintiffs' motion. After plaintiffs' motion was denied and the settlement was disapproved, the intervenors sought attorney's fees under rule 82. In denying attorney's fees, the trial court held that the intervenors were not prevailing parties because the settlement would have been disapproved without intervention. Upon review, the court held that even though the trial court had not adopted all of the intervenors' arguments or vindicated all their claims, it had refused to approve the settlement. As such, the court held that the intervenors were prevailing parties because they had been successful on the action's "main issue" and because prevailing party status did not depend on whether an argument directly caused a favorable decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

Williams v. Department of Revenue


Supreme Court No. S-6862, No. 4834


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


938 P.2d 1065; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 83



June 13, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Joan M. Woodward, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-4757 CI.



DISPOSITION: Superior Court's decision of Williams's claims not time-barred, AFFIRMED. Superior Court's decision of Board's denial of Williams's mental injury claim, AFFIRMED. Superior Court's decision affirming the denial of Williams's physical injury claim REVERSED. REMANDED with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which affirmed the board's denial of the employee's claim for workers' compensation benefits for physical and mental injuries she allegedly sustained as a result of her employment with the State Child Support Enforcement Division (CSED), a division of appellee State of Alaska, Department of Revenue.


OVERVIEW: The employee, who suffered from work-related stress, worked for CSED for more than 10 years. She retired under the state's Retirement Incentive Program and then filed a report of occupational injury or illness with the board, claiming that numerous stress-related physical and mental injuries arose from her employment. The board denied the employee's claims, and the superior court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that: (1) the employee's claims were not time-barred under Alaska Stat. § 23.30.100, because CSED had notice of the employee's illness and was not prejudiced by her failure to give timely notice; (2) the board did not err in denying the employee's mental injury claim, because substantial evidence supported the board's finding that the employee did not face extraordinary or unusual stress; and (3) the board erred in denying the employee's physical injury claim, because the employee made out a prima facie case of aggravation or acceleration of a pre-existing disease or condition sufficient to raise the statutory presumption of compensability, and the state did not offer substantial evidence rebutting that presumption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's decision that the employee's claims were not time-barred. The court also affirmed the superior court's decision affirming the board's denial of the employee's mental injury claim. The court reversed the superior court's decision affirming the denial of the employee's physical injury claim and remanded, with instructions to remand to the board to determine the benefits due the employee.

Krohn v. State


No. 4825, Supreme Court No. S-7343


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


938 P.2d 1019; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 74



May 30, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, M. Francis Neville, Judge. Superior Court No. 3KN-94-730 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, the State of Alaska Department of Fish and Game and its Commissioner, in his action challenging the validity of emergency and permanent subsistence regulations adopted in 1994 by the Board of Fisheries, the Board of Game, and the Commissioner of the Department of Fish and Game.


OVERVIEW: A law designating nonsubsistence hunting and fishing areas was challenged and found to be unconstitutional. Thus, the Boards of Fisheries and Game were required to adopt emergency subsistence regulations for those areas. Authority to adopt the necessary regulations was delegated to the Commissioner, who issued notice to adopt the emergency regulations as permanent regulations. After appellant commenced this action challenging the validity of the regulations, the court held that the creation of nonsubsistence areas pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(c) did not violate the constitution. The subsistence hunting and fishing regulations adopted by the Commissioner were then repealed, which rendered appellant's action moot. The court found that the public interest exception to the mootness doctrine should not be applied because the factual and legal issues were inextricably dependent upon the particular factual and procedural circumstances that existed at the time of the adoption of the subsistence regulations and therefore were not capable of repetition. The court also found that the Commissioner's actions did not come within the Open Meetings Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 44.62.310 - .312.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the superior court.

Weiss v. State


No. 4816, Supreme Court No. S-6845


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


939 P.2d 380; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 63



May 2, 1997, Decided

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED superior court's decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the father and next friend of a member of the subject class and members of the class for themselves and all others similarly situated, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, that approved an agreement settling a class action lawsuit concerning the lands granted to Alaska under the Alaska Mental Health Enabling Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 709, 711-712 (1956).


OVERVIEW: Appellants, the father and next friend of a member of the subject class and members of the class, challenged the superior court's decision that approved the settlement of a class action lawsuit after almost a decade of negotiations regarding the lands granted to Alaska under the Alaska Mental Health Enabling Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 709, 711-712 (1956). Turney v. State


Supreme Court No. S-6932, No. 4799


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


936 P.2d 533; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 46



April 4, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Ralph R. Beistline, Judge. Court of Appeals No. A-5852.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED superior court's order denying Turney's motion to dismiss indictment and information, and REMANDED for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District (Alaska), in which he was convicted of jury tampering, Alaska Stat. § 11.56.590, and criminal trespass, Alaska Stat. § 11.46.330(a)(1). He claimed that the jury tampering statute was unconstitutionally overbroad or void for vagueness, and that he could not commit a criminal trespass on public property at a time when the property was open to the public.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of jury tampering, Alaska Stat. § 11.56.590, and criminal trespass, Alaska Stat. § 11.46.330(a)(1), as a result of his activities in and around a courthouse during a trial. Defendant approached several jurors and attempted to inform them about jury nullification, whereby the juror's would ignore the judge's instructions and make an independent determination as to the validity of the law they were to apply. Defendant claimed that the jury tampering statute was void for vagueness and unconstitutionally overbroad and that he could not commit a criminal trespass on a public building during a time when the building was open to the public. The court rejected his contentions and affirmed his conviction. The court held that defendant's free speech rights could be curtailed to insure a fair trial, and that the jury tampering statute did not reach constitutionally protected speech. The court examined the statute's chilling effect, whether it gave adequate notice, and whether the language encouraged arbitrary enforcement in determining that it was not void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which found defendant guilty of jury tampering and criminal trespass. The court held that the jury tampering statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad or void for vagueness, and free speech rights were properly curtailed by the statute in the interest of insuring fair trials.

City of North Pole v. Zabek


Supreme Court No. S-6777, Supreme Court Nos. S-6907/6927, No. 4781


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


934 P.2d 1292; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 26



February 21, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Dale O. Curda, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-92-733 CI. Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-93-2252 CI.



DISPOSITION:  AFFIRMED in part, REVERSED in part, VACATED in part, and REMANDED.  [**2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Although the ruling of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, was not an appealable final judgment, appellant employer contested that court's finding of a due process violation and the reduction in the award of attorneys fees and costs. Respondent employee cross appealed from the trial court's entry of summary judgment as to her allegations of slander, violation of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, and the award of fees and costs.


OVERVIEW: After the employer terminated the employee without providing a hearing, the employee appealed to an administrative board that affirmed the termination after a hearing. The employee appealed to the trial court, which remanded to the board after finding that the termination violated due process of law, that there was no curative post-termination hearing, and that the employee was entitled to back pay, attorneys fees, and costs. The employer appealed, although the trial court's ruling was not an appealable final judgment. The court treated the appeal as a petition for review, which was granted because it presented an important constitutional question. The court ruled that the post-hearing supplementation of the record with witness affidavits provided sufficient due process for a curative post-termination hearing because the employee was terminated solely for failure to maintain a required job clearance. The summary judgment in favor of the employer was affirmed as to the employee's claims of slander and violation of her federal civil rights. The court remanded for a redetermination of the employee's back pay, prevailing party status, attorneys fees, and costs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling that the employee was provided no curative post-termination hearing. The court vacated the award of attorneys fees for the employee's due process claim and remanded for a redetermination of the employee's back pay, prevailing party status, attorneys fees, and costs. The court affirmed the summary judgment for the employer on the claims of slander and civil rights violations.

Matanuska-Susitna Borough Sch. Dist. v. State


Supreme Court No. S-5513, No. 4470


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


931 P.2d 391; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 15



January 31, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Palmer, Beverly W. Cutler and James A. Hanson, Judges. Superior Court No. 3PA-86-2022 CIV.



DISPOSITION: We REVERSE the superior court's assessment of costs against the individual plaintiffs, and REMAND for a determination of whether the Borough and District should be liable for the full award of costs, or whether the award of costs should be reduced. We AFFIRM the judgment of the superior court in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District granted summary judgment in favor of defendant state in plaintiff borough and plaintiff taxpayers' action challenging the constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 14.11.100 and Alaska Stat. § 14.17.025. The trial court also awarded the state litigation costs against the borough and the taxpayers.


OVERVIEW: The borough and the taxpayers initiated an action against the state alleging that § 14.11.100, concerning state funding for school construction, and § 14.17.025, concerning local contribution for school construction, violated their equal protection rights. The borough and the taxpayers contended that under the sections, regional education attendance areas received 98 percent state funding for construction, while borough areas only received 70 percent state funding. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the state finding that the borough had no equal protection rights and the sections did not deprive the taxpayers their equal protection rights. The trial court also awarded the state litigation costs. On appeal, the court found that the taxpayers did not prove that they had been disadvantaged somehow in relation to the taxpayers residing in the regional education attendance areas. The taxpayers qualified as public interest litigants, so they were be required to pay litigation costs. The borough was not a public interest litigant, so it was properly ordered to pay costs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision granting summary judgment in favor of the state. The court reversed the decision awarding the state litigation costs against the taxpayers. The court remanded for a determination whether the borough should be held responsible for all of the litigation costs.

Distributel, Inc. v. State


No. 4458, Supreme Court No. S-7157


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


933 P.2d 1137; 1997 Alas. LEXIS 2



January 3, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 5355.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, John Reese, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-94-7353 CI.



DISPOSITION: Order of supreme court AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant seller challenged an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which was entered in appellee State's action that charged the seller with making telephone sales without registering as a telephonic seller under § 45.63.010 of the Telephonic Solicitation Act, Alaska Stat. § 45.63.010. The order forbade the seller from initiating calls to Alaska customers under Alaska Stat. § 45.63.080(14).


OVERVIEW: The seller made numerous sales by calling customers on the telephone. Thereafter, the State charged that the seller had made such sales without registering as a telephonic seller under Alaska Stat. § 45.63.010. The parties stipulated to a permanent injunction that precluded the seller from making telephone sales without first registering in accordance with the Act. The injunction also required the trial court's permission for the seller to operate through the "mail order sales" exemption in Alaska Stat. § 45.63.080(14). The trial court denied the seller's motion for permission to operate through § 45.63.080(14), and limited it to receiving calls from customers responding to the seller's catalog. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's order and held that the exemption did not encompass sales that were initiated by a telephone call from a seller following the delivery of a mail order catalog to consumers. The court held that this interpretation comported with the Act's purpose, which was to prevent the unregistered telephone marketers from making unsolicited sales calls. The court concluded that the interpretation was consistent with the legislative history of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which forbade the seller from initiating calls to Alaska customers under the catalog exemption.

Snyder v. State


No. 4451, Supreme Court No. S-6661


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


930 P.2d 1274; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 158



December 27, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the District Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, William R. Smith and Mark I. Wood, Judges. Court of Appeals No. A-4991. Superior Court No. 4FA-S93-745 Cr.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant for driving while intoxicated (DWI) and refusing to submit to a breath test under Alaska Stat. § 28.35.032(f).


OVERVIEW: After his arrest for DWI, defendant requested a blood test of his alcohol level; the police officer refused. After several unsuccessful attempts to obtain a breath sample, the officer charged defendant with refusal to submit to a breath test, although he offered to keep trying and again requested a blood test. The intermediate appellate court affirmed his DWI conviction. Defendant claimed that the state violated his statutory and constitutional right to an independent chemical test and that the trial court erred in refusing to instruct the jury on the defense of subsequent consent. The court reversed his conviction. The court held that the Due Process Clause, Alaska Const. art. I, § 7, entitled a DWI arrestee to an independent chemical test even if he refused to take the statutorily prescribed breath test as long as such a test was not impracticable. The remedy for the state's violation of defendant's due process right was a presumption on remand that an independent blood test would have been favorable to defendant. Some evidence supported defendant's request for an instruction on subsequent consent to a breath test to cure a prior refusal; the instruction should have been given.


OUTCOME: The court held that the state should have provided defendant with the opportunity to obtain an independent test of his blood alcohol content. The court found that the trial court should have instructed the jury on the defense of subsequent consent. The court reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

 

47 of 606 DOCUMENTS


In the Disciplinary Matter Involving Triem


No. 4443, Supreme Court Nos. S-6702/6732


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


929 P.2d 634; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 148



December 6, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Decision of the Disciplinary Board of the Alaska Bar Association.

This Opinion Substituted on Partial Grant of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of October 25, 1996, Previously Reported at: 1996 Alas. LEXIS 120.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED in part, AFFIRMED in part, REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sought review of a decision from appellee Disciplinary Board of the Alaska Bar Association, which dismissed a client's grievance against appellant, and found that appellant had committed ethical violations in his representation of a second client and in his professional dealings with another attorney.


OVERVIEW: A client retained appellant attorney to represent him in the purchase of a limited entry fishing permit. A second client retained appellant to represent him in an action to quiet title to certain property and to recover damages. The clients later filed grievances against appellant. Appellee Alaska Bar Association dismissed the first client's grievance, but found that appellant had committed ethical violations in his representation of the second client and in his professional dealings with another attorney. Appellant sought review. The court reversed as to the first client because the delays in the proceedings did not warrant dismissal. In the matter concerning the second client, the court ordered a 90 day suspension, probation, and continuing education. In the matter involving the other attorney, the court ordered public censure. The court held that appellant's dishonesty during the disciplinary process was an aggravating factor, which could properly be considered in protecting the integrity of the hearing process. The court held that the record provided substantial evidence that appellant had necessary intent to act dishonestly in violation of Alaska Disciplinary R. 1-102(A)(4).


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part, affirmed in part, and remanded appellee Alaska Bar Association's dismissal of a client's grievance against appellant attorney, which found that appellant committed ethical violations in his representation of a second client because the delays in the first client's proceedings did not warrant dismissal, and appellant had the necessary intent to act dishonestly in violation of an Alaska Disciplinary Rule.

 

48 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Area G Home & Landowners Org. v. Anchorage


No. 4435, Supreme Court No. S-7731


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


927 P.2d 728; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 141



November 22, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied May 12, 1997, Reported at 1997 U.S. LEXIS 2879.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-96-01661 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which entered judgment in favor of defendant municipality in the taxpayer's suit to prevent the creation of a new police service area.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the taxpayers argued that in creating the new service area, the municipality violated its own charter and ordinances as well as the equal protection guarantees in the state and federal constitutions. The court affirmed the superior court's decision in favor of the municipality. In doing so, the court concluded from the legislative history of the Home Rule Charter for the Municipality of Anchorage (Charter) that the Charter Commission did not limit the term "area affected" to only the geographic area into which services would be extended, and thus, the Anchorage Assembly had the power to determine that all persons living in the Anchorage Police Service Area (APSA) and the Hillside were within the area affected by AO § 96-30(S), even though that determination would mean that residents of the Hillside would not be permitted to approve the new APSA by a separate vote. Because the APSA was within the area affected, the assembly correctly permitted APSA residents to vote. Thus, every voter within the area affected by AO § 96-30(S) was given an equally powerful vote, and no person's right to equal protection was violated.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the municipality was affirmed.

 

49 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Muller v. BP Exploration (Alaska)


No. 4402, Supreme Court No. S-7128


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


923 P.2d 783; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 103; 73 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 579; 69 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P44,539



September 13, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]


PRIOR HISTORY: Certified Question from the United States District Court for the District of Alaska, James K. Singleton, Jr., Judge. United States District Court No. A93-109 CV (JKS).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Alaska certified the following questions: (1) whether Alaska Stat. § 18.80.220(a)(1) prevented an employer from discriminating against an employee based on the identity of his spouse; and (2) whether Alaska Stat. § 18.80.220(a)(1) was limited to preventing employers from discriminating based on the status of being married.


OVERVIEW: A couple engaged to be married brought suit against their employer for unlawful discrimination. The district court granted the employer's motion for summary judgment on all claims, except that of marital status discrimination in violation of Alaska Stat. § 18.80.220, and certified questions concerning the scope of the statute's coverage pursuant to Alaska R. App. P. 407(a). The court held that § 18.80.220(a)(1) was limited to preventing employers from discriminating against an employee based on the status of being married, and did not prohibit an employer from discriminating against an employee based on the identity of his or her spouse or future spouse. The court found that the legislature intended that the term "marital status" be interpreted in accordance with its plain meaning and rejected the couple's interpretation that the term embraced the identity of a spouse or intended spouse. The legislature was presumed to act with other statutes in mind and the absence of a legislative definition or provision for the impact of a more expansive definition on anti-nepotism laws indicated that the plain meaning was intended.


OUTCOME: The court answered no to the first question and yes to the second.

 

50 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Pullen v. Ulmer


No. 4394, Supreme Court No. S-7642


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


923 P.2d 54; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 96



August 26, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected September 5, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-95-2385 CI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee organization (proponent) circulated an initiative application to place a fishing issue on the ballot. After appellee Lieutenant Governor found that the application complied with Alaska Stat. § 15.45.010 and certified it for inclusion on the ballot, appellants, a civic group (group), sought an injunction. When the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, denied relief, the group appealed the order.


OVERVIEW: The group challenged the lieutenant governor's certification of the proponent's initiative application for inclusion on the general election ballot claiming that the proposed bill was not a proper subject of an initiative because it would cause state property, being its salmon resources, to be appropriated in violation of anti-appropriation provisions in Alaska Stat. § 15.45.010. When the superior court granted summary judgment to the proponent and the lieutenant governor, the group appealed. The court reversed. It ruled that the proposed initiative made an appropriation of state property in violation of Alaska Const. art. XI, § 7, because even though the state did not "own" the salmon in the same fashion that it owned state land, salmon was a state asset to be managed by the state as a public trust. It also held that proscriptions against the making of appropriations by initiative in Alaska Const. art. XI, § 7, and Alaska Stat. § 15.45.010 were violated by the proposed measure because it appealed to self-interest and impinged on the legislature's power to allocate resources among competing users.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's order and remanded to that court with directions to amend the judgment to provide that the lieutenant governor was permanently enjoined from placing the proposed initiative on the ballot.

 

51 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Usibelli Coal Mine v. Department of Natural Resources


No. 4391, Supreme Court No. S-6650


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


921 P.2d 1134; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 91



August 16, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Peter A. Michalski, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-5470 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coal mine sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which affirmed the decision of appellee Alaska Department of Natural Resources (DNR) holding that the mine owed back royalties to the state of Alaska under three state coal leases.


OVERVIEW: The DNR claimed back royalties plus interest due on three non-competitive coal leases held by the coal mine. Before the initial royalty term on any of the leases had expired, the DNR promulgated regulations setting a standard royalty rate in Alaska Admin. Code tit. 11, § 85.220 (1996). The coal mine was granted some measure of relief but was ultimately required to pay all back royalties. The superior court affirmed the DNR's decision and upon the coal mine's appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the DNR's regulations were valid because there had been a valid implied delegation of rule-making authority to the DNR to adjust royalty rates for coal leases. Further, there were sufficient standards and procedures to ensure the valid exercise of the DNR's authority and the regulations were consistent with Alaskan law. The DNR's interpretation of the regulations was reasonable. The coal mine did not have a contractual right to have the adjusted royalty rates on its leases established by negotiation, and the DNR was not equitably estopped from collecting the back royalties. Finally, the court declined to review the DNR's decision not to forgive royalties owed by the coal mine.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's decision, which in turn had affirmed the DNR's determination that the coal mine owed back royalties on certain coal leases.

Capital Info. Group v. Office of the Governor


No. 4386, Supreme Court No. S-6443


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


923 P.2d 29; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 92



August 16, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1]


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Rodger W. Pegues, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-93-1578 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED, REVERSED AND REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, news publisher and individual, sought review of a summary judgment from the Superior Court, First Judicial District, Juneau (Alaska), which denied access to certain state government documents on the basis of the deliberative process privilege.


OVERVIEW: The documents at issue consisted of budget impact memoranda and legislative proposals sent to the governor. The court affirmed the denial of public access to the legislative proposals, finding that the proposals were submitted to aid the governor in reaching decisions. Hence, they were privileged as predecisional and deliberative materials. The court reversed with respect to the budget impact memoranda, holding that such materials were public information under Alaska Stat. § 37.07.050(g). The court found no abuse of discretion in the trial judge's decision not to disqualify himself based on the appearance of impartiality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment denying access to the legislative proposals sent to the governor, reversed the denial of public access to the budget impact memoranda, and affirmed the denial of the motion for disqualification. The court remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

53 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Javed v. Department of Pub. Safety, Div. of Motor Vehicles


No. 4375, Supreme Court No. S-6296


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


921 P.2d 620; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 74



July 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-00458 CI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed to the court after the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, affirmed an administrative determination of the Department of Public Safety to revoke the driver's license of defendant after he was arrested for driving while intoxicated. The scope of the administrative hearing was limited by Alaska Statute § 28.15.166(g), and an issue was the constitutionality of that limitation.


OVERVIEW: When police found defendant, he was tied to a post as part of a citizen's arrest. He had walked into a lot and bickered with a woman. According to witnesses including that woman, he had previously been following her with a car and drove into the lot and left and came back on foot. At the revocation proceeding, defendant claimed that he had not been driving; thus, he denied an essential element of the offense of driving while intoxicated. Alaska Statute § 28.15.166(g) said that the revocation hearing was limited to determining whether or not the police had a reasonable basis to believe that defendant had been operating while intoxicated a motor vehicle, commercial motor vehicle, or aircraft. The court ruled that as applied § 28.15.166(g) was unconstitutional. That was because the statute foreclosed any opportunity to show that the police were mistaken about defendant driving while intoxicated even though they had a reasonable ground or grounds to believe that he had been driving while intoxicated. At the revocation hearing, defendant had to be granted the opportunity to contest fully issues of central importance to the revocation decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's decision, and the court remanded the case to the superior court with an instruction to remand it to the Department for a new hearing to include an inquiry about whether or not defendant was driving at the time of the alleged offense.

 

54 of 606 DOCUMENTS


A. Fred Miller, P.C. v. Purvis


No. 4372, Supreme Court No. S-6679


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


921 P.2d 610; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 76



July 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, Michael A. Thompson, Judge. Superior Court No. 1KE-94-212 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney brought a petition before the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, seeking to vacate the award issued to appellee client in a fee dispute, after the client invoked the mandatory fee arbitration provisions of Alaska Bar Rules 34-42. The trial court denied the petition and entered judgment on the award. The attorney appealed.


OVERVIEW: The attorney contended the fee arbitration provisions, which were mandatory for attorneys pursuant to the state bar rules, constituted a denial of due process because mandatory arbitration was constitutional only when there was judicial review on the merits. After reviewing law form sister states on similar issues, the court agreed with the client that mandatory arbitration provisions without judicial review on the merits served the purpose of permitting a client to challenge a lawyers fees without the burden of retaining a new lawyer or the expense of further legal costs. Because due process required only that a party have an adequate opportunity to represent his interests, the mandatory arbitration provisions were not proven not to offend that right. The attorney had the burden of demonstrating the unconstitutionality of the challenged provision and he failed to establish that the bar rules requiring arbitration so violated his due process rights as to overcome the beneficial effect it had on the legal system by providing clients with a recourse against unfair legal fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment on the mandatory arbitration fee award, entered in favor of the client against the attorney in their fee dispute.

 

55 of 606 DOCUMENTS


R.R. v. State


No. 4359, Supreme Court No. S-6756


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


919 P.2d 754; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 70



June 21, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Ralph R. Beistline, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-92-160 CP.



DISPOSITION: VACATED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the decision of the Fairbanks Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District (Alaska), which removed her children from her custody. The mother contended that the superior court made inadequate findings under Alaska CINA R. 15(g) (2000), erroneously concluded that the youngest child was a child in need of aid, and that Alaska Stat. § 13.26.045, authorizing the superior court to appoint guardians, was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: The state was awarded temporary custody over the three older children and assumed emergency custody over the youngest child when it was born. The trial court found the youngest child to be a child in need of aid under Alaska Stat. § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A) and appointed guardians for all of the children. The trial court ultimately concluded that the mother was currently unable to provide for the physical, emotional, and social needs of her children. On appeal, the court held that the trial court satisfied the requirements of Alaska CINA R. 15(g) (2000) when it made a finding that the treatment plan was reasonable. Alaska Stat. § 13.26.045 did not satisfy any of the criteria used to determine vagueness and held the statute constitutional. However, because the youngest child was not a child with "no parent, guardian, custodian, or relative caring or willing to care" for her, the trial court's finding that she was a child in need of aid under Alaska Stat. § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A) was erroneous. The court noted that on remand the trial court might find the child to be in need of aid under either Alaska Stat. § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A) or (a)(2)(F).


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the lower court and remanded for further proceedings.

Todd v. State


No. 4357, Supreme Court No. S-6709, Supreme Court No. S-6807


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


917 P.2d 674; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 69



June 21, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Todd v. Alaska, 519 U.S. 966, 117 S. Ct. 391, 136 L. Ed. 2d 306, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 6635 (1996)
Related proceeding at Todd v. Todd, 989 P.2d 141, 1999 Alas. LEXIS 135 (Alaska, 1999)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Motion denied by Harvey v. Antrim, 2007 Alas. App. LEXIS 106 (Alaska Ct. App., May 4, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Rene J. Gonzalez, Judge. Court of Appeals No. A-4618, Superior Court No. 3AN-S91-8149 Cr., Court of Appeals No. A-4648, Superior Court No. 3AN-S91-8148 Cr.

This Opinion Substituted on Denial of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of April 26, 1996, Previously Reported at: 1996 Alas. LEXIS 40.
Todd v. State, 917 P.2d 674, 1996 Alas. LEXIS 40 (Alaska, 1996)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a petition for hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which affirmed an order of the superior court convicting and sentencing defendants for both second-degree (felony) murder and first-degree robbery. Defendants asserted that the Double Jeopardy Clauses of the United States and Alaska Constitutions barred conviction and punishment for both felony murder and the predicate felony.


OVERVIEW: Defendants entered a bar to commit a robbery and killed an employee. Defendants were convicted of first-degree robbery and second-degree murder, along with other charges. Defendants were sentenced to imprisonment for the murder charges and to consecutive years of imprisonment for the robbery charges. The court of appeals affirmed the convictions and sentences. On further review, the court affirmed judgment and held that the consecutive sentences for felony murder and the predicate felony of first-degree robbery did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clauses of the United States and Alaska Constitutions. The court reasoned that: (1) under an examination of the language and legislative history of the felony-murder statute, Alaska Stat. § 11.41.110(a)(3), the legislature intended to allow multiple punishments for felony murder and the predicate offense of robbery; (2) the offenses encompassed different constituent elements, in that murder had an element that someone was killed, and robbery had an element of taking or attempting to take another's property; and (3) felony murder was a distinct area of the criminal law not governed by traditional lesser-included offense analysis.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment convicting and consecutively sentencing defendants for both second-degree (felony) murder and first-degree robbery.

Carlson v. Commercial Fisheries Entry Comm'n


No. 4356, Supreme Court No. S-6590


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


919 P.2d 1337; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 60



June 21, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied January 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 676.
Motion granted by, Writ of certiorari denied Carlson v. Alaska Commer. Fisheries Entry Comm'n, 519 U.S. 1101, 136 L. Ed. 2d 730, 117 S. Ct. 789, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 676 (1997)
Appeal after remand at State v. Carlson, 2003 Alas. LEXIS 20 (Alaska, Mar. 14, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Peter A. Michalski, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-84-5790 CI.
Carlson v. State, 798 P.2d 1269, 1990 Alas. LEXIS 113 (Alaska, 1990)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fishermen filed an action that challenged appellee state's practice of charging nonresident commercial fishermen licensing and permit fees that were three times greater than the fees charged residents. On remand to determine unresolved issues from a prior appeal, the Superior Court of Alaska granted the state's motion for summary judgment. The fishermen appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state charged non-resident commercial fishermen higher fees that it charged residents pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 16.05.480 and Alaska Admin. Code tit. 20, § 5.240. In an earlier appeal, the court had determined that the higher fees were legislatively authorized. It remanded to the trial court for a determination of the constitutional reasonableness of the higher fees and whether the 3:1 ratio bore a sufficiently close relationship to that goal. The trial court adopted the states pro rata approach. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded because the correct analytical approach for privileges and immunities purposes was the per capita formula suggested by the fishermen, which computed the contribution made by each resident to the cost of maintaining the commercial fisheries and compared that with the fee differential. The court remanded for application of that formula with instructions to consider additional budget figures submitted by the state on appropriate procedural terms and to decide if the fisherman were entitled to recover interest pursuant to Alaska Stat. §§ 43.15.010, 43.10.210(a).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's approval of the state's pro rata formula of calculating and comparing the burden placed on resident and nonresident commercial fishermen and remanded for application of the fishermen's per capita formula. It ordered the trial court to address the appropriate budget items and to determine refund issue, and what, if any interest was due on any refund.

 

58 of 606 DOCUMENTS



O'Callaghan v. State


No. 4338, Supreme Court No. S-6249


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


914 P.2d 1250; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 39



April 12, 1996, Decided  
April 12, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge. Superior Court Nos. 3AN-92-08552 CI and 3AN-92-11150 CI (Consolidated).

This Opinion Substituted on Grant of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of March 20, 1996, Previously Reported at: 1996 Alas. LEXIS 23.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voter sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, that granted appellee government's motion for summary judgment. The superior court held that stipulated electoral regulations that provided for two separate ballots for primary elections were not inconsistent with the Alaska Elections Code, Alaska Stat. § 15.25.010 et seq., which provided for a "blanket" primary.


OVERVIEW: The Alaska Election Code, Alaska Stat. § 15.25.010 et seq., provided for a blanket primary in which a voter had the right to vote for any candidate regardless of party affiliation. The Republican Party (RP) enacted a rule the prevented only those registered with another party to vote in its primary. The government entered into stipulated regulations with the RP that provided a blanket primary ballot, and a party ballot under which only republicans, nonpartisan, and undeclared voters were available to nominate the RP's candidates. The court reversed the summary judgment that upheld the regulations because the blanket primary did not violate the RP's freedom of association rights given the government's legitimate reasons for a blanket primary. While the court agreed that a blanket primary allowed "raid voting," the court noted that such voting was also possible under the party rule ballot. The court also held that the government's interest in ensuring that elected officials represented the broad cross-section of the population who were mostly nonpartisan or undeclared were compelling in comparison to the RP's assertion to need more control over party discipline to its principles.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment in favor of the government that upheld the stipulated regulations because the blanket primary statute was constitutional and therefore the stipulated regulations were invalid. The court granted the voter a declaratory judgment that required the next primary to be a blanket primary, but refused to negate the last primaries and elections. The court denied the voter's claim for damages.


 

59 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Chokwak v. Worley


No. 4323, Supreme Court No. S-6353


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


912 P.2d 1248; 1996 Alas. LEXIS 19



March 8, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Dana Fabe, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-686 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant guest challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage (Alaska), that granted summary judgment to appellee hosts in the guest's action to recover for injuries he incurred while driving after being served intoxicating liquor at the hosts' house.


OVERVIEW: The guest, a minor, had been drinking at a party at the hosts' home. The guest was paralyzed in a car accident after leaving the party, and he brought a tort action against the hosts. The hosts claimed statutory immunity from civil liability under Alaska Stat. § 04.21.020. The court held that the trial court's grant of summary judgment for the hosts was proper because by its terms, § 04.21.020 applied only to those licensed to sell alcohol. The court reviewed the legislative history of the statute and concluded that it was designed to protect both licensees and non-licensees from tort liability arising from serving alcohol. The fact that Alaska Stat. § 04.16.051(a) criminally prohibited furnishing alcohol to minors did not imply that a minor had a private tort right of action against the alcohol provider. A cause of action implied from a criminal statute, § 04.16.051(a), could not be harmonized with a later statute, Alaska Stat. § 04.21.020, that expressly abrogated civil liability. The statute's purpose was legitimate and there was a fair and substantial relationship between the purpose and the law, so the provision was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

 

60 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Katmailand, Inc. v. Lake & Peninsula Borough


No. 4265, Supreme Court No. S-6400


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


904 P.2d 397; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 119



October 6, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected October 12, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, John Reese and J. Justin Ripley, Judges. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-7569 CI.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, that awarded summary judgment to appellee borough in the borough's action to have an occupational license tax declared valid and enforceable. The trial court concluded that the tax on lodge operators and guides did not deny them equal protection under the law.


OVERVIEW: The borough enacted an ordinance that imposed an annual permit fee on lodge operators and professional guides. When the taxpayers refused to pay the tax, the borough filed this declaratory judgment action. The taxpayers argued that the tax was impermissible because it violated the equal protection provisions of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Alaska Const. art. I, § 1 because the tax was imposed only on a select group, i.e., lodge owners and guides. On appeal, the court first found that the tax did not affect a fundamental interest or utilize a suspect classification and that it therefore only needed to survive review under the rational basis test. The court then noted that the borough articulated a number of reasons in support of the tax: to encourage new business; to rectify perceived taxation inequities by spreading the charges to businesses which burden services provided by the borough; and to raise revenues. In affirming the trial court, the court determined that the taxpayers failed to present evidence that contradicted the factual basis for the borough's justification for the tax and failed to support their claim that the tax was enacted with no legitimate purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that the guide and lodge tax did not violate either the United States Constitution or the Alaska State Constitution.

Lazy Mountain Land Club v. Matanuska-Susitna Borough Bd. of Adjustment & Appeals


No. 4242, Supreme Court Nos. S-5713, S-5714


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


904 P.2d 373; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 97



September 1, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing Denied October 27, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Palmer, Beverly W. Cutler, Judge. Superior Court No. 3PA-91-852 Civil.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRM


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant land club was denied a conditional use permit by appellee, the borough board of adjustment and appeals. On appeal, the Superior Court of the State of Alaska affirmed the board's denial of the conditional use permit, holding for the borough on all issues except the borough's claim that laches barred the suit. The land club appealed and the borough cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The land club sought a conditional use permit to operate some land as a private commercial refuse area. The borough's planning commission denied the application, as did the board. The trial court, affirming the denial, held for the borough on all issues other than the borough's claim that the suit was barred by laches. Both parties appealed. Affirming the trial court's judgment, the court held that the borough's comprehensive development plan was validly enacted because it was incorporated by reference into a later borough ordinance. The court ruled that Matanuska-Susitna, Alaska, Borough Code § 17.60, the borough zoning ordinance requiring the permit, was not void for vagueness and therefore did not violate the land club's right to due process of law because there was no history of arbitrary or capricious enforcement of the ordinance. Further, the board rationally could have found that § 17.60 applied to the land club's proposed use and therefore that a conditional use permit was required because there was no evidence of a conflicting contemporaneous construction of the statute by the planning commission.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision denying the land club a conditional use permit and finding that laches did not bar the suit.

Carr-Gottstein Properties v. State


No. 4230, Supreme Court No. S-6846


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


899 P.2d 136; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 79



July 14, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-94-4995 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which (1) affirmed a decision of appellee Alaska Department of Natural Resources (DNR) approving a lease-purchase agreement for the Alaska Court System (ACS), and (2) denied the property owner public interest litigant status and awarded attorney's fees to the DNR and appellee State of Alaska.


OVERVIEW: Upon the expiration of a lease between the property owner and the ACS, the ACS decided to enter into a lease-purchase agreement to acquire a different property, not owned by the property owner. The DNR determined that the lease-purchase agreement was in the best interests of the state and, after an initial procedural error, issued a final finding approving the agreement. The superior court affirmed the decision of the DNR and held, inter alia, that: (1) the lease-purchase agreement did not create "debt" as prohibited by Alaska Const. art. IX, § 8; (2) the DNR had a broad statutory grant of authority to purchase land for the state before December 31, 1994 for any purpose by means of a lease-purchase agreement, as long as the agreement was subject to annual appropriation, the lessor was the DNR, and the lessee was the ACS; and (3) the renovation fund approved by the legislature did not violate Alaska Const. art. IX, § 13. In addition, the superior court denied the property owner public interest litigant status and awarded attorney's fees to the state and the DNR. On appeal, the court affirmed the decisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court, which (1) affirmed the DNR's approval of the lease-purchase agreement, and (2) awarded attorney's fees to the state and the DNR.

 

63 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Albert


No. 4225, Supreme Court No. S-5754


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


899 P.2d 103; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 73



June 23, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-89-3009 CR.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a criminal action in which defendant was represented by court-appointed counsel, the district court judge, sua sponte, declared Alaska R. Crim. P. 39 unconstitutional. The state appealed the order to the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, which transferred the appeal by order. Subsequent lower court decisions declaring Rule 39 unconstitutional were appealed and all the cases consolidated.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared Rule 39 unconstitutional, holding that it violated an indigent defendant's rights to counsel and equal protection. The court held that the right to counsel was not violated because the recoupment judgments were nondiscriminatory, there were no correctional consequences if payment was not made, recoupment debtors had the same protections against hardship as did civil debtors, recoupment debtors were notified of the possibility of a judgment and given an opportunity to oppose the entry of judgment before it was entered, and there was no evidence that the recoupment system resulted in indigents refusing counsel at a significantly higher rate than non-indigents. The court also held that: (1) the equal protection guarantee of the state constitution was not violated because Rule 39's challenged provisions served a legitimate purpose and the means employed by Rule 39 were substantially related to the purpose of fair but efficient collection; and (2) Rule 39 did not violate the federal constitutional right to equal protection because the purpose of Rule 39's classification was legitimate and the means chosen were rationally related to that end.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the trial courts in the consolidated cases that found a criminal rule allowing cost recoupment for indigent defendants unconstitutional.

Chiropractors for Justice v. State


No. 4210, Supreme Court No. S-5648


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


895 P.2d 962; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 51



May 19, 1995, Decided  
May 19, 1995, Filed, ENTERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Peter A. Michalski, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-90-2198 CI.

This Opinion Substituted by the Court for Withdrawn Opinion of May 12, 1995, Previously Reported at: 1995 Alas. LEXIS 50.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant chiropractors filed an action against appellees, state and state workers' compensation board (state), asserting that Alaska Stat. § 23.30.095(c), a frequency-of-treatment statute and the corresponding legislation, Alaska Admin. Code tit. 8, § 45.082(f) violated their federal and state guarantees of due process, equal protection, and privacy.


OVERVIEW: The state adopted new procedures for payment of workers' compensation benefits for continuing and multiple treatments of a similar nature through Alaska Stat. § 23.30.095(c), requiring the physician or health care provider to submit a written treatment plan to the employer and employee within 14 days after treatment began. The statute and its regulations, Alaska Admin. Code tit 8, § 045.082(f) and (g) excused the employer and employee from paying for treatments exceeding the frequency standards if the treatment plan was not furnished within 14 days. The chiropractors claimed that the statute and regulation violated their right to equal protection. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the state. On appeal, the summary judgment in favor of the state was affirmed, and the court determined that there was a logical connection between limiting the process by which physicians and health care providers received payment for repetitive, similar and frequent treatments and the legitimate governmental purposes of ensuring the quick, efficient, fair, and predictable delivery of indemnity and medical benefits to injured workers at a reasonable cost to the employers.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of the state was affirmed.

 

65 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Department of Revenue


No. 4208, Supreme Court No. S-5722


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


895 P.2d 99; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 52



May 19, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Joan M. Katz, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-4757 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which rejected the employee's claim that Alaska Stat. §§ 23.30.120(c) and 23.30.265(17) were unconstitutional and affirmed the denial of benefits for stress-related mental injury under the Alaska Workers' Compensation Act (Act). Review was granted under Alaska R. App. P. 402(b)(2).


OVERVIEW: The employee filed a claim for stress-related mental injuries under the Act. The Alaska Workers' Compensation Board (board) denied her claim. The superior court affirmed the board's decision. On review, the employee argued that the Act was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the superior court's decision and ruled that Alaska Stat. §§ 23.30.120(c) and 23.30.265(17) were not unconstitutional. The statutes removed the presumption for compensability of mental injuries and enacted the "extraordinary and unusual" test to define a mental injury. The court ruled that a reasonable basis existed for the legislative action adopting an objective standard to determine whether mental injuries were compensable. Thus, the statutes did not violate the employee's substantive due process rights. The court further ruled that the distinctions under the statutes between physically injured workers and workers with mental injuries was substantially related to the state's legitimate goal of efficiently and fairly distributing benefits while cutting costs for employers. Therefore, the statutes did not violate the employee's equal protection rights under the federal or state constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's decision, which affirmed the board's denial of benefits to the employee for her claim of stress-related mental injuries under the Alaska Workers' Compensation Act.

 

66 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Shepherd v. State


No. 4204, Supreme Court Nos. S-5668/S-5698


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


897 P.2d 33; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 56



May 19, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing Denied June 30, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-90-1514 Civil.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED, in part, REVERSED, in part, and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs guides brought suit against defendant state to challenge to constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 16.05.255(d). The Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District upheld the statute. The guides appealed. Both parties also appealed the trial court's decision denying of attorney fees to either party.


OVERVIEW: The guides argued that Alaska Stat. § 16.05.255(d) violated the Equal Protection Clause and the Uniform Application Clause of the Alaska Constitution and the Equal Protection Clause, the Commerce Clause and the Privileges and Immunities Clause of the United States Constitution. The court determined that the trial court correctly found for the state on the guides' constitutional claims. The guides did not have standing, as an Alaska resident, to challenge Alaska Stat. § 16.05.255(d) under the federal Privileges and Immunities Clause. The guides' Commerce Clause claim also failed, since unharvested game was not an article of interstate commerce and the restriction on his right to guide nonresident hunters was a permissible incidental effect of the regulation. The guides' equal protection claims also fail. Resident and nonresident hunters, and their guides, were not similarly situated with respect to access to the state's wildlife resources. Finally, although the trial court did not abuse its discretion in determining that the guides were not public interest litigants, the court erred in finding that the state was not the prevailing party.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision with respect to the guides' constitutional challenges and pendent state law claims. However, the court determined that the trial court erred in ruling that the state was not the prevailing party. Therefore, the court reversed the trial court's denial of attorney fees to the state.

State v. Kenaitze Indian Tribe


No. 4201, Supreme Court No. S-6162


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


894 P.2d 632; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 46



May 9, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing Denied June 2, 1995.
Appeal after remand at State v. Kenaitze Indian Tribe, 2004 Alas. LEXIS 9 (Alaska, Jan. 16, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Dana Fabe, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-91-4569 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and the Commissioner of the Alaska Department of Fish and Game, sought review of a summary judgment in favor of appellees, Indian tribes and organizations, by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska Third Judicial District Anchorage (Anchorage), which ruled that Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(c), violated Alaska Const. art. VII, § 3, 15, 17. The trial court severed Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(c), and declared the other claims moot.


OVERVIEW: The Indian tribes and organizations filed suit against the state and the Commissioner of the Alaska Department of Fish and Game and sought a judicial declaration that the Indian tribes and organizations were entitled to operate a communal set net on a river and that the state was not managing salmon stock in accordance with the subsistence priority as required by law. Nonsubsistence areas were established in 1992 and the Indian tribes and organizations claimed that the nonsubsistence areas violated its members' state constitutional rights under the equal access clauses of Alaska Const. art. VII, § 3,15,17. The trial court found that Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(c), was unconstitutional and declared the other claims moot. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded and held that: (1) Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(b)(4)(B)(ii), which used the proximity of the domicile of the Tier II subsistence permit applicant to the fish or game population which the applicant wished to harvest as a basis for the applicant's eligibility, violated Alaska Const. art. VII, §§ 3, 15, 17, and (2) Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(c), without the domicile proximity requirement, was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and held that the part of the statute that barred Alaska residents from participation in certain subsistence activities based on where the residents lived was unconstitutional. The court held that the statutory section that mandated the creation of nonsubsistence areas did not violate the Alaska Constitution.

Bauman v. Day


No. 4182, Supreme Court Nos. S-5350/S-5368


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


892 P.2d 817; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 30



March 24, 1995, Decided  
March 24, 1995, Filed, Entered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Richard D. Savell, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-92-123 CI.

This Opinion Substituted on Denial of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of November 25, 1994, Previously Reported at: 1994 Alas. LEXIS 116.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant buyers challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which dismissed their fraud and contract claims filed against appellee sellers. Appellees cross-appealed the trial court's setting aside of the foreclosure sale of the real property at issue.


OVERVIEW: Appellant buyers filed a complaint alleging fraud and breach of contract against appellee sellers on the day that appellees attempted to foreclose on the land at issue. The trial court set aside the foreclosure and dismissed appellants' fraud and contract complaints on the ground that the statute of limitations for each cause of action had run. Appellants challenged the court's dismissal of their claims, and appellees challenged the setting aside of the foreclosure sale. The court concluded that the trial court had not erred in using its equitable powers to set aside the foreclosure sale. The court also held that appellants' fraud claim was subject to Alaska Stat. § 09.10.070, the two-year statute of limitations that began to run when they stopped making payments. The 10-year statute of limitations in Alaska Stat. § 09.10.230 was not applicable because the ownership interest of the property itself was not at issue. The court applied the discovery rule to common law contract causes of action that brought appellants' contract claims within the statute of limitations period. The trial court's judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in part and reversed in part because the trial court had authority to use its equitable powers to set aside appellee sellers' nonjudicial foreclosure sale. Appellants' fraud claims were untimely because the discovery of the problem had taken place after the statute of limitations period, but the contract claims were timely due to the application of the discovery rule.

O'Callaghan v. Coghill


Order No. 31, Supreme Court No. S-6249


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


888 P.2d 1302; 1995 Alas. LEXIS 6



February 3, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Superior Court Nos. 3AN-92-08552 CI & 3AN-92-11150 CI (Consolidated).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voter challenged a judgment of the Superior Court (Alaska), which denied the voter's motion for a temporary restraining order to prevent appellee state from implementing permanent regulations providing for separate ballots. The trial court granted summary judgment to the state.


OVERVIEW: The voter challenged regulations that exempted the Republican Party from Alaska's blanket primary statute. The voter entered into a stipulation with the state that emergency temporary regulations were to be adopted providing for a separate primary ballot for the Republican Party. The voter then challenged the legality of next primary election and sought to enjoin the state from making the temporary regulations permanent. The trial court granted summary judgment to the state. The court held that the stipulation was invalid because it declared a law invalid. The court held that neither the stipulation nor the blanket primary statute, Alaska Stat. § 15.25.060, could have upheld the challenged regulations; therefore, the court needed to decide whether § 15.25.060 was constitutional. As the parties had not briefed the issue adequately, the court invited supplementary briefing on the issue.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that supplementary briefing on the issue of the constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 15.25.060 was necessary in order to decide whether the regulations were valid.

Alaskans for Legislative Reform v. State


No. 4158, Supreme Court No. S-5717


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


887 P.2d 960; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 126



December 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian Shortell, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-7079 CI.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which entered judgment in favor of appellees, the State and the Lieutenant Governor, and dismissed the citizen's challenge to the Lieutenant Governor's denial of certification of an initiative application for term limits.


OVERVIEW: The citizens supported an initiative proposal that would, if passed, limit the terms of members of the Alaska legislature to two consecutive senate terms, four consecutive house terms, or eight consecutive years in any combination of house and senate service. The Lieutenant Governor denied certification of the initiative. The citizens commenced suit against the State and the Lieutenant Governor. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the State and the Lieutenant Governor and dismissed the complaint. The citizens sought review. The court affirmed. The court held: (1) Alaska Const. art. II, §§ 2, 3, and 5 contained no language limiting the number of terms that a legislator may serve; (2) the qualifications of the legislators as set out in the Alaska Constitution were not subject to change by the legislature or by initiative absent specific constitutional authority; (3) the term limits, as imposed by the initiative, rise to such as level as to constitute a qualification for office and would unconstitutionally limit the right of the people to choose their lawmakers; and (4) the Lieutenant Governor was correct in making his decision to deny certification of the initiative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which had entered judgment in favor of the State and the Lieutenant Governor and dismissed the citizen's challenge to the Lieutenant Governor's denial of certification of an initiative application for term limits.
