Dewey v. Dewey


No. 4149, Supreme Court No. S-5572


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


886 P.2d 623; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 114



November 25, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 21, 1994. Petition for Rehearing Denied January 7, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Petersburg, Thomas M. Jahnke, Judge. Superior Court No. 1PE-85-04 DR



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which denied his motion for relief from a judgment for arrearages in child support pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 60(b) and Alaska Stat. § 25.24.170.


OVERVIEW: The ex-husband was divorced from appellee ex-wife and agreed to pay child support for both their son, born during the marriage, and the ex-husband's stepdaughter, born to the ex-wife before the marriage. When a judgment for support arrearages was entered against him seven years later, he filed a motion for relief from judgment in which he sought to terminate the support obligation as to his stepdaughter. The trial court denied the motion, and he appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the ex-husband had entered into an enforceable contractual obligation to support the stepdaughter, creating an exception to the general rule that no such duty existed. Any mistake as to his legal obligation made at the time of the agreement did not warrant modification. The court held that the ex-husband did not provide sufficient evidence of a substantial and material change in circumstances to justify modifying the child support obligation under § 25.24.170 because he did not show how his income had decreased or of how his ex-wife's remarriage had altered her ability to support the stepdaughter. Termination of the support obligation would not have been in the stepdaughter's best interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's denial of the ex-husband's motion for relief from the judgment of arrearages in his support obligation for his stepdaughter.

McCarter v. Alaska Nat'l Ins. Co.


No. 4140, Supreme Court No. S-5746


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


883 P.2d 986; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 106



November 4, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-92-1780 CI



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured worker challenged a declaratory judgment by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, ordering the worker to reimburse appellee worker's compensation carrier after a recovery from the third party that caused the injury.


OVERVIEW: The worker's first argument was that the legislature never intended Alaska Stat. § 23.30.015(g) to apply to cases like his. But the worker was able to sue the third party without jeopardizing the payments the carrier had already made to him. The court concluded that the superior court's decision requiring reimbursement was consistent with the command of § 23.30.015(g). The worker also asserted that § 23.30.015(g) violated substantive and procedural due process. Although he maintained that § 23.30.015(g) was irrational, he acknowledged the legitimacy of its underlying purpose. Thus, the worker could not meet his heavy burden of demonstrating that no rational basis for the challenged law existed. The worker attacked the legislative policy but did not allege that the statute was enforced arbitrarily. Thus, he has failed to state a valid procedural due process claim. The statute implicated no fundamental right. More importantly, there was a rational basis for § 23.30.015 in general and subsection (g) in particular. The declaratory judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The declaratory judgment in favor of the workers' compensation carrier on its claim for reimbursement from the worker, after the worker recovered a settlement from a third-party, was affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother, whose children were in state custody, sought review of the decision by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which held that the mother could not take her children with her when she sought to petition to redress her grievances with the government and that she could not release certain confidential information. The trial court denied the mother's motion for reconsideration.

Because the statutory amendment was directly pertinent to the case, the court ordered a vacation of the order.

Gilmore v. Alaska Workers' Compensation Bd.


No. 4135, Supreme Court No. S-4765


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


882 P.2d 922; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 99



October 14, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Order Regarding Emergency Motion For Stay of Decision of November 22, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Alas. LEXIS 139. As Corrected November 22, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, Thomas E. Schulz, Judge. Trial Court No. 1KE-90-891 Civil



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which affirmed appellee worker's compensation board's award of benefits in accordance with Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220(a), a section which prescribed the wage base on which an injured worker's disability benefits were calculated. The court, sua sponte, ordered the parties to file supplemental memoranda addressing certain questions.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the formula contained in Alaska Stat. § 220(a) would result in substantially different compensation rates for workers who appeared to be in important respects similarly situated. The court concluded that the means chosen for determining an injured worker's gross weekly wage did not bear a substantial relationship to that goal of compensating injured workers based on their actual losses. Therefore, the court held that that the Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220(a) formula violated the equal protection clause of the Alaska Constitution. Accordingly, the court reversed the trial court's determination and remanded the matter for a recalculation of the claimant's benefits.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the determination of the claimant's weekly compensation rate as it was based on an unconstitutional statutory requirement. On remand, the court instructed that the board should recalculate the claimant's gross weekly earnings by using the alternative method.

In the Disciplinary Matter Involving Burrell


No. 30, Supreme Court No. S-6178


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


882 P.2d 1257; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 124



October 14, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ABA File No. 1990P001. (District Court No. 3AN-S90-3892 CR).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent attorney moved to dismiss the Alaska Bar Association's petition to revoke the attorney's probation. The bar association sough to revoke the probation on grounds that the attorney had practiced law while on suspended status, in violation of the terms of his probation and Alaska Bar R. 15.


OVERVIEW: The Alaska Bar Association filed a petition seeking to revoke the attorney's probation on grounds that he had practiced law while on suspended status in violation of the terms of his probation. The attorney was convicted of criminal contempt and was required to serve a prison term followed by a period of probation. The court denied the attorney's motion to dismiss the bar association's petition and concluded that the evidence was sufficient to support a finding that the attorney had violation the conditions of his probation. The evidence showed that the attorney appeared in court in his capacity as an officer of the corporation, which was permitted under the district court rules. But given the prohibition against a corporation appearing without counsel and the attorney's appearance and behavior in court and signature on an assignment, it was clearly demonstrated that he was acting in the capacity of an attorney. There was also evidence that the attorney wrote letters of the type an attorney would write on behalf of clients and evidence that he advise a litigant in the course of his divorce. As such, the court revoked the attorney's probation and scheduled a sentencing hearing.


OUTCOME: The court denied the attorney's motion to dismiss the Alaska Bar Association's petition to revoke his probation. The court revoked the attorney's probation and directed the clerk to schedule a sentencing hearing.

Gonzales v. Safeway Stores


No. 4131, Supreme Court No. S-5224


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


882 P.2d 389; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 94



October 7, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen Hunt, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-86-14160 CI



DISPOSITION: The judgment is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured passenger sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which was in favor of appellee grocery store and held that the grocery store did not make a criminally negligent sale of alcoholic beverage to a drunken person.


OVERVIEW: The injured passenger filed an action against the grocery store claiming that the store had negligently and recklessly sold liquor to the other occupants of the vehicle, under circumstances in which the store's clerks either knew or should have known that such occupants were intoxicated. After deciding various motions, the trial court entered judgment for the store and the passenger filed motions for a new trial and judgment notwithstanding the verdict. The trial court denied the motions, and the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that the jury's conclusion that the store did not with criminal negligence sell to a drunken person meant that the store was immune under Alaska Stat. § 04.21.020 for all unlawful providing claims. The court held that even if Alaska Stat. § 04.21.030 stated duties giving rise to tort liability, such liability was nonetheless subject to the rule of immunity expressed in § 04.21.020. The court found that the evidence could have reasonably supported a conclusion that the driver was not a drunken person. The court found that the standard for liability higher than ordinary negligence was justified and did not violate equal protection guarantees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that was against the injured passenger and in favor of the grocery store that the store did not make a criminally negligent sale of alcoholic beverage to a drunken person.

 

79 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Underwood v. State


No. 4124, Supreme Court No. S-5802


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


881 P.2d 322; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 89



September 30, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge. Superior Court No. 4FA-93-234 Civil



DISPOSITION: The State's amendment to the eligibility statute for 1993 PFDs did not violate the Underwoods' constitutional rights. Nor is the State equitably estopped from denying the Underwoods a 1993 dividend. Accordingly, the superior court's order granting summary judgment in favor of the State is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, granted the motion for summary judgment filed by defendants, State, Governor, and Department of Revenue. Plaintiff residents filed an action that challenged the constitutionality of a 1992 amendment to the permanent fund dividend (PFD) statutes, 1992 Alaska Session Laws ch. 4, § 4. The amendment changed the qualifying date set forth in Alaska Stat. § 43.23.005(a).


OVERVIEW: The residents timed their move to Alaska so that they would qualify for a 1993 PFD. The family arrived on March 25, 1992. On March 31, 1992, the Governor signed the amendment, changed the eligibility requirements for a PFD. As a result, applicants had to show Alaska residency as of January 1, 1992 to be eligible for a 1993 PFD. The residents filed a lawsuit. The trial court granted defendants' motion for summary judgment. The court affirmed. Because the challenged amendment derived from a legitimate governmental objective, and the means bore a fair and substantial relation to that objective, the amendment survived minimal scrutiny under Alaska's Constitution. The residents had no property right in a 1993 PFD, thus, there was no due process violation. The amendment did not unfairly or unreasonably impinge upon any property rights or settled expectations. Therefore, the amendment did not violate the prohibition against retroactive legislation. The State had engaged in no conduct encouraging the residents to move or in any way guaranteeing that they would qualify for a 1993 PFD if they arrived in March. The State was not equitably estopped from denying the residents their 1993 PFDs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

80 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Campion v. Department of Community & Regional Affairs, Hous. Assistance Div.


No. 4096, Supreme Court No. S-5547


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


876 P.2d 1096; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 61



July 1, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, J. Justin Ripley, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-1593 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant debtor sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which granted appellee state's motion for summary judgment in the state's action to recover a deficiency judgment against the debtor.


OVERVIEW: After the state obtained a default judgment against the debtor in a suit to foreclose upon a deed of trust, the state filed a suit in federal court to recover the deficiency from the sale. The federal court dismissed the suit because the state had failed to properly serve the debtor in the foreclosure suit, so the default judgment against the debtor was void. Subsequently, the state filed an action in the superior court to recover the deficiency judgment, and the superior court granted the state's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the state was collaterally estopped from relying on the void default judgment. The court further held that the federal court's decision regarding the improper service on the debtor was entitled to preclusive effect as well as full faith and credit because the issue of service of process upon the debtor had been fully litigated by the parties in the federal suit. Finally, the court held that the federal court's interpretation of Alaska R. Civ. P. 4(h) was entitled to full faith and credit and precluded the state from relitigating the issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order of the superior court, which granted the state's motion for summary judgment in its action to recover a deficiency judgment against the debtor.

 

81 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Moore v. Department of Transp. & Pub. Facilities


No. 4093, Supreme Court No. S-5324


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


875 P.2d 765; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 57



June 17, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-92-4102 CI.



DISPOSITION: The superior court's order granting summary judgment is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state worker sought review of a summary judgment entered in favor of appellee state agency by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska relative to the privatization of the maintenance of a state-operated airport.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to the state worker's contesting his discharge due to the state's desire to cut costs by privatizing the maintenance at a state-owned airport, he filed a motion for summary judgment on his claim. The state agency opposed the motion and filed a cross motion for summary judgment, which was granted. The state worker argued that Alaska Const. art. XII, § 6 forbade state agencies from seeking to reduce spending by privatizing state jobs. The court affirmed the superior court's entry of summary judgment in favor of the state agency because the merit principle, expressed in art. XII, § 6, allowed state agencies broad discretion to eliminate positions and order layoffs for reasons of efficiency and economy, provided that their decisions were not politically motivated. Although the State Personnel Act, Alaska Stat. § 39.25.010-.220, included state jobs as well as state workers within the ambit of the Alaska Constitution's merit system language, the court found that the drafters of the Alaska Constitution made it clear that the merit principle was not intended to impede the efficient management of state affairs.


OUTCOME: The superior court's order granting summary judgment was affirmed because the court found that the Alaska Constitution did not categorically bar the privatization of state jobs for economic reasons.

 

82 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Hickel v. Cowper


No. 4089, Supreme Court Nos. S-6294/6304


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


874 P.2d 922; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 51



May 27, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petitions for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, John Reese, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-93-6848 CI.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Superior court is AFFIRMED, for the reason stated in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, governor, commissioner of revenue, and state, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted respondent former governor's motion for partial summary judgment and declared that Alaska Stat. § 37.10.420 was unconstitutional under Alaska Const. art. IX, § 17 on the grounds that it unduly limited funds counted as available for appropriation.


OVERVIEW: Respondent former governor challenged the constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 37.10.420, which defined terms governing the legislature's ability to withdraw funds from the budget reserve fund by a simple majority vote. Petitioners, governor, commissioner of revenue, and state, also filed a motion for partial summary judgment, claiming the statute was constitutional. The trial court held that § 37.10.420 was unconstitutional because it unduly limited the funds counted as available for appropriation. The court affirmed on different grounds, holding that the "amount available for appropriation" within the meaning of Alaska Const. art. IX, § 17 included all monies over which the legislature retained power to appropriate and that required further appropriation before expenditure. All amounts actually appropriated, whether or not they would have been considered available prior to appropriation, were available within the meaning of art. IX, § 17. Because § 37.10.420 failed to include several funds in the "amount available for appropriation" that were in fact available, it did not provide an accurate definition of the constitutional term.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision in favor of respondent former governor on his claim that a statute purporting to define terms relating to the legislature's ability to withdraw from the budget reserve fund was unconstitutional. The phrase "amount available for appropriation" included all monies over which the legislature retained the power to appropriate and that required further appropriation before expenditure.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which reversed a decision of a workers' compensation board terminating appellee employee's workers' compensation benefits.


OVERVIEW: The employee was awarded temporary total disability benefits, but after the benefits were terminated, he claimed that his benefits were terminated in violation of the federal and state constitutions. Swanner v. Anchorage Equal Rights Comm'n

DISPOSITION: The AERC's final order and the superior court's opinion are AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, that affirmed an order from the Anchorage Equal Rights Commission (AERC), which found that the landlord's policy against renting to unmarried couples constituted unlawful discrimination based on marital status.

In affirming, the court held that the landlord unlawfully discriminated against the tenants based on their marital status. The court further held that enforcing the fair housing laws did not deprive him of his right to free exercise of his religion and the proceedings of the AERC did not deprive him of his right to due process of law.

Morry v. State


No. 4076, Supreme Court No. S-4686


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


872 P.2d 1209; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 36



April 29, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial District, Barrow, Michael I. Jeffery, Judge. Superior Court No. 2BA-87-83 CI



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both parties sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Second Judicial District, (Alaska), which dismissed plaintiff hunter's tort claims against defendants, the State and the Trooper, for compensation for their enforcement against him of the State's subsistence hunting regulations, which were subsequently determined to be invalid.


OVERVIEW: The hunter, who had a valid hunting license, was accustomed to feed his family by bear hunting. The State filed a criminal complaint against the hunter for failure to comply with the subsistence hunting regulations, Alaska Admin. Code tit. 5, §§ 92.012(c), 92.165. The state later voluntarily dismissed the criminal charges. The hunter brought an action against the State and the Trooper for declaratory relief and damages. The trial court found the regulations invalid and dismissed the hunter's tort claims, and both parties sought review. The court held that: (1) under the Alaska Tort Claims Act, Alaska Stat. § 09.50.250(1), there was no liability because the Trooper had exercised due care in the enforcement of the regulation, even though the regulation was invalid; (2) even if the state agency acted in excess of its authority or failed to comply with the requirements of the statute, it was immune from suit under the discretionary function exception to the statute; and (3) pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 16.05.255(b), the state agency had discretionary policymaking authority and not mere ministerial authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that dismissed the hunter's tort claims against the State and the Trooper for having enforced unconstitutional regulations against him.

 

86 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Department of Corrections


No. 4074, Supreme Court No. S-5336


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


872 P.2d 1218; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 35



April 29, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Palmer, Karen L. Hunt, Judge. Superior Court No. 3PA-90-615 CI



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, the decision of the superior court is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parolee appealed a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which granted appellee state's motion for summary judgment in the parolee's suit against appellees, state, parole board, and officials, after the board imposed supplemental conditions of parole.


OVERVIEW: After the board imposed supplemental conditions of parole on the parolee, he filed a suit against the board, challenging the regulations and procedures under which the conditions were imposed. When the superior court granted the state's motion for summary judgment, the parolee appealed. The court affirmed. The parolee was given an opportunity to be heard and to represent his interests. That was all that due process required. The parolee claimed that due process required 15 days to object, rather than the 10 days which he was given. However, he filed a opposition within four days, did not ask for more time, and did not show how he was prejudiced by the 10-day period. His claim that an unduly short response period violated his due process rights was correctly rejected. A board member who imposed the conditions gave reasons which were minimally sufficient. The board approved all search conditions imposed. The parolee had an opportunity to object to each condition. The purpose of the hearing was different from the hearing given those eligible for discretionary parole. Discretionary and mandatory parolees were not similarly situated. Thus, the parolee's equal protection argument failed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court, which granted the state's motion for summary judgment in the parolee's suit, which challenged the regulations and procedures under which the supplemental conditions were imposed.

Peloza v. Freas


No. 4067, Supreme Court No. S-4772/4796


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


871 P.2d 687; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 28



March 25, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai. Superior Court No. 3KN-91-695 CI. Charles K. Cranston, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant political candidate sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which dismissed his constitutional challenge to the three-year residency requirement imposed upon city council candidates under Kenia, Alaska, City Charter § 2-1(b). Appellee city clerk had rejected the candidate's nominating petition because it did not show compliance with the residency requirement.


OVERVIEW: The candidate's petition to run for city council was rejected by the city clerk because the candidate had failed to attest that he had been a resident of the city for three years, as required by Kenia, Alaska, City Charter § 2-1(b). After the candidate unsuccessfully filed an action challenging the legality of § 2-1(b), he appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's finding that § 2-1(b) was constitutional. The court held that although the issue was moot, because the election had been held, it would address the constitutional issue raised under the public interest exception to the mootness doctrine. It found that the city was not an indispensable party and that its interests had been represented through the city clerk. The court noted that although the candidate had challenged the residency requirement on federal equal protection grounds, it was more appropriate to analyze the issue based upon the more demanding state equal rights provision of Alaska Const. art. I, § 1. It found that the right to seek public office was of extreme importance, required the use of a strict scrutiny analysis, and that three years was an unacceptably long time to burden voters' rights.


OUTCOME: The court held that the three-year residency requirement imposed upon Kenai city council candidates violated the equal rights clause of the Alaska Constitution and reversed the trial court's judgment that had upheld the constitutionality of the residency requirement.

Hickel v. Southeast Conference


No. 4055, Supreme Court File Nos. S-5553/5573, S-5093/5154


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


868 P.2d 919; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 17



February 18, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Superior Court File No. 1JU-91-01608. Larry R. Weeks, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, State of Alaska and Governor of Alaska, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau granting an award of attorney's fees to appellees, Alaska Democratic Party (ADP) and non-profit corporations, pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 82.


OVERVIEW: Appellees brought an action challenging the state legislative redistricting plan. The superior court held that plan violated the Alaska Constitution. After final action on an interim redistricting plan, appellees sought attorney's fees pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 82. The superior court found each appellee to be a prevailing party and awarded appellees full reasonable attorney's fees. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the superior court. The court held that (1) the award of attorney's fees was not apportionable by issue, (2) attorney's fees for post-trial proceedings was proper when the attorney's services were rendered prior to final judgment being entered in superior court, (3) appellee ADP was a prevailing party as the Reapportionment Board (Alaska) violated the Open Meetings Act and the Public Records Act despite the superior court not awarding judgment on these issues, (4) the award of attorney's fees for work used for Department of Justice proceedings was proper when also used in present litigation, (5) expert witness fees were not recoverable under Alaska Administrative Rule 7(c), and (6) appellees were not entitled to attorney's fees for appellate work.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court in awarding full attorney's fee on behalf of appellees.

Tongass Sport Fishing Ass'n v. State


No. 4043, Supreme Court No. S-5202


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


866 P.2d 1314; 1994 Alas. LEXIS 4



January 14, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, No. 1JU-92-938 CIVIL. Walter L. Carpeneti, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sport fishing associations sought review of a summary judgment that was granted by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska in favor of appellee Alaska Board of Fisheries (board), in the association's lawsuit that challenged the board's regulation, which allocated the number of chinook salmon that could be harvested by commercial seiners and gillnetters, commercial trollers, and sport fishers in southeast Alaska.


OVERVIEW: The sportfishing associations contended that the regulation was preferential and that it violated the "common use" clause of Alaska Const. art. VIII, § 3 and the "no exclusive right of fishery" clause of Alaska Const. art. VIII, § 15, because those sections embodied a clear policy in favor of general public access to the natural resources of the state, with no special privilege accorded to any individual group. In affirming summary judgment in favor of the board, the court held that (1) Alaska Const. art. VIII limitations on the state's power to restrict access to natural resource user groups did not apply to the state's authority to allocate fishery resources among sport, commercial, and subsistence users, (2) the board possessed the authority to allocate chinook between sport and commercial users, pursuant to the Pacific Salmon Treaty, Jan. 28, 1985, U.S.-Can., 16 U.S.C.S. §§ 3631-3644, (3) The board's allocation decision was not a limitation on admission to a particular user group, and (4) the regulation was not violative of the "common use" or "no exclusive right of fishery" clauses of Alaska Const. art. VIII.


OUTCOME: The grant of summary judgment in favor of the board and against the sportfishing associations was affirmed, in the associations' action that challenged the board's regulation, which allocated the number of chinook salmon that could be harvested by commercial seiners and gillnetters, commercial trollers, and sport fishers in southeast Alaska.

Haynes v. Department of Pub. Safety


No. 4040, Supreme Court File No. S-4677


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


865 P.2d 753; 1993 Alas. LEXIS 131



December 30, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, Superior Court File No. 1KE-90-914 CI. Thomas E. Schultz, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which affirmed the decision of appellee department of public safety to revoke his driver's license based on the results of a breath test for alcohol. Appellant contended that the department erred in considering evidence regarding appellant's appearance and behavior in determining whether his actual breath alcohol level was over .10 grams.


OVERVIEW: The department revoked appellant's driver's license after receiving the results of his breath alcohol test, which indicated that his breath alcohol was .106. The hearing officer considered the margin of error inherent in appellant's test result, which meant that his actual breath alcohol content could have been as low as .096, but declined to apply it in appellant's favor. Instead, the hearing officer relied on the arresting officers' testimony that appellant appeared to be intoxicated, to find that appellant's breath alcohol content was .10 or higher at the time of the test. Consequently, the department revoked his license. The superior court affirmed the department's order, and appellant challenged the judgment. The court reversed, concluding that Alaska Stat. § 28.15.165(c) did not provide for consideration of other factors or circumstances, such as the appearance and behavior of the individual, in determining whether the person's breath did, in fact, contain the requisite level of alcohol. Therefore, the hearing officer should not have considered evidence regarding appellant's appearance and behavior in determining whether his actual breath alcohol level was over .10 grams.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the department to revoke appellant's driver's license and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Public Safety challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Palmer County (Alaska), which reversed the revocation of appellee driver's license for driving while intoxicated pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 28.15.165(a).


OVERVIEW: The administrative hearing officer revoked the driver's license based on prior convictions in the State and a foreign jurisdiction. The superior court reversed the administrative hearing officer, holding that the Department had not met its burden of proving the constitutional validity of the driver's foreign conviction. The driver argued that the foreign conviction should not have been a factor in the decision to revoke the license because the in-state conviction and foreign conviction were based on dissimilar statutes. The Department argued that the superior court had misallocated its burden of proving the constitutionality of the foreign conviction. The court agreed and reversed the superior court's decision. The court found that even though the superior court had the jurisdiction to consider the validity of the driver's conviction in the foreign jurisdiction it had misallocated the Department's burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The superior court's judgment that reversed the decision to revoke the driver's license was reversed and remanded.

Dep't of Revenue, Permanent Fund Dividend Div. v. Cosio


No. 3998, Supreme Court No. S-4344


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


858 P.2d 621; 1993 Alas. LEXIS 86



August 20, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Trial Court No. 3AN-89-7128 Civil. Peter A. Michalski, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state department of revenue (state) challenged a decision by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which ruled that a regulation defining "state resident" to determine entitlement to the state's permanent fund dividend was invalid. Respondents, illegal aliens, were initially granted such dividends from the state but were later declared ineligible and asked to repay the state.


OVERVIEW: The court held that Alaska Admin. Code tit. 15, § 23.615(d), the regulation defining "state resident," was authorized by statute. It explained that Alaska Stat. § 43.23.015(a) delegated authority to the commissioner to regulate the eligibility of individuals for permanent fund dividends. The court also held that the enacted regulation was constitutional. It explained that the illegal aliens did not fall within any of the categories requiring heightened constitutional scrutiny under the federal Equal Protection Clause. Thus, the state's dividend eligibility requirement only warranted rational basis review. The court believed that the program's permanent residence requirement, as interpreted to require legal residence, was rationally related to the attainment of the legitimate purposes of the statute. In its analysis under the Alaska Equal Protection clause, the court found that limiting the distribution of dividends to those who were lawful permanent residents was rationally related to legitimate objectives of the dividend program and that the limitation was fairly and substantially related to these goals.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision which invalidated the regulation defining "state resident" for purposes of distributing the state's permanent fund dividend and remanded the case.

Dilley v. Ketchikan Gateway Borough


No. 3977, Supreme Court File No. S-5408


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


855 P.2d 1335; 1993 Alas. LEXIS 66



July 23, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Superior Court File No. 1KE-91-01024 Civil. Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, Thomas M. Jahnke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which affirmed a determination of the borough assembly denying his appeal and upholding an assessment by appellee borough of sales tax and interest on pull-tab gambling sales. The taxpayer claimed that pull-tabs were intangible personal property not subject to the borough's sales tax. The borough claimed that they were taxable amusement services.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer paid sales tax on the net proceeds of pull-tab sales, deducting prizes from his gross receipts before payment. The borough demanded sales tax on gross receipts and assessed an estimated tax plus interest when the taxpayer did not comply. The taxpayer unsuccessfully appealed to the borough assembly and the superior court, and again appealed to the court. The taxpayer alternatively argued that the sales tax applied only to the net proceeds of pull-tab sales, was unconstitutional, and did not apply to pull-tab sales at all. The court reversed the superior court, holding that pull-tabs were intangible property not subject to the borough's sales tax. The court explained that a pull-tab was a contractual right to receive payment in the event the owner received a winning combination of numbers. As such, pull-tabs fit the definition of intangible property. The court noted that the borough's sales tax ordinance could have included pull-tab sales, as the State of Alaska had done in Alaska Stat. § 05.15.184.


OUTCOME: The court found for the taxpayer, reversed the superior court, and directed the superior court to enter judgment in favor of the taxpayer consistent with the court's opinion.

State v. Gonzalez


No. 3962, Supreme Court No. S-5003


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


853 P.2d 526; 1993 Alas. LEXIS 53; 5 A.L.R. 2204; 29 A.L.R.5th 747



June 4, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Court of Appeals No. A-4063. Superior Court Nos. 3AN-S91-693 Cr., 3AN-S91-694 Cr. Rene Gonzalez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed the ruling of the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the trial court's judgment holding that Alaska Stat. § 12.50.101 was unconstitutional under Alaska Const. art. I, § 9. The statute authorized an order compelling testimony based on a grant of use and derivative use immunity.


OVERVIEW: In a murder trial, the State attempted to compel the testimony of a co-defendant under § 12.50.101, which allowed the State to compel a witness to testify in exchange for immunity from use or derivative use of the compelled testimony. The trial court held that the statute was unconstitutional as it violated the self-incrimination right of the co-defendant. Finding no error in the lower courts' judgments, the court affirmed. The scope of the protection against self-incrimination under Alaska Const. art I, § 9 was that: (1) an individual could not be compelled to give testimony unless the State had taken measures to remove the hazard of incrimination; and (2) an individual faced a hazard of incrimination whenever the answers elicited could supported a conviction or could furnish a link in the chain of evidence leading to a conviction. Where the hazard of incrimination had been removed, the privilege against self-incrimination was no longer required. The court held that a grant of use and derivative use immunity did not remove the hazard of incrimination. The State could not meaningfully safeguard against nonevidentiary use of the compelled testimony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

95 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Walter J. Hickel v. Southeast Conference


No. 3911, Supreme Court Nos. S-5093/5154, Supreme Court File No. S-5156


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


846 P.2d 38; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 136



December 29, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing Denied March 12, 1993, Reported at 1993 Alas. LEXIS 23.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU 91-01608 Civil



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED and REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the State of Alaska and the governor, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which invalidated the governor's legislative redistricting plan as a violation of the Alaska Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Respondent voters brought an action against the State of Alaska and the governor challenging the governor's legislative redistricting plan. The superior court found the plan unconstitutional. On review, the court noted that the governor had the power and duty to reapportion the state legislature every 10 years pursuant to Alaska Const. art. VI, § 3. However, a redistricting plan required contiguity, compactness, and relative socio-economic integration. The court found that the plan lacked socio-economic integration of the state's communities. The state constitution also required districts to be comprised of relatively integrated areas pursuant to Alaska Const. art. VI, § 6. The court found that the redistricting board had taken a "hard look" at the redistricting issue through the use of expert advice and the consideration of alternatives. The court also found that the redistricting board was subject to the Open Meetings Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 44.62.310-.312, and the Public Records Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 09.25.110-.140.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment of the superior court that the redistricting plan was unconstitutional. The court remanded the case to the superior court with directions to devise an interim redistricting plan.

Anchorage v. Anchorage Police Dep't Employees Ass'n


No. 3893, Supreme Court Nos. S-4044, S-4151


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


839 P.2d 1080; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 116



October 23, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Victor D. Carlson, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-89-10124 CIVIL



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED. VACATED. REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for declaratory relief seeking to have the binding arbitration provision of the Anchorage, Alaska Municipal Code § 3.70.110 declared unconstitutional, appellant municipality sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska that granted summary judgment for appellee associations and awarded attorney's fees.


OVERVIEW: Municipality filed a complaint for declaratory relief seeking to have the binding arbitration provision of the Anchorage, Alaska Municipal Code § 3.70.110 declared unconstitutional. At the time the complaint was filed, associations were in the process of negotiating a new collective bargaining agreement. Municipality argued that the compulsory binding interest arbitration provisions constituted an impermissible delegation of the legislative authority to a politically unaccountable private third party and the provisions in question were unconstitutional as they provided no meaningful standards to guide the arbitrator and did not provide for meaningful judicial review. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of associations and awarded 100 percent of requested attorney's fees to one association and 70 percent of the attorney's fees to the other. Municipality challenged the results, and the court affirmed the judgment while vacating the award of fees. The questioned delegation of the legislative power to an arbitrator was not unconstitutional per se. The arbitrator's authority was limited. The disparate attorney's fees award was an abuse of discretion unless fully explained.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment was affirmed. The award of attorneys' fees to associations was vacated and remanded to the lower court to enter appropriate findings of fact and/or explanations.

Alaska Fish Spotters Ass'n v. Department of Fish & Game


No. 3884, Supreme Court File No. S-4540


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


838 P.2d 798; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 107



September 11, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Superior Court File No. 1JU-90-821 Civil



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fish spotters challenged an order from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which granted the summary judgment motion of appellee State and which upheld Alaska Admin. Code tit. 5, § 06.378 (1991), a regulation prohibiting the use of aircraft to locate salmon during open commercial salmon fishing periods.


OVERVIEW: Alaska Board of Fisheries promulgated the regulation, and the fish spotters filed an action alleging that the regulation was unconstitutional and beyond the Board's regulatory authority. The trial court entered summary judgment for the State, and on appeal, the court affirmed. The Board had authority to ban fish spotting if the ban was properly based on conservation, development, or allocation goals. The regulation did not violate the common use clause of the Alaska Constitution because it constituted a permissible limitation of the type traditionally imposed by the state on the means and methods which citizens may employ as they utilize fishery resources. The regulation did not violate the no exclusive rights clause of the Alaska Constitution because the regulation did not shelter some fishers against competition from others. The regulation did not implicate the equal protection or uniform application clauses of the Alaska Constitution because it applied equally to all citizens.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's entry of summary judgment in favor of the State.

 

98 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Sonneman v. Hickel


No. 3876, Supreme Court No. S-4372


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


836 P.2d 936; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 100



August 14, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Larry R. Weeks, Judge. Trial Court No. 1JU-90-800 Civil


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which entered a judgment in favor of appellees, various state officials, upholding the validity of the act that created the Alaska Marine Highway System Fund, Alaska Stat. § 19.65.050-100. Appellant claimed that the Fund was dedicated to a special purpose in violation of Alaska Const. art. IX, § 7.


OVERVIEW: Alaska Const. art. IX, § 7 prohibited the dedication of any tax or license revenues for any special purpose. The issue presented was whether the act that created the Alaska Marine Highway System Fund, Alaska Stat. § 19.65.050-100, violated that provision. The trial court held that it did not because it permitted, but did not require, the legislature to appropriate money from the Fund to the Marine Highway System. On appellate review, the court reversed in part, holding that Alaska Stat. § 19.65.080(b) was unconstitutional, but that it was severable from the remainder of the statute. The offending provision prohibited the Alaska Department of Transportation and Public Facilities from seeking appropriations from the Fund except in limited circumstances. The court held that this was merely an indirect way of restricting or earmarking the Fund for the exclusive use of the Marine Highway System. The unconstitutional provision was found to be severable, however, because the remaining provisions were complete and it was presumed that the legislature would have intended them to stand. Alaska Stat. § 01.10.030 provided a weak presumption of severability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment in part, but reversed in part, holding that the portion of the statute that prevented an agency from requesting appropriations from the Fund violated the Alaska Constitution, but that the remainder of the statute did not.

Coghill v. Coghill


No. 3865, Supreme Court No. S-4071


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


836 P.2d 921; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 85



July 10, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court No. 4FA-85-266. Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Niesje J. Steinkruger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks (Alaska), which ordered him to pay child support to appellee wife pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.3.


OVERVIEW: The husband and the wife were divorced and the decree of dissolution of marriage made no provision for child support. The wife brought a motion for child support pursuant to Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.3, which established guidelines to enable courts to determine child support awards and instituted a formula for child support awards based on the income of the noncustodial parent. The trial court ordered the husband to pay child support and the husband appealed, arguing that Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.3 was unconstitutional because it constituted a substantive change in the law. The court ruled that Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.3 did not modify or amend Alaska Stat. § 25.24.160, which allowed courts to set child support awards as could be just and proper for the parties to contribute toward the nurture and education of their children. Further, the court held that Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.3 interpreted Alaska Stat. § 25.24.160 and established guidelines to enable courts to determine what was a just and proper contribution. The court affirmed the judgment, concluding that Alaska R. Civ. P. 90.3 did not change the existing case law, and that child support awards still must be fair and equitable.


OUTCOME: The judgment awarding child support to the wife was affirmed.

State v. Morry


No. 3866, Supreme Court No. S-4632, 4660


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


836 P.2d 358; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 87



July 10, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing and Petition for Partial Rehearing Denied August 13, 1992. As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court No. 2BA-87-83 Civil. Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial District, Barrow, Michael I. Jeffery, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED and REVERSED and VACATED and REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and board of game, and appellees, hunter and citizen's council, both sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which invalidated subsistence hunting regulations, Alaska Admin. Code tit. 5, §§ 92.012(c) and 92.165, and an all Alaskans policy for first tier subsistence hunting eligibility. The hunter had filed an action for declaratory relief claiming that the regulations were invalid.


OVERVIEW: The board of game adopted regulations requiring subsistence hunters to purchase a tag prior to hunting for brown bears and to have parts of a killed animal sealed by a state official. These regulations were first adopted to regulate trophy hunting and made applicable to subsistence hunting without a public hearing. The board also adopted a policy that made all Alaskans eligible as first tier subsistence hunters. The trial court invalidated the regulations and the policy. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part, remanding the case with directions to remand it to the board. The court held that the regulations violated both the subsistence preference law, Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258(c) and the Administrative Procedures Act, Alaska Stat. § 16.05.255(a) because the board failed to conduct an analysis of the effect that the regulations had on subsistence uses and the board did not conduct a hearing or provide public notice before adopting the regulations. Since Alaska Stat. § 16.05.258 did not provide a basis for distinguishing between beneficial users at the first tier, the court held that the board's All Alaskan policy for first tier eligibility was valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that part of the order that invalidated the brown bear tag fee and bear sealing requirements as subsistence regulations and reversed that part of the order invalidating the board of game's All Alaskan first tier eligibility for subsistence hunting. The court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to remand it to the board for the purpose of adopting appropriate subsistence hunting regulations.

Cook Inlet Pipe Line Co. v. Alaska Pub. Utils. Comm'n

June 26, 1992, Decided

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pipeline company appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which upheld appellee Alaska Public Utilities Commission's methodology and tariff orders in appellant's challenge to the intrastate crude oil transportation tariffs ordered by appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Alaska Public Utilities Commission ordered appellant pipeline company to refund excess fare amounts collected from intrastate commerce in 1982. Appellant sought judicial review of the commission's order after appellee denied reconsideration of the order on several grounds. Among them, the court held that there was no error in the appellee's use of a hypothetical capital structure and that its rate of return determinations were adequately supported by facts and a reasonable basis in law. Nor did the tariff violate 49 U.S.C.S. § 13(4) because appellant failed to establish any evidence that supported its claim that interstate shippers suffered unfair prejudice The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment upholding appellee Alaska Public Utilities Commission's methodology and tariff order because among other things the commission's rate determinations were supported by a reasonable basis in law, and the tariff order was not preempted by federal law.

Sun v. State


No. 3830, Supreme Court No. S-4251


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


830 P.2d 772; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 40



April 10, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial District, Nome, Charles R. Tunley, Judge. Superior Court No. 2KB-89-79 Civil



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial District, Nome, which granted summary judgment to appellees, the State and two State troopers, in his action for personal injuries.


OVERVIEW: The injured party alleged that the two state troopers used excessive force by shooting him a number of times while attempting to apprehend him. On appeal, the injured party argued, inter alia, that (1) Alaska Stat. § 09.17.030 violated the due process clause of Alaska's Constitution; and (2) there were genuine issues of material fact that precluded summary judgment. Upon review, the court found the injured party's arguments without merit. A review of the record failed to disclose the existence of a genuine issue of material fact as to whether or not the injured party's assault of one of the troopers substantially contributed to his injuries. Nothing in the record showed a break in the nexus between the injured party's assault of the trooper and the troopers' instantaneous response thereto with deadly force. There was no indication that the injured party clearly and unequivocally broke off his assault prior to receiving his injuries. There was simply no genuine issue of material fact as to whether the injured party's criminal assault substantially contributed to the injuries he received.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the State and the two State troopers.

Anchorage v. Leigh


No. 3793, Supreme Court No. S-4076


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


823 P.2d 1241; 1992 Alas. LEXIS 1



January 3, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Victor D. Carlson, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-89-9484 Civil



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner municipality, as the employer, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which granted judgment for respondent employee, ruled that Alaska's Workers' Compensation Act's definition of "medical stability" violated substantive due process, and concluded that the termination of the employee's temporary total disability benefits pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 23.30.185 was improper.


OVERVIEW: An employee filed an action seeking judicial review of the Workers' Compensation Board's decision to apply the statutory definition of medical stability in finding that he was medically stable and denying his claim for temporary total disability benefits. The employee contended that the legislature violated substantive due process by changing the definition of medical stability from the AMA definition to its own definition. The court reversed the superior court's judgment for the employee and concluded that the employee's substantive due process attack on the definition of medical stability articulated by Alaska Stat. § 23.30.265(21) should be rejected. The court held that the employee failed to meet his heavy burden of demonstrating that no rational basis existed for the definition adopted by the legislature. The court ruled that the legislature adopted a clear definition as to when medical stability was achieved for purposes of the Workers' Compensation Act in its quest to lower costs and facilitate the resolution of disputes. The Legislature's adopted definition for medical stability was not arbitrary and bore a reasonable relationship to a legitimate governmental policy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment for the employee and ruled that Alaska's Workers' Compensation Act's definition of "medical stability" did not violate the employee's substantive due process as it was used to terminate his temporary total disability benefits.

Lagos v. Sitka


No. 3791, Supreme Court No. S-4136


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


823 P.2d 641; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 148



December 27, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Sitka, Rodger W. Pegues, Judge. Superior Court No. 1SI-89-394 Civil



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners of businesses that sold alcoholic beverages, challenged a summary judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Sitka, that was granted in favor of appellees, a city and borough. The superior court dismissed appellants' complaint for a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief, which requested invalidation of a ballot proposition that increased a sales tax on alcoholic beverages.


OVERVIEW: The superior court denied appellants' motion for summary judgment, which had claimed that appellees' sales tax was illegal under Alaska Stat. § 04.21.010(c). The court held that an ordinance that taxed the sales of alcoholic beverage at a four percent higher rate than the tax on the sale of other commodities violated § 04.21.010(c). The court determined that the text of § 04.21.010(c)(2) failed to clearly indicate if it prohibited the imposition of discriminatory rates of sales taxes on sales of alcoholic beverages. The court found that § 04.21.010(c)(3) and the legislative history regarding a 1985 amendment to § 04.21.010(c) indicated that the amendment prohibited the imposition of discriminatory sales taxes, whether in the form of sales tax rate differentials or a sales tax imposed solely on the sale of alcoholic beverages. Therefore, the court concluded that §§ 04.21.010(c)(2) and 04.21.010(c)(3), when read together, barred a municipality from taxing only the sale of alcoholic beverages and also required that if sales taxes were imposed on other commodities, then the rate of taxation on the sale of alcoholic beverages could not exceed the rate on the sales of other commodities.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's summary judgment.

Gates v. Tenakee Springs


No. 3781, Supreme Court File No. S-3624


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


822 P.2d 455; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 137



December 6, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Rehearing Denied January 10, 1992. Released for Publication January 19, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Thomas E. Schulz, Judge. Superior Court No. 1JU-87-375CI


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner (owner) sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant city in the owner's action that resulted from the city's decision to have moved a fence that the owner had put up in front of her property.


OVERVIEW: The owner challenged both the summary judgment and the trial court's award of costs and attorney's fees to the city. The judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part, vacated in part, and remanded for further proceedings. The court held that summary judgment under Alaska R. App. P. 602(a)(2) was inappropriate on the owner's claims for damages, her archaeological site claims, and her statutory claims because her complaint was not an administrative appeal. The court determined that there was a material issue of fact as to how much damage to the owner's property was caused by the method by which the city had decided to remove the owner's encroachment. Remand was appropriate for the determination of whether any of the owner's alleged damages to her property were the result of the negligent removal of the fence. The court also held that the owner's non-damage claims failed on the merits. The owner had provided no evidence that demonstrated a discriminatory intent on the part of the city, the owner received all the process that was due, and there was no evidence regarding the finding of an archaeological site. Finally, the court vacated the award of costs and attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of the city in the owner's action based upon the city's removal of a fence was affirmed in part, reversed in part, vacated in part, and remanded back to the trial court. The claim for damages to the owner's property was reversed and remanded and the attorney's fees and costs awarded to the city was vacated and remanded. The remainder of the judgment was affirmed.

DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED to the court of appeals to in turn remand the case to the district court with directions to vacate petitioners' convictions.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners appealed the denial of their application for post conviction relief pursuant to Alaska R. Crim. P. 35.1(a)(1) on the grounds that their convictions for violation of Alaska Stat. § 16.30.010(a), wanton waste of a big game animal, violated due process. The district court denied their claim for post-conviction relief and the Court of Appeals (Alaska) remanded the matter to the district court for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were convicted of violating Alaska Stat. § 16.30.010(a), wanton waste of a big game animal. They sought post conviction relief on the grounds that their convictions under §16.30.010(a) violated due process. The court held that Alaska Admin. OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court and remanded petitioners' cases to the to the district court with directions to vacate their convictions.

Fairbanks v. Fairbanks Convention & Visitors Bureau


No. 3760. Supreme Court No. S-3663


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


818 P.2d 1153; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 117



October 11, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] THE APPELLATE COURTS, 303 K STREET, ANCHORAGE, ALASKA 99501 As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks. Mary E. Greene, Judge. 4FA-89-1478 Civil


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, which declared a proposed initiative unconstitutional, granted appellee city visitors bureau's (bureau) motion for summary judgment, and issued a permanent injunction barring the initiative from appearing on the ballot. The bureau sought declaratory and injunctive relief against the initiative.


OVERVIEW: Fairbanks, Alaska, Fairbanks Gen. Code Ordinance § 5.401 (1979) provided up to 70 percent of the revenues generated by the bed tax to the bureau. A citizens' group proposed an initiative that removed almost all of the restrictions of the ordinance, which controlled the spending of the revenue. The superior court concluded that the proposed initiative repealed an existing appropriation and granted the bureau's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed the superior court's judgment and held that the initiative was constitutional because the initiative neither made an appropriation nor dedicated funds. Section 5.402 was not an appropriation under Alaska Sta. § 29.35.100 because it did not reflect an action taken by the city after an annual approval of the budget, nor was the ordinance a supplemental or emergency act of the city. The initiative was not an appropriation because it specified no sums to be distributed, no purpose to be funded, and no mandatory process. The initiative did not infringe on flexibility in the budget process or restrict the power of the city council in distributing the bed tax revenues.


OUTCOME: The court held that the initiative was constitutional, reversed the superior court's grant of the bureau's motion for summary judgment and permanent injunction, and remanded the case with directions to enter judgment on behalf of the city and to dismiss the bureau's complaint.

O'Leary v. Superior Court, Third Judicial Dist.


No. 3736, Supreme Court Nos. S-4105, S-4131, S-4132, S-4133, S-4134, S-4159


Supreme Court of Alaska


816 P.2d 163; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 86



August 9, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge.

Trial Court Nos. 3AN-90-5881 Civil, 3AN-90-832 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police chief sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which issued an order ruling that the applications of all parties to hold Alaska R. Crim. P. 6.1 unconstitutional were denied. These appeals concerned an investigative grand jury report which was subject to judicial review under Alaska R. Crim. P. 6.1.


OVERVIEW: The issues raised in these cases were whether Alaska R. Crim. P. 6.1 violated Alaska Const. art. I, § 8, and whether the superior court erred in ordering an entire grand jury report released. The court had previously ordered that only a certain section of the report be immediately released. On appeal, the court held that the requirement of rule 6.1 that recommendations be based on substantial evidence elicited in the course of the grand jury's investigation was based both on due process considerations and on the connection between the investigation and the recommendation made in article I, § 8 of the Alaska constitution. The court held that rule 6.1 was constitutional. The court also held that much of the remainder of the grand jury report should have been released. However, the court held that the grand jury's discussion of allegations against certain teachers added little to the comprehensibility of the recommendations. The court held that publication of the discussion would harm the reputations of the teachers. The court, therefore, ordered expunged from the grand jury report all mention of the relevant investigation.


OUTCOME: The court ordered released part II of the relevant grand jury report, with certain deletions. The court also ordered that the final written responses of those interested parties who were still mentioned in the expunged report and who had preserved their right to file such responses be attached as an appendix. The court held that the state rule providing a procedure for judicial review of grand jury reports before release was constitutional.

Stepanov v. Homer Elec. Ass'n


No. 3714, Supreme Court File Nos. S-3410, S-3496, S-3497


Supreme Court of Alaska


814 P.2d 731; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 59



July 5, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Roy H. Madsen, Judge. Superior Court Case Nos. 3AN-87-2643 Civil Consolidated with 3HO-87-132 Civil.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part, affirmed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developers and appellees, an electric company, the state, and a public utility commission, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which held that the parties' contract was partially enforceable.


OVERVIEW: The developers owned real property that they wanted sub-divided. They entered into a contract with the electric company which provided that the company would not charge for overhead line extensions, but that the developers would pay for underground lines. The company petitioned the commission to change its line extension policy to include a tariff. A hearing was held by the commission, which stated that the contracts were special contracts, which were not valid until approved by the commission. The superior court affirmed the commission's decision but on different grounds. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court determined that under the contracts, the developers were insulated from the tariff changes and should have been compensated for harm suffered because they reasonably relied on the company's knowledge to comply with the commission's regulations. The court held that the commission did not have the authority to award attorney's fees in the absence of specific authorization and that it was an abuse of discretion for the superior court to have awarded actual fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court that held that the contracts were partially enforceable. The court affirmed the superior court's denial of attorney's fees for administrative proceedings and reversed its award of actual fees to the developers for the appellate proceedings.

McGrath v. University of Alaska


No. 3708, Supreme Court No. S-3418


Supreme Court of Alaska


813 P.2d 1370; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 55



June 21, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge. Superior Court No. 3AN-S88-08936 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant community college professors challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted appellee university's motion for summary judgment. The trial court held that Alaska's Administrative Procedure Act (APA), Alaska Stat. §§ 44.62.330-.650, did not apply to grievance proceedings.


OVERVIEW: When the university adopted a state-wide system, which eliminated the separate administration of the community college, the professors were combined with the university faculty. The professors had no rank or tenure provisions in a prior collective bargaining agreement. Based on years, none of the professors were offered a full professorship. A grievance was filed, but the APA did not govern the new interim procedures. The professors initiated an action, contending that the APA procedures were required and that the contemplated grievance procedures denied them due process. The court reversed the judgment, finding that Alaska Stat. § 44.62.330 et seq. did apply to the university and that its grievance proceedings had to comply with the provisions of the Act. The court found no inconsistency's between the university president's power and the Act. The exclusion of other state personnel from the APA did not conclusively demonstrate that university personnel were similarly excluded. However, because the formal grievance complaint filed by the professors did not explicitly distinguish between legislative facts and administrative facts, remand was necessary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment of the trial court.
