Citizens Coalition for Tort Reform v. McAlpine


No. 3686, File No. S-3714


Supreme Court of Alaska


810 P.2d 162; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 29



April 26, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen Hunt, Judge. 3AN 87 11043 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tort reform coalition filed an application for certification of an initiative relating to, among other things, the regulation of attorney fees. Appellee lieutenant governor denied certification of the initiative. The Superior Court of Anchorage (Alaska) granted a motion for summary judgment in favor of the lieutenant governor and the coalition appealed.


OVERVIEW: The tort reform coalition filed an application for certification of an initiative. One of the items that the initiative purported to regulate was attorney fees. The lieutenant governor denied certification of the initiative. The coalition brought an action and the superior court granted a motion for summary judgment in favor of the lieutenant governor. On review, the court affirmed the order granting summary judgment. The court found that it had the authority to regulate the courts and the practice of law under Alaska Const. art IV § 1. Further, the court found that it had repeatedly exercised its authority to regulate the practice of law, including attorney fees. The court reasoned further that Alaska Const. art. XI, § 7 expressly forbid an initiative to prescribe a rule of court and that under Alaska Const. art. IV, § 15 such rules could only be changed by two-thirds of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted summary judgment in favor of the lieutenant governor on the issue of whether the lieutenant governor properly denied certification of an initiative proposed the tort reform coalition.

 

113 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Anthony


No. 3685, Supreme Court Nos. S-3650, S-3765


Supreme Court of Alaska


810 P.2d 155; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 31



April 26, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Granted September 13, 1991. Reported at 1991 Alas. LEXIS 107.
On rehearing at State v. Anthony, 816 P.2d 1377, 1991 Alas. LEXIS 107 (Alaska, 1991)
Related proceeding at Hertz v. State, 2010 Alas. LEXIS 101 (Alaska, Sept. 22, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Palmer/Anchorage, Beverly W. Cutler and Karen L. Hunt, Judges. 3AN-89-3807 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Palmer/Anchorage, which entered a judgment in favor of appellee inmates in the inmates' action to challenge the constitutionality of Alaska Stat. § 43.23.005(d). The superior court held the statute violative of "equal protection."


OVERVIEW: The inmates alleged that § 43.23.005(d) violated the equal protection clause, the due process clause, and the prohibitions against ex post facto laws, bills of attainder, and cruel and unusual punishment in the Alaska and United States Constitutions. The inmates prevailed on only the equal protection challenge. The sole issue before the court was whether the superior court erred in holding that § 43.23.005(d) violated either of the equal protection clauses. Section 43.23.005(d) provided that persons convicted of a felony, who were incarcerated during any period of a fiscal year as a result of the conviction, were ineligible for a permanent fund dividend for that year. The court found that an individual's interest in a permanent fund dividend, like other economic interests, was entitled to minimum scrutiny. Therefore, the State had to show only that its purposes were legitimate and that the challenged statute bore a fair and substantial relationship to the purposes. Because § 43.23.005(d) had a fair and substantial relationship to the legitimate goal of compensating crime victims, the statute did not violate the equal protection clause of either the Alaska or U.S. Constitution.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

114 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Kenai Peninsula Borough v. Tyonek Native Corp.


No. 3672, Supreme Court No. S-2253


Supreme Court of Alaska


807 P.2d 502; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 17



March 15, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, James Hanson, Judge. Trial Court No. 3KN-86-485 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant local borough challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, which reversed a local tax assessor's determination that property owned by appellee native corporation was subject to property taxation under 43 U.S.C.S. § 1620(d) and Alaska Stat. § 29.45.030. The borough's motion to set aside the decision was denied.


OVERVIEW: The corporation's property was exempt from local taxation pursuant to 43 U.S.C.S. § 1620(d) and Alaska Stat. § 29.45.030 as long as it remained undeveloped or became undeveloped after use. The corporation had previously leased the property to a third party; because the lessee had improved and developed it, the property had been taxed during the tenant's occupancy. The corporation successfully claimed that, after the lease expired, the property was unused and therefore had reverted to an undeveloped state. The trial court declared that the property was exempt so long as it remained unoccupied and idle. In reversing, the court held that the parcel had been so extensively improved that it was necessarily "developed" within the meaning of 43 U.S.C.S. § 1620(d). Because it was developed property, the mere fact that it was not currently leased did not render it "undeveloped" or make it exempt from taxes under Alaska Stat. § 29.45.030. Some additional, tangible change to the property had to occur, such as the destruction or decay of the improvements, for the property to revert to an exempt, undeveloped state. The property remained taxable because no such deterioration had occurred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause for further proceedings.

 

115 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Kenai Peninsula Borough v. Cook Inlet Region


No. 3671, Supreme Court No. S-3117


Supreme Court of Alaska


807 P.2d 487; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 20



March 15, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Ralph Stemp, Judge. Trial Court Nos. 3KN-86-486/3KN-86-841 CI.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, borough and borough assessor, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which reversed the borough assessor's ruling and which ruled that the properties of appellees, village and corporation, were exempt and awarded actual attorney's fees and sanctions against appellants in appellees' actions for taxes paid by them under protest.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the village's appeal as to certain tax years were time barred, but found that appellees' appeals as to other tax years were timely filed. As to the exempt status of the properties at issue, the court held that "developed" as used in the Alaska Native Claims Settlement Act (ANCSA), 43 U.S.C.S. §§ 1601-1628, included parcels which were not only legally but practically suitable for sale and that Alaska Stat. § 29.45.030 was consistent with ANCSA with respect to the meaning of developed. The definition was broad enough to include appellees' subdivided land which was ready for sale. The court remanded the case for a determination of the status of certain parcels under the appropriate legal standard. The court affirmed the awards of sanctions, but held that under the doctrine of plain error the awards constituted an impermissible double recovery of attorney's fees if both the awards of sanctions and the awards of attorney's fees were allowed to stand. Therefore, the court vacated the awards of attorney's fees on the basis that the trial court's finding of bad faith was based upon an incorrect interpretation of the law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment as to the timeliness of the village's appeals for certain tax years. The court affirmed the rulings with respect to the unsubdivided parcels at issue and reversed as to the subdivided parcels. The court affirmed the awards of sanctions and vacated the awards of attorney's fees and costs. The court remanded the case for further proceedings as to the status of certain parcels.

 

116 of 606 DOCUMENTS



Diedrich v. Ketchikan


No. 3661, Supreme Court Nos. S-3568, S-3659


Supreme Court of Alaska


805 P.2d 362; 1991 Alas. LEXIS 3



January 25, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, Thomas E. Schulz, Judge.

Superior Court No. 1KE-88-642 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city employee challenged a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which granted summary judgment to appellee city, and dismissed the employee's claims that alleged a breach of a covenant of good faith and fair dealing implied in his employment contract, and that violated his rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. On cross-appeal, the city challenged the denial of its request for attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: The city employee was terminated and reinstated after filing an appeal to the city personnel board (board). After his reinstatement, his position was eliminated, and his termination was upheld on appeal to the board. Fifteen months later, the employee brought his civil action. On appeal, the court affirmed the dismissal of his action. However denominated, any claim which was functionally an administrative appeal had to be brought within the 30 day limit imposed by Alaska R. App. P. 602(a)(2). All of the employee's claims, except those under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 were functionally appeals from the board's decision, and therefore they were barred by the passage of time. Requiring public employees to bring a timely administrative appeal as a prerequisite to invoking the appellate jurisdiction of the superior court did not create a denial of equal protection. Because the employee's § 1983 claims were essentially the same as those he presented to the board, then issue preclusion prevented re-litigation in state court. Although the city was not entitled to attorney fees under Alaska R. Civ. P. 82, the court remanded for consideration of fees under Alaska R. App. P. 508(e).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the city, except that the order that denied the city's request for attorney fees was remanded for further proceedings.

Gilbert v. Department of Fish & Game, Bd. of Fisheries


No. 3649, File No. S-2963


Supreme Court of Alaska


803 P.2d 391; 1990 Alas. LEXIS 125



December 7, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge. No. 3AN 85 5724 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff fishermen challenged the defendant Alaska Board of Fisheries' regulation imposing a limit on the total number of salmon in a fishery. The Superior Court Third Judicial District (Alaska) granted summary judgment in favor of the board. The fishermen appealed.


OVERVIEW: The fishermen argued that the trial court erred in concluding, as a matter of law, that the public noticed requirement in Alaska. Stat. § 44.62.200(a) was satisfied. The court determined that the notice was sufficient to make the fishermen aware that the board was considering modifying the salmon allocations. The fisherman contended that the regulation was adopted to conform to an invalid board policy. The court determined that since the board's policy fell within the definition of a regulation under § 44.62.640(a)(3), it was invalid for its failure to comply with the Administrative Procedures Act (APA). The court determined that the regulation was reasonable; therefore, the invalidation of the policy did not affect the validity of the regulation. Furthermore, the court determined that the regulation did not violate the uniform application clause under Alaska Const. Art. VIII, § 17.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the board, but reversed the portion of the trial court's judgment that concluded that the mixed stock fishery policy was not a regulation under the APA.

State v. Herbert


No. 3651, Supreme Court No. S-3357


Supreme Court of Alaska


803 P.2d 863; 1990 Alas. LEXIS 126



December 7, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on appeal from the District Court of the State of Alaska, Second Judicial District, Nome, Bradley N. Gater, Magistrate. Court of Appeals Nos. A-2470, A-2480; Trial Court No. 3NO-S86-69 CR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant herring sac roe fishermen were charged with violating Alaska Admin. Code tit. 5, § 27.987 by fishing in a superexclusive use fishery after having fished in another area. They challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Alaska) denying the fishermen's federal constitutional challenges and state equal rights challenged to the regulation. The court of appeals certified the Alaska Const. art. VIII, §§ 3, 15, and 17 challenges.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the court of appeals' decision which held that the Board of Fisheries (Alaska) had authority to promulgate superexclusive use regulations, that the regulation did not discriminate on the basis of race in violation of Alaska Const. art. I, § 1, and that the regulations violated neither the privileges and immunities clause nor the commerce clause of the federal constitution. With respect to the certified constitutional issues, the court observed that there was no discrimination between residents and nonresidents and that the limitations as to "time and area" and "gear size" were not violative of the Alaska Constitution as only persons, not nets or boats, were entitled to equal rights. Accordingly, the court concluded that the regulation did not violate any Alaska Const. art. VIII clauses. The court further observed that as such regulatory devices were in use when the Alaska Constitution was promulgated, it was impossible to conclude that the framers intended to prohibit this method of regulation upon drafting art. VIII. Further, the regulation, if anything, furthered the interests underlying the common use mandate of art. VIII, § 3.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.

Code tit. 20, § 05.220(a) was a proper implementation of Alaska Stat. § 16.43.110(a), which delegated regulatory authority to the CFEC, and (3) the fisherman could seek a refund of the excess payments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, in part, and reversed, in part, the trial court's order granting partial summary judgment to the state and the CFEC. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings.

Valdez v. Department of Community & Regional Affairs


No. 3601, Supreme Court No. S-3135


Supreme Court of Alaska


793 P.2d 532; 1990 Alas. LEXIS 67; 109 Oil & Gas Rep. 219



June 1, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Valdez, John Bosshard, III, Judge. Trial Court File No. 3VA-87--90 Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought relief from a decision by the Superior Court, Third Judicial District (Alaska) in favor of appellee state agency in the action brought by the state agency asserting that the city's imposition of a higher property tax mill rate on oil and gas property than on any other property in the city to pay the cost of providing oil and spill prevention and response services violated Alaska Stat. § 43.56.010(b).


OVERVIEW: The city asserted that its disparate tax on the oil and gas property was justified, under Alaska Stat. § 29.45.080, by the higher level of city services provided to the property by the city. On appeal, the court affirmed. It found that the city had the authority to levy a property tax on all real and personal property within the city. The city could levy a tax on oil and gas property, but only at the rate of taxation that applied to other property taxed by the city. Since the language of the statutes relied upon by the parties could not be reconciled, the more specific statute that prohibited the city from taxing oil and gas properties at a higher rate than other city properties governed. Also, as well intentioned as the city might be in its attempt to provide services tailored to the needs of the oil and gas property, the potential for a spillover of benefits to those that did not own oil and gas property was great. In fact, the costs of the public dock for the residents of the city were being paid for by the oil and gas industry and the other residents of the State of Alaska. The court affirmed the decision in favor of the state agency.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision in favor of the state agency.

Farmer v. State


Nos. 3567, File Nos. S-2890, 3072


Supreme Court of Alaska


788 P.2d 43; 1990 Alas. LEXIS 30



March 2, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, 4FA 87 1265 Civil, Richard D. Savell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff log harvester appealed from the summary judgment entered by the Superior Court, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks (Alaska) for defendants, State of Alaska and two state troopers, on the ground that his claim for alleged constitutional violations was time-barred. The harvester also challenged the dismissal of his complaint with prejudice under Alaska R. Civ. P. 19 for failure to join a timber company as an indispensable party.


OVERVIEW: The harvester claimed the troopers violated his rights to be free from unreasonable government seizures and to enjoy the rewards of his own industry when they confiscated logs to which both the harvester and a timber company claimed title. The trial court ruled that the relation back doctrine did not apply to the trooper whose identity was unknown when the harvester filed suit and that this trooper did not receive imputed notice under the facts of the case. The trial court also ordered joinder of the timber company as an indispensable party because an adjudication of the central issue of log ownership was required in the case. The court held that the unidentified trooper had imputed notice via the attorney general, which represented all defendants from the outset, and adopted the identity of interest theory as to service upon state law enforcement agencies. The court also held that the order directing joinder of the timber company was an abuse of discretion. The state could not assert any threat to the timber company's interests when it disavowed any interest in the case, and it was unnecessary to join the timber company to determine if it had a property interest in the logs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment for defendants in the harvester's action for constitutional violations because the claim against the state trooper whose identity was unknown at the time that the action commenced was not time-barred. The court also vacated the order that dismissed the complaint with prejudice for the harvester's failure to join the timber company as an indispensable party and remanded the case for further findings.

McDowell v. State


No. 3540, Supreme Court File No. S-2732


Supreme Court of Alaska


785 P.2d 1; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 163



December 22, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Douglas J. Serdahely, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alaska, Third Judicial District that upheld the constitutionality of 1986 Alaska Sess. Laws 56 (1986 Act), which granted a preference to rural residents to take fish and game for subsistence purposes.


OVERVIEW: The 1986 Act's only requirement to be met by a subsistence fisherman or hunter was residency in a rural area of the state. The rural preference was challenged under several provisions of the Alaska Constitution: the common use clause, the no exclusive right of fishery clause, the uniform application clause, the equal rights clause, and the due process clause (Alaska Const. art. VIII, §§ 3, 15, 17, respectively). In addition, violation of the equal protection and due process clauses of the United States Constitution was claimed. The court held that the rural preference violated Alaska Const. art. VIII, §§ 3, 15, 17. The court found that, although the ramifications of these clauses were varied, they shared the meaning that the exclusive or special privileges to take fish and wildlife were prohibited. The court noted that it did not hold that everyone could engage in subsistence hunting or fishing. The court articulated that only the residency criterion used in the 1986 Act, which conclusively excluded all urban residents from subsistence hunting and fishing regardless of their individual characteristics, was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded finding that the residency criterion used in the 1986 Act was unconstitutional.

State by Departments of Transp. & Labor v. Enserch Alaska Constr.


Nos. 3539, File Nos. S-2693, S-2694, S-2731, S-2736


Supreme Court of Alaska


787 P.2d 624; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 162



December 18, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, 3AN-87-11329 Civil, Douglas J. Serdahely, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the state of Alaska, a regional corporation, and a municipality, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which granted partial summary judgment for appellee public works contractor and held that Alaska Stat. § 36.10.160, a statute creating a hiring preference for eligible residents of an economically distressed zone, was unconstitutional. The contractor cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: A public works contractor filed suit against the state seeking a declaration that § 36.10.160 was unconstitutional and damages for its increased costs in complying with the law. The superior court permitted intervention on both sides, and both sides filed motions for summary judgment. The superior court entered partial summary judgment for the contractor after concluding that the preference law violated the equal protection provision of the Alaska Constitution and that the state was not entitled to summary judgment on the issue whether the contractor waived its right to seek damages for the state's enforcement of the law. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the superior court that § 36.10.160 violated the Alaska Constitution and stated that the disparate treatment of unemployed workers in one region in order to confer an economic benefit on similarly situated workers in another region was not a legitimate legislative goal. The court reversed the superior court's determination without deciding the waiver issue, because the contractor did not have the right to seek damages for the enforcement of an unconstitutional law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, in part, and reversed, in part. The court affirmed the superior court's decision declaring that the regional preference statute and the implementing regulations and orders contravened the equal protection provision of the state constitution. The court reversed the superior court's decision concerning damages and stated that the contractor had no right to seek damages for the state's enforcement of the unconstitutional law.

Kenai Peninsula Borough v. English Bay Village Corp.


No. 3517, Supreme Court File No. S-2033


Supreme Court of Alaska


781 P.2d 6; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 138



October 13, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Trial Court File No. 3KN-84-563 Civil, Charles K. Cranston, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough challenged an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which denied its motion under Alaska R. Civ. P. 60(b) to set aside for mistake, neglect, or fraud, a default judgment entered against it in appellee corporation's action. The default judgment included a preliminary injunction, which enjoined the borough from pursuing foreclosure proceedings against the corporation for nonpayment of real property taxes.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied the borough's motion because it found no grounds of mistake, neglect, or fraud and the borough had waived its jurisdictional objection by its voluntary appearance. On appeal, the borough contended that the trial court erred in declining to set aside the default judgment. The court disagreed and affirmed. The court held that the trial court obtained jurisdiction over the borough under Alaska Stat. § 09.05.010, even though service was defective, when it filed a motion without challenging jurisdiction. The court further held that the trial court did not plainly usurp its power by entering the default judgment. Application of the plain usurpation standard was limited to cases that involved an arrogation of authority that a court clearly lacked. While taking a default judgment without notice was a serious error, it did not render the judgment void. Because the default judgment was taken 30 days after the borough filed opposition to the motion for default judgment, it was not void. Also, where the borough filed opposition to the corporation's application for default judgment, there was a presumed valid application under Alaska R. Civ. P. 55.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the borough's motion to set aside the default judgment entered against it and in favor of the corporation.

 

126 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Principal Mut. Life Ins. Co. v. Division of Ins., Dep't of Commerce & Economic Dev.


No. 3509, File Nos. S-2517, S-2518


Supreme Court of Alaska


780 P.2d 1023; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 133



September 29, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, 1JU 87 585 Civil, Juneau, Duane Craske, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foreign insurer sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the denial by appellee, the State of Alaska Division of Insurance (state), of a refund of premium taxes paid by the insurer, under Alaska Stat. § 21.09.210 (repealed 1986). The state sought review of the finding that the statute was unconstitutional under the equal protection clauses of the federal and state constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The legislature imposed, under § 21.09.210, a tax on foreign insurers on premiums for the insurance of property or risks within the state. After the statute was repealed, the insurer requested a refund, which was denied by the state. The insurer appealed to the trial court the state's denial of refund. The court affirmed the trial court's finding of unconstitutionality but reversed the denial of refund and remanded. The court held that: 1) § 21.09.210 failed to meet the federal rational basis test and the state's stricter test because the tax was a discrimination lacking any legitimate state purpose; 2) the state failed to present any evidence to support its claim that the tax advanced any of its stated purposes; and 3) because the insurer did not make a formal protest, it was entitled to a refund only if the state waived the protest requirement of Alaska Stat. § 43.15.010; thus remand was required to determine if a waiver occurred and if the insurer's claim was made timely. The court overruled State v. Wakefield Fisheries, Inc., 495 P.2d 166 (Alaska 1972), to the extent that it held that a formal protest was not required to maintain an action in assumpsit for a tax refund.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's ruling that the differential premium tax was unconstitutional. The court reversed the trial court's denial of a refund of the premium tax and remanded for the trial court to determine whether the state waived the protest requirement, and, if waived, whether the insurer was entitled to a refund under the applicable limitations period.

In re Adoption of B.S.L.


No. 3501, Supreme Court No. S-2431


Supreme Court of Alaska


779 P.2d 1222; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 120



September 15, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, Superior Court Nos. 1KE-85-125 P/A, 1KE-86-649 DR, Thomas E. Jahnke, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In adoption proceedings initiated by appellees, who were prospective adoptive parents of a minor child, the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Ketchikan, found appellant natural mother's consent was not required for the adoption pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 25.23.050(a) and entered the decree of adoption. The entry of the decree terminated the natural mother's parental rights. The natural mother filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The prospective adoptive parents initiated adoption proceedings for the purpose of adopting the minor child. The natural mother filed an objection. The trial court found that the natural mother had for three years failed significantly without justifiable cause to communicate meaningfully with the minor child. Accordingly, the trial court concluded that the natural mother's consent to the adoption was not required, Alaska Stat. § 25.23.050(a). The trial court entered the decree of adoption and terminated the natural mother's parental rights. On review, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that the trial court committed no error in finding that the natural mother's failure to communicate with the minor child was without justifiable cause. The court so held, despite its recognition of efforts by the natural father and the natural father's family to discourage communication between the natural mother and the minor child. Furthermore, the court concluded that the provisions for termination of parental rights pursuant to an adoption proceeding withstood the natural mother's constitutional challenge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

129 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Barber v. Anchorage


No. 3452, Supreme Court No. S-2252


Supreme Court of Alaska


776 P.2d 1035; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 70



June 30, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which issued an oral decision and subsequently entered a final judgment in favor of appellee municipality in the applicant's action to enjoin enforcement of the municipality's zoning ordinance prohibiting the use of temporary signs.


OVERVIEW: The municipality enacted amendments to an ordinance, which prohibited portable signs. The applicant placed a portable sign on his property and the municipality cited the applicant. The applicant sued the municipality seeking to enjoin enforcement of the ordinance, alleging that the sign ordinance violated his constitutional rights of free expression, due process, and equal protection. The trial court found in favor of the municipality. The applicant appealed. The court held that the sign ordinance was adequately justified by substantial aesthetic concerns on the part of the municipality, and found that the ordinance was not overly restrictive. The court held that the incidental restriction on commercial speech contained in the ordinance was narrowly tailored to achieve the municipality's aesthetic goal of eliminating visual blight. The court held that the ordinance did not violate the First Amendment. The court also found that the applicant did not demonstrate that his right to equal protection under either the United States or Alaska Constitutions had been violated. The court affirmed the decision in favor of the municipality, but reversed the award of partial attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court which entered final judgment in favor of the municipality in the applicant's action to enjoin the enforcement of the municipality's zoning ordinance prohibiting the use of temporary signs. The court reversed the part of the decision awarding attorney's fees to the municipality.

 

130 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Tachick Freight Lines v. Department of Labor, Employment Sec. Div.


No. 3439, File No. S-2739


Supreme Court of Alaska


773 P.2d 451; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 47



May 12, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, James C. Hornaday, Judge, Pro Tem, No. 3AN-87-2336-Civil.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which affirmed a decision of the Commissioner of Labor of Alaska finding that the employer was liable for unemployment insurance premiums under the Alaska Employment Security Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 23.20.005 - 23.20.535 because its workers were employees rather than independent contractors.


OVERVIEW: To avoid liability employees' unemployment insurance premiums, officers of the employer resolved that all persons working for it would be independent contractors rather than employees. An investigation and hearing resulted, and the Commissioner held the employer liable for premiums. On judicial review, the trial court affirmed, and on appeal, the court affirmed. The hearing was not procedurally inadequate due to inadequate notice of the scope of the hearing, and the hearing officer did not lack impartiality. The Commissioner did not abuse its discretion in finding that the employer's workers were employees rather than independent contractors, where the employer did not produce clear evidence that all three prongs of Alaska Stat. § 23.20.525(a)(10) were met as to any of the workers. The court noted that the fact that several employees had business licenses was not sufficient to make them independent contractors. The Commissioner's decision was not an unconstitutional impairment of the employer's "freedom to contract" and did not result in a denial of equal protection to the employer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Standard Alaska Prod. Co. v. Department of Revenue


No. 3425, File No. S-2445


Supreme Court of Alaska


773 P.2d 201; 1989 Alas. LEXIS 27



April 21, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporate taxpayer (taxpayer) sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which found in favor of appellee Alaska Department of Revenue (department) in the taxpayer's action alleging that the department lacked authority to increase disputed tax assessments after three years had passed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed a complaint in the superior court seeking a declaration that the department lacked the authority to increase disputed tax assessments after three years had passed from the date the tax return was filed. The superior court dismissed the declaratory judgment action on the ground that the taxpayer would first have to exhaust available administrative grievance procedures before seeking a judicial remedy. The taxpayer appealed and the court affirmed, holding that the taxpayer did indeed fail to exhaust available administrative remedies before seeking judicial review of the department's actions. Further, it appeared highly possible that the department would find the 1985 assessment valid under Alaska Stat. § 43.05.260. On the record before the court, a decision adverse to the taxpayer's interest did not appear to be a certainty. The court therefore held that the "futility" exception to the doctrine of administrative exhaustion did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court in favor of the department in the taxpayer's action seeking a declaration that the department lacked the authority to increase disputed tax assessments after three years had passed from the date the tax return was filed.

Patrick v. Lynden Transp.


No. 3401, File No. S-2294


Supreme Court of Alaska


765 P.2d 1375; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 154



December 23, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] On Review from the Superior Court for the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, James R. Blair, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nonresident individual sought review of an order by the Superior Court for the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which required the individual to post a bond for anticipated costs and attorney fees as a condition a maintaining a suit against respondent corporation in an Alaska court.


OVERVIEW: The individual filed an action against the corporation seeking damages for an alleged breach of a lease. The corporation filed a motion to require the individual to post a cost bond, asserting that the individual was not a resident of the state. The trial court ordered a stay of the proceedings until the individual posted a bond to cover the corporation's anticipated costs and attorney fees. On petition for review, the court held that the bond, as required by Alaska Stat. § 09.60.060, violated the individual's equal protection rights. The court determined that the individual's right to access to a court was an important right that was deserving of close scrutiny. The corporation claimed that the purpose of the statute was to provide security for attorney fees that might be awarded against the individual, from whom it might be difficult to collect because of his non-residence. The court stated that the means of effectuating this purpose were not sufficiently well tailored to its ends. The court found that the statute was overinclusive because it assumed that all non-residents would be uncooperative in paying cost and attorney fee awards entered against them.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court.

 

133 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Owsichek v. Guide Licensing & Control Bd.


No. 3389, File No. S-1650


Supreme Court of Alaska


763 P.2d 488; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 146



October 21, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Milton Souter, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hunting guide challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which upheld the decision of the State of Alaska Guide Licensing and Control Board denying him permission to utilize certain lands for his hunting business.


OVERVIEW: The state established a scheme of exclusive guide areas and joint use areas wherein a guide would be able to register his camp and be entitled to exclusive guiding privileges in a designated area surrounding it. Joint use areas would be assigned where the areas used by two or more guides overlapped. A hunting guide was denied the use of certain lands because they overlapped with another guide. The guide had spent considerable money on cabins and challenged the constitutionality of the statute. The court held that the exclusive guide areas and joint use areas fell within the category of grants prohibited by the common use clause. These areas allowed one guide to exclude all other guides from leading hunts professionally in his area. To grant such a special privilege based primarily on seniority ran counter to the notion of common use. The judgment was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the State of Alaska Guide Licensing and Control Board was reversed and remanded.

Ben Lomond, Inc. v. Anchorage


No. S-2184


Supreme Court of Alaska


761 P.2d 119; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 133



September 16, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied October 12, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant building owner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which denied the owner's request for compensation from appellee city for allegedly unconstitutionally revoking its building permits.


OVERVIEW: The owner claimed that the city unconstitutionally revoked its permits and, therefore, was liable in damages. The trial court denied the owner compensation reasoning that the city was immune from an action for damages. The owner appealed. The court found that the trial court improperly reached the merits in this case because the owner failed to exhaust its administrative remedies. The court found that when the city revoked the owner's permits, the owner could have promptly appealed the revocation to the Zoning Board of Examiners and Appeals. The court held that by failing to take even the first step in the administrative appeal process, the owner deprived the city of the opportunity to make a factual record and correct its own errors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

McAlpine v. University of Alaska


No. 3382, File No. S-2674


Supreme Court of Alaska


762 P.2d 81; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 135



September 9, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Thomas M. Jahnke, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Lieutenant Governor of the State of Alaska, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee University of Alaska (University) in its action that alleged that the certification of an initiative application was improper.


OVERVIEW: A coalition submitted an initiative application that proposed to establish an independent community college system and provided for the transfer of property from the University to the new system. The Lt. Governor approved the application. Overruling prior case law, the court held that the University's action should have been barred by the statute of limitation in Alaska Stat. § 15.45.240. However, the suit was not barred because conditions did not favor retroactivity of its ruling. The inclusion of the third sentence caused the initiative to make an appropriation by designating the use of state assets in a manner that was executable, mandatory, and reasonably definite with no further legislative action. The court severed the impermissible portion because it found that, when standing alone, the remainder enacted law; deleting the impermissible portion did not substantially change the spirit of the measure; and that it was evident from the content of the measure and the circumstances surrounding its proposal that the sponsors and subscribers would prefer the measure to stand as altered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the superior court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the University. It directed the Lt. Governor to sever the portion of the initiative that made an appropriation and ordered him to place the remainder on the ballot.

R.C. v. Department of Health & Social Servs.


Nos. 3373, Nos. S-2363, S-2386, S-2410


Supreme Court of Alaska


760 P.2d 501; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 125



August 12, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Jay Hodges, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, parents, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District that terminated their parental rights to the children and gave custody to appellee, the Department of Health and Social Services.


OVERVIEW: The Department was granted 30 days temporary custody of the children based on evidence of several-years history of drinking, violence, and basic neglect of the children by the parents. Subsequently, the Department petitioned the court for termination of parental rights under Alaska Stat. § 47.10.080(c)(3), asserting that the parental conduct that had caused the children to be adjudicated children in need of aid was likely to continue if parental rights were not terminated. The trial court granted the Department's petition and terminated the parents' rights. The parents appealed. The court found, based on the parties' stipulation, that there was clear and convincing evidence that the children were in need of aid under § 47.10.080(c)(3) and Alaska Stat. § 47.10.010(a)(2)(A) as a result of the conduct of the parents. The court held that a party could not challenge on appeal an order that he had agreed to in the trial court. Therefore, the stipulation was valid at the time of the second hearing, and it was not erroneous for the trial court to rely on it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Arkanakyak v. Commercial Fisheries Entry Comm'n


No. 3363, File No. S-1061


Supreme Court of Alaska


759 P.2d 513; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 119



July 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karen L. Hunt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which sustained a decision of appellee state Commercial Fisheries Entry Commission (CFEC).


OVERVIEW: In 1977, applicant applied for a Bristol Bay salmon set gill net entry permit. The CFEC classified her application at four verified points under its priority classification system and issued her a non-transferable entry permit, which cannot be sold, inherited, or even given away. She objected, claiming among other things that her 1971 and 1972 participation in the Bristol Bay fishery as a crew member entitled her to two additional points and therefore a freely transferable entry permit. Accordingly, it remanded the action to the CFEC for explicit determination as to whether the factual circumstances relating to the application for crewing points warrant granting an exception to the licensure requirement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the superior court and remanded the action for further consideration to the CFEC.

Johns v. Commercial Fisheries Entry Comm'n


No. 3356, File No. S-2057


Supreme Court of Alaska


758 P.2d 1256; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 102



July 1, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Henry C. Keene, Jr. Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fishermen challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which granted the motion for summary judgment of appellees, Commercial Fisheries Entry Commission (CFEC) and the state, on the fishermen's claim for a permit to a limited entry purse seine fishery. The fishermen claimed that the CFEC erred under the Limited Entry Act, Alaska Stat. § 16.43.010 through .990.


OVERVIEW: The fishermen sought a preliminary injunction and claimed that the CFEC made errors in establishing the maximum number of entry permits for the roe herring purse seine fishery and that the CFEC erred in failing to set an optimum number for the fishery. The superior court granted summary judgment to the CFEC and the state. The court remanded the case with instructions to order the CFEC to begin the optimum number process. The court affirmed the superior court's judgment in all other respects. The CFEC erred in establishing a maximum number of permits that was lower than the highest number of units of gear fished in the four years prior to the limitation date because the CFEC was obligated to select the maximum number by reference to past participation at the fishery. Because the CFEC actually issued more permits through hardship exceptions than it should have set as a maximum, the error did not mandate reversal. The CFEC's action in setting a limited number of permits for resource conservation reasons was expressly authorized by Alaska Const. art. VIII, § 15. The CFEC had a duty not to delay in initiating the optimum number process for the fishery.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the superior court with instructions to order the CFEC to begin the optimum number process. The court affirmed the superior court's judgment in all other respects.

Rice v. Rice


Nos. 3358, File Nos. S-2249, 2275


Supreme Court of Alaska


757 P.2d 60; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 109



July 1, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, James R. Blair, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a divorce action, appellant husband sought review of the decree of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska that treated the husband's retirement fund as a marital asset. Appellee wife sought review of the trial court's valuation of a fox fur farm.


OVERVIEW: Before and throughout the parties' marriage, the husband was enrolled in the Public Employees' Retirement System (PERS). The trial court treated the retirement fund as a marital asset, divided it in half as of the date of the divorce, and entered a Qualified Domestic Relations Order (QDRO) to pay one-half of the benefits to the wife when the husband either resigned, was terminated, or died. The husband contended that dividing his PERS account by use of a QDRO unconstitutionally diminished his retirement benefits. In affirming the decree, the court held that the use of the QDRO did not increase his liability to the wife or affect his other substantive rights; thus, it was proper for the trial court to have applied retroactively an amendment to Alaska Stat. § 39.35.500, which permitted the assignment of retirement benefits pursuant to a QDRO. The court also held that the trial court's finding that the fur farm had negligible value due to debt was not clearly erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree.

 

140 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Vest v. Schafer


No. 3346, File No. S-2091


Supreme Court of Alaska


757 P.2d 588; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 90



June 17, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Walter L. Carpeneti, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which granted summary judgment on the issue of damages in favor of defendants, state and state official, in the citizen's action to have the state's Longevity Bonus Program declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In a prior appeal in the citizen's class action, the court determined that Alaska's Longevity Bonus Program violated equal protection. On remand, the trial court granted injunctive and declarative relief but denied damages. On appeal, the citizen argued that damages were available either under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, directly under the United States Constitution, or under the state constitution. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that: (1) damages against the state were not available under § 1983 because a state was not a "person" subject to liability within the meaning of § 1983; (2) damages were not available for the state's violation of the federal or state constitutions because in the absence of a statute allowing such damages, it was not appropriate for the judiciary to assess damages against a state on the ground that the legislature enacted a law later held unconstitutional; (3) damages were not available against the state official in her official capacity because in that capacity she was indistinguishable from the state; and (4) damages were not available against the state official personally because there was no wrongful individual action on her part.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to deny the citizen damages in his class action against defendants over the constitutionality of the state's Longevity Bonus Program.

Perito v. Perito

Supreme Court of Alaska


756 P.2d 895; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 81



May 27, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Peter A. Michalski, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted the parties a divorce upon appellee wife's petition. The trial court also awarded attorney's fees to the wife.


OVERVIEW: The wife filed for divorce in Alaska one day after she arrived in the state, to which the husband, a resident of New York, sought review. On appeal, the husband contended that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to grant the divorce, and that the trial court's award of attorney's fees was an abuse of discretion. The court held that the definition of "residency" in Alaska Stat. § 01.10.055 was irrelevant because there was no separate statute requiring that plaintiffs in divorce actions be residents. The court affirmed the trial court's finding that the intent to remain permanently was to be interpreted to mean the intent to remain indefinitely. The court held that there was no clear error in the trial court's finding that the wife had the requisite intent to remain in Alaska indefinitely or permanently. The court found no abuse of the trial court's discretion in its award of attorney's fees to the wife.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Homer Elec. Ass'n v. Alaska Pub. Utils. Comm'n


No. 3327, File No. S-1952


Supreme Court of Alaska


756 P.2d 874; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 77



May 20, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Charles K. Cranston, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant electric utility sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which affirmed appellee Alaska Public Utility Commission's order that excluded lobbying expenses from the electric utility's revenue requirements. The trial court also allocated 100 percent of the cost to the electric utility, which included costs of attorney's fees from the attorney general's office.


OVERVIEW: The electric utility filed a request for a rate increase with the Alaska Public Utilities Commission. The Commission concluded that 50 percent of the dues paid by the electric utility to a utility association were for lobbying, and excluded lobbying expenses from the electric utility's revenue requirement. The Commission allocated 100 percent of the cost to the electric utility and included attorney's fees of the attorney general as cost. The superior court affirmed the Commission on all substantive issues, but remanded the case for a determination of the specific proportion of the dues actually used for lobbing purposes. On appeal, the court held that: (1) under its general authority to establish just, fair, and reasonable rates, it was not unreasonable for the Commission to exclude lobbying expenses from the electric utility's revenue requirements; (2) under Alaska Stat. § 42.05.651(a), it was not proper to permit the Commission to recovery for routine services rendered by the attorney general's office; and (3) the Commission abused it discretion when it awarded 100 percent of the cost to the electric utility without an individualized consideration of the facts of the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the part of the trial court's judgment that excluded lobbying expenses from the electric utility's revenue requirements. The court reversed the part of the trial court's judgment that included in the cost allocation any attorney's fees performed by the attorney general's office, and remanded for further findings as to the propriety of a 100 percent allocation of expenses to the electric utility.

 

143 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Peck v. Alaska Aeronautical, Inc.


No. 3312, File No. S-1726


Supreme Court of Alaska


756 P.2d 282; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 79



May 6, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended May 15, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court for the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Peter A. Michalski, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of an order of the Superior Court for the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which affirmed a decision of appellee Alaska Worker's Compensation Board that held that the employee should be compensated by appellees, his employer and its insured, at the average weekly wage that he earned at the time of his injury as opposed to the larger weekly wage he was earning at the time of his total permanent disability.


OVERVIEW: The employee hurt his back in an airplane crash. The employee was temporarily disabled, and subsequently returned to work. The employee continued to work and years later was forced to retire for medical reasons. The employee filed an application for adjustment of claim and sought permanent total disability benefits. The Worker's Compensation Board held that the disability benefits must be computed based on his average weekly earnings at the time of his accident. The employee sought review. The trial court affirmed. The court reversed. The court held: (1) the limitation on earnings contained in Alaska Stat. § 23.30.175(b) (1962), was one that the legislature intended to be applied unless its application lead to a grossly unfair result; (2) the Board unfairly applied a formula to the employee, which did not fairly achieve the legislature's goal of awarding injured employees an amount reasonably representing their future earnings capacity; (3) it was grossly unfair to apply the maximum limitation under Alaska Stat. § 23.30.170(b); and (4) Alaska Stat. § 23.30.170(b) was not to be applied when it negated the legislative intent of Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220(3).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that affirmed the order of the Board, held that the employee was entitled to a compensation recovery that approximated his earnings at the time he was permanently disabled, not the time the accident initially occurred, and remanded the case to the trial court for a recalculation.

Fairbanks N. Star Borough v. State


No. 3319, File No. S-2254


Supreme Court of Alaska


753 P.2d 1158; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 71



May 6, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, James R. Blair, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which concluded that 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 was a valid curative act and modified its earlier judgment. In a previous appeal, the court found that appellee State of Alaska and Alaska's governor acted unconstitutionally in withholding the borough's funds by abrogating the legislature's responsibility over appropriations.


OVERVIEW: The state legislature passed 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 with the express intention of validating the governor's impoundment orders that had been rendered void by an earlier judgment of the court. The court found that 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 did not grant the governor sweeping power over the entire budget with no guidance or limitation, as did Alaska Stat. § 37.07.080(g)(2), which was held unconstitutional in the earlier judgment. The court rejected the borough's argument that 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 was unconstitutional for the same reasons that Alaska Stat. § 37.07.080(g)(2) was unconstitutional. The court found that 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 did not unconstitutionally impair any vested rights. The court rejected the borough's claimed violations of due process and equal protection. The court held that there was no bar to enforcement of 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 as a valid curative act. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment because 1987 Alaska Sess. Laws 9 was a valid and constitutional exercise of the legislative power, which effectively cured any constitutional infirmities in the governor's earlier actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

145 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Alaska Gold Co. v. Department of Revenue


No. 3305, File No. S-1892


Supreme Court of Alaska


754 P.2d 247; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 50



April 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Valdez, John Bosshard, III, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which affirmed a ruling of appellee state revenue department that the parent corporation, a non-Alaska subsidiary of the parent corporation, and the corporation were "unitary corporations" for income tax purposes.


OVERVIEW: A corporation was a subsidiary of a non-Alaska parent corporation. The state revenue department found that the parent corporation and a subsidiary of the parent corporation were "unitary corporations" under the Multistate Tax Compact, Alaska Stat. § 43.19.010 et seq., and the Alaska Net Income Tax Act, Alaska Stat. § 43.20.010 et seq. The corporation was assessed additional taxes as a result. The corporation sought review in the trial court. The trial court affirmed. The corporation appealed. The court affirmed. The court held that (1) the parent's corporation was involved in the finances the subsidiary corporation; (2) the evidence demonstrated that as a result of the involvement, the parent corporation benefitted by the functional integration with the corporation, and there was overlap on the board of directors and with corporate officers; and (3) the parent corporation benefitted by the economies of scale.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which affirmed a ruling of the state revenue department and held that the corporation was a "unitary corporation" with its parent corporation and non-Alaska subsidiary for tax purposes.

 

146 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Truesdell v. Halliburton Co.


No. 3302, File No. S-2259


Supreme Court of Alaska


754 P.2d 236; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 53



April 15, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Questions Certified from the United States District Court for the District of Alaska, James M. Fitzgerald, Chief Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Alaska certified two questions to the court: Should the rule of law recognizing a minor child's independent cause of action for loss of parental consortium resulting from injuries tortiously inflicted on her parent by a third person, be applied retroactively? If so, should the minor's tolling statute, Alaska Stat. § 09.10.140, be applied to such claims?


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee sought to add his minor children as plaintiffs in a count for loss of parental consortium to his action to recover damages for injuries suffered at work. Defendant employer argued that the rule of law recognizing loss of parental consortium should not be applied retroactively and that, if it were, limitations barred the action. The court answered both questions in the affirmative. The court held that the new rule of law applied retroactively because special circumstances did not warrant a departure from the general rule of retroactivity, but the court limited its retroactive application due to concerns about the effects on the administration of justice and the fairness of requiring defendants to reopen files involving 20-year-old accidents. The court rejected in advance the argument that it was not feasible to join minors' claims with their parents' claims because the cause of action was not yet recognized. The court held that the language and logic of Alaska Stat. § 09.10.140, read together with the general tort limitations statute, Alaska Stat. § 09.10.070, demanded that limitations be tolled until a child reached the age of majority.


OUTCOME: The court held that the new rule of law recognizing an independent cause of action for loss of parental consortium was to apply retroactively. The court held that minor children's actions for loss of parental consortium grounded in accidents occurring before the rule was announced were barred if one or both parents brought an action based on the accident and the action was concluded by settlement or judgment, unless joinder was not feasible.

 

147 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Creekpaum


Nos. 3300, File No. S-2054, File No. S-2208


Supreme Court of Alaska


753 P.2d 1139; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 55



April 15, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Petitions for Hearing from the Court of Appeals, on Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Walter Carpeneti, Judge, and Third Judicial District, Kenai, Charles K. Cranston, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal in two factually similar criminal cases, the State sought review of decisions of the Court of Appeals (Alaska), which held that Alaska Stat. § 12.10.020(c) was an unconstitutional ex post facto law insofar as it extended the statute of limitations for prosecutions under Alaska Stat. §§ 11.41.410-11.41.460.


OVERVIEW: In two separate cases, defendants were indicted for sexual abuse of a minor, in violation of the former Alaska Stat. § 11.41.410(a). At the time of the offenses, the applicable statute of limitations was five years pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 12.10.010. The legislature subsequently enacted Alaska Stat. § 12.10.020(c), which enlarged the limitations period. The legislature expressly provided for § 12.10.020(c) to have a retroactive effect. Defendants sought to dismiss their indictments on grounds that they were untimely under Alaska Stat. § 12.10.010 and that Alaska Stat. § 12.10.020(c) was an unconstitutional ex post facto law. The trial court and the court of appeals agreed. On appeal, the court held that the extension of a criminal statute of limitations before the original period of limitation had run was not an unconstitutional ex post facto law under the state or federal constitutions.The court reasoned that the extension of the statute of limitations was merely a procedural change that did not increase the punishment for the offense or alter the elements of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the court of appeals.

 

148 of 606 DOCUMENTS


McBirney & Assocs. v. State


No. 3298, File No. S-1896


Supreme Court of Alaska


753 P.2d 1132; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 57



April 15, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Mary E. Greene, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sole bidder challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee state in an action the bidder brought to enforce a contract to lease office buildings to the state. The trial court held that the contract was properly voided because the bidder and the state governor's staff circumvented the competitive bidding process.


OVERVIEW: The sole bidder on a contract to lease office buildings to a state government challenged summary judgment in favor of the state in an action to enforce the contract. The state rescinded the contract after the state attorney general advised that the contract was void because of irregularities in the manner by which it was obtained. The undisputed facts established that the bidder and the state governor's staff circumvented the competitive bidding process by highly improper means. For instance, the bidder received a draft Request for Proposal that was never released to the public, and changes negotiated between the bidder and the governor's staff made the bidder the only potential bidder who met the requirements. The bidder argued that the contract was ultimately awarded on a "sole source" basis. The appellate court affirmed summary judgment in favor of the state because the use of private negotiations to award government contracts invited favoritism, extravagance, fraud, and corruption.The appellate court held that in order to promote honest competition on an equal basis, the state could not be allowed to use the sole-source process to circumvent competitive bidding requirements.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment order in favor of the state was affirmed because the award of a public contract to a bidder was improper where the bidder received an unfair competitive advantage over other bidders.

 

149 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Turner Constr. Co. v. Scales


No. 3290, File Nos. S-1429, A-1600


Supreme Court of Alaska


752 P.2d 467; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 21



April 1, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Petitions for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Douglas J. Serdahely, (S-1429) and Joan M. Katz (S-1600), Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In these consolidated cases, petitioner contractors sought review of decisions of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which held in favor of respondent property owners, in actions for damages related to the errors of design professionals.


OVERVIEW: The property owners contended that Alaska's six-year statute of repose for actions against design professionals, Alaska Stat. § 09.10.055, violated the due process and equal protection clauses of the Alaska Constitution. The lower court agreed with the property owners and the court affirmed. The court held that the property owners had standing because they were precluded from asserting their own rights against defendants who might otherwise be liable. The property owners claimed the statute violated equal protection because it failed to protect owners, tenants, and materialmen, while protecting others who were similarly situated, such as architects, planners, engineers, and construction contractors. The court agreed and noted that one effect of the statute of repose was to eliminate the statutory right of contribution among tortfeasors because a plaintiff could not recover against design professionals. The court concluded that there was no substantial relationship between exempting design professionals from liability, shifting liability for defective design and construction to owners and material suppliers, and the goal of encouraging construction.


OUTCOME: The court held for the property owners and against the contractors in the actions for damages arising from alleged errors of design professionals. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, holding that Alaska's six-year statute of repose violated the equal protection clause of the Alaska Constitution.

VECO Int'l v. Alaska Pub. Offices Comm'n


No. 3295, File No. S-1598


Supreme Court of Alaska


753 P.2d 703; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 42



April 1, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Roy H. Madsen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District of Anchorage, which upheld a decision by appellee Alaska Public Offices Commission that imposed a civil penalty on the corporation pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 15.13.125 of the Alaska Campaign Disclosure Act for failing to report contributions.


OVERVIEW: The corporation encouraged its employees to authorize that money be withheld from their paychecks for the purpose of contributing to a political committee that was partially controlled by the corporation's chairman. The commission imposed a civil penalty against the corporation pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 15.13.125, for failing to report the campaign contributions that were made under the employee withholding plan. The trial court affirmed the commissions' decision. Upon review, the court remanded. The court held the payroll contribution plan constituted "joint action" under Alaska Stat. 15.13.130(4) where the corporation actively participated in determining which candidates would receive the pool of funds from the employees. The court found the corporation was not exempt from filing the reports due to an erroneous statement made by an official who was acting on behalf of the commission where the mistake was subsequently corrected. The court concluded new penalties were improperly assessed against the corporation because Alaska Admin. Code tit. 2, § 50.390(g) prohibited the accrual of new penalties until 30 days after the corporation was notified that its appeal was denied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed part of an order that determined the corporation was required to report campaign contributions that were made pursuant to an employee payroll withholding plan. The court remanded with instructions to vacate the amount of penalties assessed against the corporation and for the case to be remanded to the commission for an reassessment of penalties in accordance with the court's opinion.

 

151 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Gulf Oil Corp. v. Department of Revenue


No. 3289, File No. S-1985


Supreme Court of Alaska


755 P.2d 372; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 43; 101 Oil & Gas Rep. 413



March 25, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, John Bosshard III, Judge. Opinion Published at 752 P.2d 983 was Re-reported at 755 P.2d 372.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the administrative decision of appellee state department of revenue (DOR) that the taxpayer failed to prove unfair taxation.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer, a large corporation with worldwide income, first argued that it should have been allowed to deduct the income taxes levied on oil produced in KINEVA countries. The court held that when a state sought to tax a multi-state or multi-national business, it must determine the amount of income that was properly allocable to in-state activities according to a three-factor apportionment formula in the Multistate Tax Compact, Alaska Stat. § 43.19.010 (1970). The court determined that the taxpayer had not established that no portion of the foreign levy was an income tax. Further, there was not clear and cogent evidence showing that the income attributed to the state was out of all appropriate proportions to the business transacted in the state. In Alaska, foreign income taxes were neither deductible, under Alaska Stat. § 43.20.031(c), nor creditable, under Alaska Stat. § 43.20.036(a). The taxpayer also argued that its non-producing oil leaseholds located in the state should be valued at zero for the purpose of computing the fraction of the taxpayer's income allocable to the state. However, the court held that there was no proof of due process or commerce clause violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that the DOR's failure to grant relief to the taxpayer was not unconstitutional. The DOR had the authority and discretion to determine the amount of state income, absent clear and cogent evidence that the income was generated by business transacted out of the state.

Foster v. State


No. S-1846, No. S-1885


Supreme Court of Alaska


752 P.2d 459; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 39

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimants challenged the order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, that entered judgment in favor of appellee state in a dispute between the claimants and the state as to which party held title to certain real property located in a gold-mining district.


OVERVIEW: A dispute occurred between the claimants and the state as to the title owner of several parcels of real property located in a gold-mining district. The state alleged that it had title due to a prior foreclosure pursuant to the Land Registration Law, Alaska Stat. §§ 34.10.010-.240 (1978), which was designed, in part, to return abandoned land to that state. The first claimant asserted that he had title because he was the assign of the record owner at the time of foreclosure, and that the state wrongfully refused to allow him to repurchase the properties. The second claimant asserted title on the basis of adverse possession. At trial, the superior court entered judgment for the state. The court affirmed, holding that the superior court correctly affirmed the decision of the Department of Natural Resources that denied the first claimant's applications to repurchase the land because the claimant failed to clearly establish who the record owner was at the time of disclosure. The court also upheld the superior court's finding that the second claimant had no standing to challenge the state's prior foreclosure action, and that such challenged was barred by the doctrine of laches.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order, which held that title to certain real property located in a gold-mining district resided with the state in a dispute between the state and the claimants as to title to the property.
