Keyes v. Humana Hosp. Alaska


No. 3272, File No. S-1848


Supreme Court of Alaska


750 P.2d 343; 1988 Alas. LEXIS 27



February 19, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Petition for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the Third Judicial District (Alaska), which denied her motion for a protective order. The injured sought the protective order requesting that the trial court refuse to allow her case to be presented to an expert advisory panel due to alleged constitutional defects.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the injured claimed that the provision in Alaska Stat. § 09.55.536, which permitted submitting her case to an expert advisory panel, impaired her right to a jury trial because introduction of the panel's report into evidence would undercut the jury's role as the trier of fact. However, the court held that admissibility of a panel report did not impinge upon the right to a jury trial because the evidence only established a rebuttable presumption, and therefore, did not cut off any defenses, interposed no obstacle to a full contestation of all of the issues, and took no question of fact from either the court or the jury. The injured further argued that Alaska Stat. § 09.55.536, denied her right of due process. However, the court held that substantively, the statute had a reasonable relationship to the legitimate governmental purpose of alleviating the effects of the malpractice insurance crisis, and procedurally, she was given a full opportunity to litigate her position before a jury. The injured further argued that Alaska Stat. § 09.55.536, denied her right to equal protection. However, the court held that the statute bore a fair and substantial relation to its purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying the injured's motion for a protective order.

The court also held that the individual could not have maintained a negligence action against the DOR because the DOR owed no duty of care to the individual with regard to its efforts to collect on the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's grant of summary judgment to the DOR.

Peck v. Alaska Aeronautical


File No. S-1726, No. 3240


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


744 P.2d 663; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 336



October 30, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court for the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Peter A. Michalski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant disability benefit applicant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage (Alaska), which affirmed the decision and order from the Workers' Compensation Board where it was held that the applicant was to be compensated at the average weekly wage he earned at the time of his injury, rather than the significantly greater wage he earned at the time of his permanent total disability.


OVERVIEW: The applicant challenged the trial court decision as it affirmed the Board's calculation of the applicant's permanent total disability benefits. The applicant argued that the Board erred in calculating his permanent total disability benefits under the statute in effect at the time of his injury and asserted that the statute in effect at the time of his disability should have been used to calculate his benefits. The court determined that the Board's calculation of the applicant's benefits was grossly unfair because of the great disparity between his average weekly wage at the time of his injury and the time of his disability. The Board improperly refused to apply Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220(3), which required the Board to address the fairness and undue hardship posed by the calculation procedures set forth in Alaska Stat. § 23.30.220(2). Since the calculation of the applicant's benefits under § 23.30.220(2) caused an undue hardship on the applicant, the court directed the Board to recalculate the amount of benefits the applicant was entitled to receive by applying his average weekly wage at the time of his disability.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment and remanded for recalculation of the applicant's permanent total disability benefits.

Kenai Peninsula Borough v. State


Nos. 3231, File Nos. S-1207, S-1216


Supreme Court of Alaska


743 P.2d 1352; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 311



October 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian Shortell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, borough and voters, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which found that a state legislative reapportionment plan promulgated by appellee state was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The reapportionment plan had the effect of creating a new state house district and a new state senate district. On appeal, the court partially affirmed and partially reversed the decision below. The realignment effectuated consistent and rational state policies by retaining a certain district in order to promote social and economic integration. Therefore, it did not violate the Equal Protection Clauses of either the Federal or Alaska Constitution. The creation of the new house district satisfied the socio-economic integration requirement of Alaska Const. art. VI, § 6 because the communities were relatively close geographically and were connected by a common hub. However, the new senate district violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Alaska Constitution, which imposed a stricter standard than its federal counterpart when no fundamental right was a stake, because the district was deliberately fashioned to dilute the voting power of Anchorage voters. Had truly proportionate representation been employed, Anchorage would have received an additional district. Therefore, that portion of the judgment was subject to reversal.


OUTCOME: The decision upholding the realignment and the creation of the new house district was affirmed, but the decision upholding the creation of the new senate district was reversed.

Denardo v. State

Supreme Court of Alaska


741 P.2d 1197; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 300

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prospective candidate sought review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which dismissed the candidate's action claiming that appellee Alaska Division of Elections improperly refused to place his name on the ballot for the 1986 gubernatorial election.


OVERVIEW: The Division refused to place the candidate's name on the ballot because the candidate failed to comply with a rule which required independent gubernatorial candidates to submit nominating petitions signed by qualified voters equal in number to at least one percent of those voting in the previous general election. The trial court sustained the division's action. The candidate sought review, contending that the regulation was invalid. On review, the court noted that caselaw cited by the candidate was inapplicable. The court held that the specific regulation actually adopted by the Division was valid. The court also found that the regulation was reasonable and not arbitrary. The court noted that the division of elections acted within the scope of the authority delegated to it by the legislature when it enacted the rule and the candidate failed to comply with its provisions and the division properly rejected his petition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

159 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Patterson


No. 3202, File No. S-1316


Supreme Court of Alaska


740 P.2d 944; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 276



July 31, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals, on Appeal from the Superior Court, the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, J. Justin Ripley, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the State of Alaska, was granted a petition for hearing to address a ruling of the Court of Appeals (Alaska), which had reversed defendant's conviction for first-degree robbery on the ground that the jury instructions were improper. The court of appeals had held that the statutory definition of the insanity defense in Alaska Stat. § 12.47.010(a) encompassed both prongs of the traditional M'Naghten test.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first-degree robbery. The jury found her guilty but mentally ill under Alaska Stat. § 12.47.030(a). The court of appeals reversed the conviction on the ground that one of the jury instructions merged the insanity defense with the element of mens rea required for a robbery conviction, making it impossible to determine whether the jury actually found that defendant acted with the requisite mens rea. The court of appeals also ruled that Alaska Stat. § 12.47.010(a) encompassed both prongs of the traditional M'Naghten insanity test. The court reversed the court of appeal's ruling on the insanity test and remanded for a new trial. The court held that when the legislature enacted § 12.47.010(a), it intended to incorporate only the first prong of the M'Naghten test, which allowed defendant to be found not guilty by reason of insanity if she did not understand the basic nature and quality of her conduct. If the legislature had intended § 12.47.010(a) to encompass the second prong, a defendant's capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his or her conduct, it would have had little reason to enact Alaska Stat. § 12.47.030(a) defining "guilty but mentally ill."


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the court of appeals with directions to remand to the superior court for a new trial on the charges against defendant for first-degree robbery.

De Nardo v. State


No. 3201, File No. S-1260


Supreme Court of Alaska


740 P.2d 453; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 277; 127 L.R.R.M. 2651



July 31, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian Shortell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which dismissed his complaint against the state, that challenged his termination, as res judicata. The employee argued that the decision of the Alaska Public Employees Association (APEA) to terminate his employment was void and unconstitutionally impaired his employment contract.


OVERVIEW: The state fired the employee at the request of the APEA pursuant to a collective bargaining agreement because he failed to pay his shop dues. The employee filed an unfair labor practices charge, and the state Labor Relations Agency determined that the termination was for just cause. The employee filed a lawsuit against the state and the APEA alleging taking and conversion in violation of his 14th Amendment property interest in employment. The trial court granted summary judgment and dismissed the matter with prejudice because the employee failed to appeal the agency decision. A second action in federal court was dismissed as res judicata. In a third action, the trial court again dismissed the matter as res judicata. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that: 1) the prior actions involved the same parties and arose for the same transaction; 2) the evidence and facts were identical to those in the prior actions; 3) the fact that the issues were reframed as constitutional violations was not a basis for permitting relitigation of the first judgment; and 4) no exceptions to the doctrine of res judicata applied because the agency's judgment was not void or subject to collateral attack.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed the employee's challenge to his employment termination as res judicata.

Vienna v. Scott Wetzel Servs.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, J. Justin Ripley, Judge (S-1289/S-1290), and the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Walter L. Carpeneti, Judge (S-932).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Consolidated appeal from the First and Third Judicial Districts of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska that considered the extent of retroactive application to petitioners, injured workers, who were non parties in previous litigation that held Alaska Stat. § 23.30.175(d) unconstitutional, and who sought damages for compensation underpayments.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, as individuals and as members of a class action, sought damages for compensation underpayments, from respondents, insurers, and adjusters. Petitioners suffered work-related injuries in Alaska, filed claims, were paid temporary total disability compensation, and resided outside of Alaska. Petitioners received a lower out-of-state compensation rate. The court had previously determined Alaska Stat. § 23.30.175 (d) to be unconstitutional and awarded damages prospectively. Petitioners argued that damages should be awarded beginning with the period that the superior court declared Alaska Stat. § 23.30.175 (d) unconstitutional. The court applied the Byayuk test, accepted the position of the petitioners, and limited the holding to those workers whose claims remained open to adjudication and to those who have preserved the issue for appeal. Further, as the adjusters were not undisclosed agents and had not committed any tortious act against the petitioners, the court reversed the denial of the adjusters' motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: Awarded damages retroactively from the superior court's previous determination that a reduced payment for workers' compensation to workers was unconstitutional because the court sought to balance the compensatory interests of the petitioners with the reliance interests of the insurers.

De Lisio v. Alaska Superior Court

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sought review of an order of appellee Superior Court of the State of Alaska (superior court), which committed the attorney to jail for contempt of court for refusing to undertake his appointment by the superior court as counsel to represent an indigent defendant charged with sexual abuse of a minor.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was appointed by superior court to represent an indigent charged with sexual abuse of a minor, but the attorney refused the appointment. At a nonjury hearing, the trial court ordered the representation by a specified date or risk jail for contempt until such time as he undertook the representation. The attorney sought a reconsideration of the order. On reconsideration, the contempt was reaffirmed. The attorney appealed, arguing, inter alia, that requiring an attorney to represent an indigent defendant without reasonable compensation was a taking of private property for a public use under the Fifth Amendment and Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and Alaska Const. art. The court, overruling its prior decisions, held that a court appointment compelling an attorney to represent an indigent criminal defendant was a taking of property for which just compensation was required. The court ruled that the attorney's service was undeniably property within the meaning of the Takings Clause and that the appropriation of that property was a taking.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which committed the attorney to jail for contempt.

State v. Fairbanks N. Star Borough


No. 3180, File Nos. S-2122, 2141


Supreme Court of Alaska


736 P.2d 1140; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 259



May 6, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, James Blair, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, State and officials, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fairbanks, which denied appellants' motion for summary judgment in an action brought by appellee borough to declare Alaska Stat. § 37.07.080(g)(2), permitting the governor to withhold appropriations if there were insufficient receipts and surpluses, unconstitutional as an improper delegation of the legislative function.


OVERVIEW: The governor submitted a proposed budget based on a revenue department forecast. The forecast was subsequently reduced to reflect sharply reduced oil prices. To avoid deficient spending, which was prohibited by Alaska Const. art. IX, § 8, the governor withheld expenditure authority for certain appropriations, including a 10 percent restriction on funding for municipalities. The borough brought suit, contending that Alaska Stat. § 37.07.080(g)(2), permitting the governor to withhold appropriations if there were insufficient receipts and surpluses, was unconstitutional because it improperly delegated a legislative function and it lacked standards to guide the exercise of administrative discretion. The trial court held that § 37.07.080(g)(2) was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power because it authorized the exercise of sweeping power over the entire budget with no guidance or limitation and the governor's limited exercise of authority could not save the statute. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision as to the issue of constitutionality. However, the court held, contrary to the trial court, that the decision should be applied retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which held that the statute authorizing the governor to withhold appropriations was unconstitutional, but remanded the case with instructions to modify the judgment to allow for total retroactivity.

 

164 of 606 DOCUMENTS


American Nat'l Bank & Trust Co. v. International Seafoods


No. 3171, File No. S-1405


Supreme Court of Alaska


735 P.2d 747; 1987 Alas. LEXIS 248



April 17, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Review from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, J. Justin Ripley, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, in which the court denied defendant's motion to dismiss for lack of personal jurisdiction under plaintiff's complaint to recover money owed under a letter of credit.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff brought suit to recover money owed under a letter of credit executed between defendant and its customer in favor of plaintiff. Defendant moved to dismiss plaintiff's complaint for lack of personal jurisdiction. The lower court denied defendant's motion. Defendant subsequently petitioned the supreme court for review. The supreme court affirmed. The supreme court found that defendant had satisfied the minimum contacts requirement by purposefully availing itself of the privileges of conducting activities in the state of Alaska. According to the court, the minimum contact requirement was satisfied because defendant solicited plaintiff's performance by giving plaintiff assurance of payment, the offer was accepted in Alaska and performance was rendered in Alaska. The court continued that the exercise of personal jurisdiction comported with the notions of fair play and substantial justice because negations occurred within the state of Alaska, and Alaska had an interest in resolving the dispute.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the lower court's judgment because defendant satisfied the minimum contacts requirement where defendant solicited plaintiff's performance by giving plaintiff assurance of payment under the letter of credit, the offer was accepted in Alaska, and performance was rendered in Alaska.

 

165 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Rosson v. Boyd


No. 3017


Supreme Court of Alaska


727 P.2d 765; 1986 Alas. LEXIS 419



 October 31, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Opinion of January 31, 1986, Reported at 713 P.2d 800.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractor filed a petition for rehearing of the judgment that awarded the contractor a partial judgment of attorney's fees on appeal from a judgment entered in the contractor's favor in the contractor's action to foreclose a mechanic's lien.


OVERVIEW: The contractor and appellee homeowner entered into an agreement whereby the contractor was to build the homeowner's home. The homeowner failed to pay the contractor and the contractor filed an action to foreclose a mechanic's lien. The superior court awarded the contractor attorney's fees of $ 20,328. The homeowner appealed the award of attorney's fees, and the court remanded the case to the superior court because it was unclear if the superior court had considered Alaska Stat. § 34.35.005(b), the statute covering attorney's fees in lien actions, when making its award. The court also awarded the contractor $ 750 in attorney's fees for the cost of the appeal. The contractor filed a petition for a rehearing arguing that the attorney's fee award for the appeal was not reasonable. The court granted the petition for rehearing and found that the legislative purpose of § 34.35.005(b) was better served if the statute was construed to include attorney's fees on appeal. The court concluded that it depended on the circumstances of the case as to which court should award the attorney's fees for appellate proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court granted the contractor's petition for rehearing.

 

166 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Wickwire v. State


No. 3116


Supreme Court of Alaska


725 P.2d 695; 1986 Alas. LEXIS 390



September 19, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, James R. Blair, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former assistant attorney sought review of a summary judgment in favor of appellee state former employer in a wrongful termination suit pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 in the Superior Court of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks.


OVERVIEW: The former assistant attorney sought review of summary judgment in favor of the state former employer in a wrongful termination suit claiming he was fired for speaking out on a matter of public concern. The court affirmed the summary judgment and reversed only with regard to the award of attorney's fees against the former assistant attorney. The court found that the former assistant attorney failed to establish that the state former employer terminated him in violation of his right to free speech because the speech at issue was not a matter of public concern, but instead was an essentially personal employment dispute. The court found that the dispute did not rise to the level of being a matter of public concern where the former assistant attorney's letter referring to "problems" with a supervisor did not specify details or seek to bring to light actual or potential wrongdoing or breach of public trust. The court concluded that the lower court properly found that the conflict between the supervisor and the former assistant attorney was about internal management. The court found that the former assistant spoke not as a citizen, but as an employee on matters of personal interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in the former assistant attorney's § 1983 wrongful termination suit against the state former employer. The court reversed and remanded the award of attorney's fees against the former assistant attorney.

 

167 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Eluska


No. S-991


Supreme Court of Alaska


724 P.2d 514; 1986 Alas. LEXIS 377



August 29, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, Roy H. Madsen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from the decision of the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the charges against the defendant for violating Alaska Stat. § 16.05.255(b) by possessing game, freshly killed doe, in violation of the Board of Game's regulations.


OVERVIEW: The defendant was charged with violating § 16.05.255(b) when he was found in possession of freshly killed doe. The defendant claimed that the Board of Gaming failed to differentiate between subsistence hunting and other hunting, and the trial and appellate courts agreed, holding that § 16.05.255(b) required separate subsistence regulations. The trial court allowed defendant to plead a subsistence defense because the board did not adopt any subsistence regulations. The court reversed, although there was legislative history showing an intent for separate regulations, there was no evidence of an intent to grant any personal right to take or possess game in the absence of such regulations. The court held that defendant could not act as though he were unregulated, simply because the agency had failed to promulgate regulations. The court remanded, however, to the trial court for a determination of whether defendant's conduct constituted a "dire emergency" pursuant to Alaska Admin. Code tit. 5, § 81.90.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the charges against defendant for possessing game in violation of board regulations, and remanded for the trial court to determine whether defendant's actions constituted a "dire emergency."

 

168 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dunlop


Nos. S-923, S-1163


Supreme Court of Alaska


721 P.2d 604; 1986 Alas. LEXIS 342



June 13, 1986, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge (Case No. S-923), and on Appeal from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Charles K. Cranston, Judge (Case No. S-1163). Originally Reported at: 723 P.2d 70 and Republished at this Citation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: One defendant was convicted of two counts of manslaughter while the other defendant was convicted of three counts of third degree assault. The Court of Appeals (Alaska) allowed only one sentence to each defendant for the multiple counts. The state petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The state requested that the court modify or overrule its prior holding that separate punishments for multiple deaths resulting from a single criminal act were impermissible. The court granted the state's requested relief and held that the state's double jeopardy clause was not violated by multiple sentences for multiple violations of a single statute. The court found that the legislature had defined manslaughter and third degree assault to include responsibility for reckless behavior, and had sought to hold people criminally responsible for the results of their behavior that were within the scope of the risk created by that behavior. It was within the legislature's authority to define and punish the crimes because their actions were within constitutional limits. The court ruled that its prior holding improperly focused on the accused's intent instead of on the consequences. When more than one person was victimized during a single incident, the statutes' prohibition against causing the death or injury of another person was violated several times over. The court affirmed the consecutive sentences imposed by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the consecutive five-year presumptive terms imposed on the defendant convicted of manslaughter counts. The court affirmed the trial court's ruling that the defendant convicted of third degree assault counts should serve consecutive sentences, but remanded his case to the appellate court for consideration of other points.

Robison v. Francis


Nos. S-493, S-510, S-552


Supreme Court of Alaska


713 P.2d 259; 1986 Alas. LEXIS 288



January 17, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, State of Alaska, state officials, and union, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, in favor of appellee former employee in his action for a declaration that the local hire law, Alaska Stat. § 36.10.010, was unconstitutional under the United States Constitution and under the Alaska Constitution, seeking injunctive relief and damages under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: The employer discharged the nonresident employee after the state notified the employer that its work force had more than five percent non-residents, in violation of the local hire law, Alaska Stat. § 36.10.010. The employee filed an action against the state contending that § 36.10.010 was unconstitutional under the United States Constitution and the Alaska Constitution and sought injunctive relief and damages under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The union intervened. The trial court entered judgment for the employee because the state and the union failed to prove by a preponderance of the evidence that non-resident construction workers were a peculiar source of unemployment in the state construction industry and that there was a substantial reason to discriminate against employment of non-residents on state public works construction projects. The state and the union appealed. The court ruled that the findings of the trial court were not clearly erroneous and were not to be disturbed on appeal, Alaska R. Civ. P. 52(a). The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that declared Alaska Stat. § 36.10.010 violated the privileges and immunities clause of U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that declared that the local hire law was unconstitutional.

 

170 of 606 DOCUMENTS


In re W.E.G.


Nos. S-777, S-778, and S-803; 2998


Supreme Court of Alaska


710 P.2d 410; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 324



December 6, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Jay Hodges, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foster parents challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which allowed the foster parents to adopt two children placed in their care, but which did not terminate the children's relationship with appellee biological grandparents.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, in pertinent part, the court reversed the trial court's ruling that Alaska Stat. § 25.23.130(a) was unconstitutional as applied to the case because it did not provide adopted children with equal protection of the laws. The court held that the trial court did not (1) indicate a discriminatory classification, (2) explain what protected right might be discriminatorily infringed, nor (3) present any analytical basis for the ruling. In addition, the court held that the trial court erred in excluding the grandparents from participating in the hearing regarding the foster parents' adoption petition. The court held that, had the grandparents been allowed to intervene, the court might not have found that the adoption by the foster parents was in the children's best interests. However, the court also held that in making the determination of what was in the children's best interest, the trial court could consider the length and quality of time spent by the children with their foster parents. As a result, the court vacated the trial court's ruling that § 25.23.130(a) was unconstitutional, vacated the adoption decree, and remanded the case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court after holding that the adoption statute at issue was not unconstitutional, vacated the adoption decree after holding that the grandparents should have been allowed to intervene, and remanded the case for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion.

 

171 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Stephan v. State


Nos. S-387, S-406


Supreme Court of Alaska


711 P.2d 1156; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 326



December 6, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petitions for Hearing from the Court of Appeals of the State of Alaska, on Appeal from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, J. Justin Ripley and Ralph E. Moody, Judges, File No. S-387; Victor D. Carlson, Judge, File No. S-406.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two defendants, who were convicted of criminal offenses in separate cases, challenged the admissibility of their confessions that had been taken in violation of a judicial rule requiring that the entire statement be recorded whenever feasible. The Court of Appeals (Alaska) acknowledged the violation but declined to adopt an exclusionary rule, and defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants' incriminating statements obtained during custodial interrogation were only partially recorded and they sought suppression because the police had violated the rule announced in an earlier decision requiring that statements be electronically recorded in full whenever feasible. Although no satisfactory explanations were given for the rule's violation, the statements were admitted and both defendants were convicted. The court held that defendants' due process rights under Alaska Const. art. I, § 7, were violated and suppression of the statements was required. Compliance with the recording rule was not unduly burdensome on the police, and the contention that the presence of operating recording equipment exerted a chilling effect on an accused's willingness to speak was entitled to little weight, given the constitutional right to silence. The integrity of the judicial system was promoted by determining the admissibility of confessions on the basis of objective evidence rather than the testimony of interested parties. Exclusion was warranted because defendants' ability to present their defenses was impaired by an arbitrary failure to record their statements in full.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that defendants' statements be suppressed, reversed the judgments, and remanded the cases for further proceedings.

 

172 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Roethler v. Lutheran Hosps. & Homes Soc'y


No. 2993


Supreme Court of Alaska


709 P.2d 487; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 319



November 15, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Gerald Van Hoomissen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband had filed a medical malpractice action against respondents, a doctor, an emergency association and a hospital. The husband sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which denied his motion for dismissal of an expert advisory panel or for permission to commence discovery.


OVERVIEW: The wife was admitted through the emergency room, complaining of pain to her mid-back and pelvis. The doctor who examined her had discharged her. She later died at home, in her husband's presence, due to a collapsed lung caused by the jagged edge of a fractured rib. The husband then sued the doctor, the emergency association, and the hospital. The defending parties then made a motion for appointment of an expert advisory panel pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 09.55.536. The husband had made a motion to dismiss the panel or for permission to commence discovery because the panel was not appointed within the term prescribed in § 09.55.536. The trial court had denied the motion. On appeal, the court held that the prohibition on discovery had to be lifted at the end of 80 days, whether or not the expert advisory panel had issued a report, assuming that the party wishing to begin discovery was not responsible for any significant delay. The court noted that the inclusion of time limits in § 09.55.536 indicated that the purpose was to encourage a prompt determination of the claims. The court concluded that the husband was entitled to dismiss the expert panel and to discovery.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that denied the husband's motion for dismissal of an expert advisory panel or for permission to commence discovery. The court allowed discovery after the expiration of the 80-day statutory deadline.

 

173 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Atlantic Richfield Co. v. State


File No. S-52; No. 2965


Supreme Court of Alaska


705 P.2d 418; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 295; 86 Oil & Gas Rep. 406



August 16, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Victor D. Carlson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant oil companies challenged the constitutionality of the Oil and Gas Corporate Income Tax (Oil Tax), Alaska Stat. § 43.21 (repealed 1982), and sought a refund of taxes paid under the Oil Tax to appellee State. The Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, rejected the oil companies' claims of unconstitutionality and granted the State's motion for summary judgment. The oil companies appealed.


OVERVIEW: Under the Oil Tax, separate accounting was implemented to calculate how much of the oil companies' production and pipeline transportation income was subject to State taxation. The oil companies argued that the State, as a matter of constitutional law, had to use the formula apportionment method, and that the Oil Tax was not separate accounting. The court held that the Oil Tax operated as a separate accounting system for dividing income, distinct from both the specific allocation by situs and formula apportionment methods and that accurate and fair allocation was the legislature's goal in enacting the Oil Tax. The court held that separate accounting did not violate the due process, commerce, contract, or equal protection clauses of the federal Constitution or the equal protection clause of the State constitution. The court held that, because two-thirds of the legislature voted to make the Oil Tax Act immediately effective, as required by Alaska Const. art. II, § 18, Alaska Stat. § 01.10.070(a), a separate two-thirds vote was not required to make the Oil Tax retroactive. The approval of 11 senators was sufficient.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's action in granting the state's motion for summary judgment.

 

174 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Abood v. Gorsuch


File No. S-706; No. 2958


Supreme Court of Alaska


703 P.2d 1158; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 285



August 2, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, members of the House of Representatives (representatives), sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Third Judicial District (Alaska), which granted summary judgment to appellee state officials in the representatives' action for declaratory judgment. The representatives had challenged the state legislature's confirmation of the officials who had been appointed by the governor.


OVERVIEW: The representatives denied the confirmation of the officials by adjourning the legislature. The senate did not concur in the adjournment, and the governor issued a widely disseminated proclamation calling for a joint session at which the confirmations were to proceed. The confirmations were objected to for lack of a quorum. The confirmations were affirmed. The representatives filed suit and the trial court dismissed the case, finding nonjusticiability. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding first that the nonjusticiability doctrine did not apply to the issue of a quorum because that question was a question of constitutional law. The court then held that joint sessions of the legislature were intended to organize the legislature into a unicameral session, and the quorum required was only that of a majority of the members of the legislature. Conversely, whether the legislative rules had been violated at the joint session was a nonjusticiable question because it arose out of the legislature's rulemaking powers. Finally, the court held that there was reasonable notice given by the governor as required by the constitution, because the notice was immediately published and distributed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which gave effect to the confirmation of state officials by a joint session of the legislature despite the absence and objection by members of the house of representatives.

 

175 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Copper River Sch. Dist. v. State


No. S-488


Supreme Court of Alaska


702 P.2d 625; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 272



June 28, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian C. Shortell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Anchorage, which dismissed the school district's action seeking an injunction and a declaratory judgment against appellee Alaska Department of Education. The district brought suit seeking to enjoin the collection of allegedly overpaid funds after the Department failed to hold a requested hearing within the 30-day period allowed.


OVERVIEW: The Department funded the district's special education based on the average daily membership of special education students in attendance. The Department's audit division determined that the Department overpaid the district and requested repayment. The district requested a hearing pursuant to Alaska Admin. Code tit. 4, § 40.040(a). The Department failed to respond to the request during the 30-day period provided. Subsequently, the district declined to attend a scheduled hearing, arguing that the Department's jurisdiction had expired. The district brought suit for an injunction and a declaratory judgment against the Department. The trial court dismissed the action. On appeal, the court held that: (1) the Department was not guilty of laches where its audit division notified the district in September 1982 of its initial findings with respect to the 1980-1981 school year and the district failed to demonstrate prejudice thereby; and (2) the 30-day deadline of § 40.040(a) was directory, the Department's slight delay in responding to the district's request was substantial compliance, and the trial court properly upheld the Department's action where the district did not show prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision from the trial court, which dismissed the district's action for an injunction and a declaratory judgment.

Norton v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Bd.


File No. 7363, No. 2915


Supreme Court of Alaska


695 P.2d 1090; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 243



March 1, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Douglas J. Serdahely, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant liquor license transferees sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which reversed a decision of appellee Alaska Alcoholic Beverage Control Board to permit the transferees to assume a liquor license.


OVERVIEW: The transferees sold their business and included a liquor license. The buyers executed a security agreement on the property and gave the transferees power to retransfer the liquor license to themselves if the buyers defaulted. As a result of the buyers' accumulated debt, the transferees declared a default and applied to retransfer the license. The Board ultimately found that newly enacted Alaska Stat. § 04.11.360(4)(B), which repealed and replaced Alaska Stat. § 04.10.330(b), permitted such a transfer while the former statute did not. The trial court reversed. On appeal, the court held that: (1) application of Alaska Stat. § 04.11.360(4)(B) would give pre-enactment conduct a different legal effect from that which it would have had absent enactment of the statute, (2) § 04.11.360(4)(B) did not expressly declare its retrospective operation, and (3) the trial court properly refused to apply § 04.11.360(4)(B) retrospectively because to do so would violate the prohibition of Alaska. Stat. § 01.10.090 against retrospective application of statutes without express declarations of retrospectivity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

Kalmakoff v. Commercial Fisheries Entry Comm'n


File No. 7767, No. 2900


Supreme Court of Alaska


693 P.2d 844; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 228



January 11, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fisher challenged a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District that affirmed the denial by appellee, the Commercial Fisheries Entry Commission, of his application for a limited entry permit for a salmon fishery pursuant to the Limited Fisheries Entry Act, Alaska Stat. §§ 16.43.010-16.43.010. A hearing officer rejected the fisher's claim for points for gear ownership and for income dependence.


OVERVIEW: Under the Act, the fisher needed 20 points to qualify for a limited entry permit for the fishery. The Commission only awarded 17 points and the fisher challenged the ruling on the ground that he should have been awarded points for gear ownership and for income dependence. Pursuant to Alaska Admin. Code tit. 20, § 05.630(b)(3)(C), the fisher's gear ownership claim depended upon his alleged ownership of a usable purse seine, including a usable net, on January 1, 1973. The income dependence claim depended on his assertion that he had been a de facto gear license holder during 1972, and that this entitled him to income dependence points under the Commission's regulations and interpretive decisions. Alaska Admin. Code tit. 20, § 05.630(b)(1). On appeal, the court ruled that the hearing officer's findings on the gear ownership claim were not supported by substantial evidence. The court also ruled that the hearing officer's findings on income dependence were supported by substantial evidence. Furthermore, the denial of income dependence points violated neither Alaska Stat. § 16.43.010(a), nor the Equal Protection Clause of the Alaska Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Commission's decision and the lower court's affirmance of the decision that the fisher was not entitled to income dependence points. The court reversed the decision that the fisher was not entitled to points for gear ownership and remanded to the Commission for a new hearing.

 

178 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Hayes v. Charney


File Nos. 7859, 7860, No. 2896


Supreme Court of Alaska


693 P.2d 831; 1985 Alas. LEXIS 225



January 4, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Walter L. Carpeneti, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant legislators sought review of a judgment entered by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, which dismissed their action against appellees, a media company and various state officials, on that ground that it presented no justiciable questions.


OVERVIEW: The Legislative Council awarded a contract to provide television coverage of the 1983 Legislative Session even monies had not been appropriated for that purpose. The legislators commenced an action against the media company and state officials. Their complaint alleged that the Council had not complied with the Open Meetings Act, Alaska Stat. § 44.62.310, and had usurped legislative authority. They sought to enjoin performance of the contract. The trial court dismissed the legislators' complaint for want of a justiciable question. The legislators appealed from that judgment, which was affirmed. The court noted that the 1983 Legislative Session had been completed. While the doctrine of mootness was, in general, inappropriate in cases that involved the Act, the court applied the mootness doctrine because the Council was subject to the sole direction and control of the legislature. Furthermore, the legislature possessed the direct authority to cure the alleged violation and to prevent future violations. Therefore, the court stated that the legislators' complaint was not of such significant public interest that it warranted review despite its mootness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which had dismissed the legislators' complaint agianst the state officials and media company.

 

179 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Wood v. Superior Court


File No. 7386, No. 2884


Supreme Court of Alaska


690 P.2d 1225; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 362



October 30, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Ralph E. Moody, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, which held the attorney in contempt of court for refusing to accept an appointment to represent an indigent defendant in a criminal proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was appointed to represent an indigent defendant under a rule that required the private bar to accept assignments that the public defender was unable to accept due to a conflict of interest. The attorney refused the appointment, claiming he was not competent in criminal matters and the defendant's right to effective assistance of counsel would be impaired. The trial court found the attorney in contempt of court and ordered the attorney to pay a fine. The attorney sought review. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's decision, holding that the courts were authorized to compel an attorney to accept indigent representation and to punish the attorney with contempt of court if he refused, unless the attorney proved that he was incompetent to handle the case. The court also ruled that forcing the attorney to pay for defense expenses or forcing an incompetent attorney to pay for a replacement was an unconstitutional taking. The court also held that the attorney was entitled to a jury trial on the charge of contempt.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision to hold the attorney in contempt and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

180 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Deubelbeiss v. Commercial Fisheries Entry Comm'n


File No. 6494, No. 2881


Supreme Court of Alaska


689 P.2d 487; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 358



October 12, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, Roy H. Madsen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fisherman sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Kodiak, which affirmed appellee Commercial Fisheries Entry Commission's (CFEC) refusal to grant the fisherman a hearing to challenge the denial of a fishing permit.


OVERVIEW: The fisherman was denied a salmon drift gillnet fishing limited entry permit because he failed to obtain the required 16 points under Alaska Admin. Code tit. 20, § 05.630(b)(4). The CFEC refused to grant him a hearing. The superior court affirmed the CFEC's position. On appeal, the court reversed. The regulation was unconstitutional because the use of census districts did not bear a fair and substantial relation to the determination of the availability to an applicant of alternative occupations. The record provided no justification for the CFEC's decision to use census districts. Persons such as the fisherman were automatically allocated fewer points for availability of alternative occupations because they were located in one of the more populous census districts, in spite of their residence in a small isolated coastal town dependent upon fishing. The classification was in this respect impermissibly underinclusive. And because persons who were located within less populous census districts were automatically awarded the maximum point allotment, despite their proximity to a major urban center across the census district boundary, the classification was impermissibly overinclusive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment and remanded the matter to the CFEC for further proceedings.

 

181 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Haley


File Nos. 6604, 6608, 6609, 6610, 2860


Supreme Court of Alaska


687 P.2d 305; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 337



August 10, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Thomas B. Stewart and Thomas E. Schulz, Judges.



DISPOSITION:  For the foregoing reasons, the judgment of the superior court is AFFIRMED, except as to the award of attorney's fees, for a redetermination of which this case is REMANDED for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and managers, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which found that appellee employee was dismissed from her employment in violation of her right to free expression for stating her views during a television interview. The employee cross-appealed her attorney fee award.


OVERVIEW: The employee was terminated from her position as a legislative researcher after expressing her views about multi-national corporations on a television program. The employee brought an action against the state and her former managers for wrongful termination in violation of her right to free expression. The superior court ruled in favor of the employee but denied her attorney's fees. On review, the court affirmed the superior court judgment but remanded the case for a redetermination of attorney's fees because the court found that the employee was the prevailing party under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The state asserted that the employee was under a contract that required her remain non-partisan. However, the court found this contract to be a prior restraint and any system of prior restraint of expression came to the court bearing a heavy presumption against its constitutional validity. The court concluded that the mere possibility of a loss of legislative confidence for the employee's statements was insufficient to meet the material and substantial disruption requirement. Thus, to the extent that the employee's firing was based on her past statements, it was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court in favor of the employee but remanded the case for a redetermination as to the award of attorney's fees.

Alaska Transp. Comm'n v. Airpac


File No. 7587, No. 2854


Supreme Court of Alaska


685 P.2d 1248; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 334



July 27, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Alaska Transportation Commission challenged a decision from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which reversed the Commission's ruling that appellee air taxi had maintained a supplemental base of operations at Anchorage without authorization in violation of Alaska Stat. § 02.05.050(a)(5). The trial court held that only a base of operations, not a supplemental base, had to be authorized.


OVERVIEW: The Commission authorized the air taxi to maintain two bases, but it never authorized one in Anchorage. The Commission argued that the reasonable basis test had to be used, while the air taxi argued that the independent judgment test applied because the issue was statutory construction. The court agreed with the air taxi, declining to defer to the agency, but it was persuaded by the Commission's argument that if the legislature only intended to regulate bases, there was no need to define supplemental bases; therefore, the air taxi's analysis made those superfluous. The court held that supplemental bases also required approval; however, it found that the Commission's proposition that they included any location other than an approved base, including where an air taxi flew to after leaving its base, violated commercial free speech. The court concluded that the air taxi had a valid operating certificate and was authorized for air commerce between its base and any in-state location, but the substantial interest test had not been met to limit the air taxi's right to solicit customers to return to its base, and restricting that right inhibited economical and efficient service.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting the air taxi summary judgment, finding that it had not created an unauthorized supplemental base.

Supreme Court of Alaska

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant senator sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage that denied his motion to quash the subpoena of appellee representatives.


OVERVIEW: A senator sought to quash a subpoena that required him to appear and give testimony at a deposition noticed by representatives. The senator contended he had legislative immunity. The representatives challenged the legality of a joint session that was held. The court elected to treat the senator's motion as an original application for relief. The court determined the senator's conversations with the governor were acts in preparation for the performance his duties as a senator, thus they were privileged. The representative suggested there should be an exception to the rule of privilege for unconstitutional behavior in preparation for legislative acts, which did not involve criminal conduct. The court declined to adopt this suggestion. The representative contended that even if the conversations were privileged the senator who was not a party in the underlying action, could not assert privilege. The court concluded the senator could not be compelled to give testimony in the matter at issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court denying the senator's motion to quash the subpoena filed by the representatives.

Valdez v. 18.99 Acres

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Milton M. Souter, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of the Sate of Alaska which affirmed the decision of an arbitration panel in an arbitration with appellees property owners in valuing certain property which was to be condemned.


OVERVIEW: A city decided to build a port on a certain piece of property. When negotiations with the property owners fell through, both sides agreed to arbitration. Using certain economic and feasibility studies commissioned by the city, the panel rendered a decision favorable to the property owners. The city sought review and the trial court affirmed the decision of the panel. Again, the city sought review and the court held that the use by the arbitrators of the studies commissioned by the city was a valid exercise of their fact finding function. Moreover, the court held that the fact that the studies increased the value of the property was not reason to preclude the owners from the benefit of that enhanced value. The panel correctly assessed the value of the property leased from the city and the owners' obligation to the Army Corps of Engineers to restore the property to its condition before the owners had applied for permission to build a barge dock. The court also affirmed the finding that Alaska Stat. § 09. 55.44(a) unconstitutionally fixed the pre- and post judgment interest rate in "quick take" condemnations at six percent. The order of the trial court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the finding that the panel of arbitrators was correct in ruling in favor of the property owners.

Schafer v. Vest


File No. S-289, No. 2824


Supreme Court of Alaska


680 P.2d 1169; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 296



April 27, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Juneau, Walter L. Carpeneti, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and Administrator of the Alaska Longevity Bonus Program, sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of the First Judicial District, Juneau (Alaska), which entered judgment for appellee resident in his action to have the residency requirement of the Program, codified at Alaska Stat. §§ 47.45.010 - 47.45.170, declared unconstitutional as violative of the Equal Protection Clause.


OVERVIEW: The resident brought an action to have the residency requirement of the Program, codified at §§ 47.45.010 - 47.45.170, declared unconstitutional under the Equal Protection Clause. The resident, who missed the residency qualification based upon Alaska's date of statehood by three months, sought severance of the residency requirement from the rest of the Program. The superior court entered judgment for the resident by finding that the Program violated the Equal Protection Clause but refused to sever the residency requirement from the rest of the Program and enjoined appellants from enforcing the Program. Appellants sought review and the court affirmed, finding that the state's objective of rewarding citizens for past contributions was not a legitimate state purpose. The Constitution could not tolerate the state's benefit program that created fixed, permanent distinctions between concededly bona fide residents based on how long they were in the state because it created a an exclusive class of residents entitled to receive special benefits. The court concluded that rewarding residents for past contributions was not a legitimate state purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that found the Program unconstitutional.

 

186 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Williams


File No. 6025, No. 2817


Supreme Court of Alaska


681 P.2d 313; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 291



April 13, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals, on Appeal from the Superior Court, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Ralph E. Moody, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged the constitutionality of Alaska R. Crim. P. 45, the speedy trial rule, and sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Alaska), which determined that the State had violated the Rule 45 in bringing charges against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with tampering with physical evidence and the trial court dismissed the indictment. The State sought review of the trial court's order and the appellate court determined that the charges were properly dismissed because the State had violated Alaska R. Crim. P. 45, which provided that defendant had to be tried within a certain period of time. On further appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The State argued that Rule 45 was unconstitutional because it violated the doctrine of separation of powers. The court held that the additional protections conferred upon defendant by Rule 45 were justified because they were incidental to the efficient implementation of the constitutional right to a speedy trial. The court further held that Rule 45 furthered the "social interest" in ensuring that crimes were prosecuted expeditiously and was therefore within the rule-making authority of the judiciary. The court found that the appellate court did not consider the State's contention that the 94 of the 120 days provided for in Rule 45 had expired by the time defendant was indicted and therefore remanded the cause for an evaluation of the State's calculations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order to the extent that it held that the speedy trial was constitutional. The court reversed the order to the extent that it held that the State violated the speedy trial rule in bringing charges against defendant for tampering with physical evidence. The court remanded the cause for an evaluation of the State's calculations on the time period under the speedy trial rule.

Burrell v. Burrell

CASE SUMMARY:

The court ruled that the husband was time-barred from requesting modification of the terms of the property settlement under Alaska R. Civ. P. 60(b).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that declined to dismiss the wife's action for an interest in payments received by the husband as a trust beneficiary. Alaska Pac. Assurance Co. v. Brown


File Nos. 6600, 6626, No. 2789


Supreme Court of Alaska


687 P.2d 264; 1984 Alas. LEXIS 318



February 17, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Granted in Part and Denied in Part July 20, 1984; As Modified July 20, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Milton M. Souter, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured employee challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which entered an order in favor of the appellee employer's insurer in the injured employee's class action alleging that Alaska Stat. § 23.30.175(d) had violated federal and state equal protection and due process guarantees, and the Privileges and Immunities and Commerce Clauses of the federal Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The injured employee had received temporary disability benefits under the Alaska Workers' Compensation Act. After the injury, the injured employee had returned to his home in California, and his benefits were adjusted under Alaska Stat. § 23.30.175(d). If he had remained in Alaska, he would have received $ 551.86 per week. Under the adjustment provision, however, his benefits were reduced to $ 211.91 per week. The lower court declared § 23.30.175(c)-(f) unconstitutional under Alaska's Equal Protection Clause. The court affirmed that portion of the lower court's decision striking down the adjustment provision, but reversed with respect to the employer's insurer's liability for damages. The court held that § 23.30.175(d) imposed a substantial penalty upon the exercise by the injured employee and the class of the right to travel out of Alaska. The court concluded that private entities who regulated their behavior in good faith compliance with a validly enacted law could not by the fact of their compliance be held legally responsible for constitutional defects in the law. The court held that the award of damages against the insurer could not be sustained.


OUTCOME: The court held that the award of damages against the employer's insurer could not be sustained in the injured employee's class action. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the lower court's judgment.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, which ruled in favor of appellee neighbors in a dispute over access to a private road.

The court also noted that the only statutory remedy for failure to comply with the subdivision requirements was a fine under Alaska Stat. § 29.33.190.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court as to the non-complying neighbors and affirmed the judgment of the trial court against the landowner.

Williford v. State


No. 5986


Supreme Court of Alaska


674 P.2d 1329; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 516



November 10, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Hearing from the Court of Appeals, on Appeal from the District Court, Third Judicial District, Kenai, Jess Nicholas, Magistrate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision by the Court of Appeals (Alaska), which affirmed her conviction for operating a motor vehicle while intoxicated in violation of Alaska Stat. § 28.35.030(a).


OVERVIEW: Prior to driving, defendant had consumed an alcoholic beverage, an antibiotic, and possibly, a prescription painkiller. Defendant asserted that Alaska Stat. § 28.35.030(a)(3) was unconstitutionally vague in its use of the terms "combined influence" and "another substance." On appeal, the court reversed, concluding that the term "combined influence" was not vague, as it was clear that whatever was combined with alcohol had to be a contributing factor in causing intoxication. Although the court of appeals deemed that "substance" was synonymous with "drug," the court found that "substance" was a much broader term, and was unconstitutionally vague as it gave no notice as to what substances combined with alcohol were prohibited. While the State argued that § 28.35.030(a)(3) was encompassed by § 28.35.030(a)(1), the court held that such an argument would render § 28.35.030(a)(3) superfluous, which would be against legislative intent. Because it could not be determined under which subsection defendant was convicted based on the jury instructions given, the court remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and remanded for a new trial.

In re Walton


No. 6289


Supreme Court of Alaska


676 P.2d 1078; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 522



September 30, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Granted in Part and Opinion Amended January 3, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Disciplinary Board of the Alaska Bar Association.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent attorney sought review of the decision from the Disciplinary Board of the Alaska Bar Association, which suspended the attorney from the practice of law for 18 months after the attorney allegedly "created" a document, a copy of which he later attached as an exhibit to an unverified complaint.


OVERVIEW: The Disciplinary Board of the Alaska Bar Association (Board) suspended attorney from the practice of law for 18 months after the attorney allegedly "created" a document, a copy of which he later attached, as an exhibit, to an unverified complaint. On appeal, the court held that the Alaska Bar Rules clearly afforded the attorney an opportunity to be heard by the Board. Furthermore, since the attorney was on notice that the Board might depart from the Hearing Committee's findings and recommendations, whether or not there was an appeal, the attorney waived his right to be heard, by entering into the stipulation. The court could not conclude, as the attorney asked it to do, that his interest in practicing law and in his reputation was so much more substantial than the public's interest in a fairly and accurately functioning judicial system that it should adopt a standard of proof which put the risk of an incorrect factual determination on the public. The court rejected the attorney's position that proof by clear and convincing evidence was required as a matter of federal due process. The court concluded that the findings of the Board were supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the findings and recommendations of the Disciplinary Board of the Alaska Bar Association (Board), which suspended the attorney from the practice of law for 18 months. The findings of the Board, in all material respects, were supported by the evidence.

Department of Revenue v. Alaska Pulp Am.


Nos. 6583, 6594


Supreme Court of Alaska


674 P.2d 268; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 508



September 30, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, Sitka, Duane Craske, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both appellant and cross-appellee Alaska Department of Revenue (department) and appellees and cross-appellants, corporation and its subsidiary corporations, sought review of an order by the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, First Judicial District, which found that the three-year statute of limitations to collect taxes under Alaska Stat. § 43.05.260(a), barred the collection of the assessed taxes, except to three of the corporations.


OVERVIEW: In 1978 the Department assessed taxes under the Alaska Business License Act (ABLA) against the corporation and six of its subsidiaries for taxes on their gross receipts for the years 1971 through 1974. In May 1976 Alaska Stat. § 43.05.260(a), established a three year statute of limitations for the assessment of taxes and was made retroactive to January 1, 1976. The corporation and it subsidiaries maintained that § 43.05.260(a) applied retroactively to bar the assessments. The trial court held that § 43.05.260(a) barred the assessments except as to three of the corporations. On appeal, the court held that: (1) section 43.05.260(a) was not applied retroactively and the three-year statute of limitations did not bar the assessments against the corporations and its subsidiaries; (2) the ABLA did not exempt affiliated corporations from the tax on gross receipts; (3) in the ABLA the term manufactured or processed did not include the cutting or sorting of logs; and (4) the commerce and import-export clauses of the federal constitution did not prohibit the Department's taxation of domestic international sales corporations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the part of the trial court's order that held that the statute of limitations barred the assessed taxes. The court affirmed the part of the trial court's order that found that the statute of limitations did not bar the assessed taxes against three of the corporations.
