KUHN v. STATE


File Nos. 6833, 7080; No. 2710


SUPREME COURT OF ALASKA


1983 Alas. LEXIS 526



August 5, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the Sate of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Warren Taylor, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured person sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, which entered judgement dismissing his negligence action against defendant state for personal injuries and property damage arising from a single-vehicle accident he was involved in while driving his semi-tractor on the Dalton Highway.


OVERVIEW: An injured person was operating a semi-trailer on the Dalton Highway in December. The Dalton Highway was closed to the public after September 1, and the semi-trailer was being operated by virtue of a permit under Alaska Stat. § 42.10. Under § 42.10, an injured person must indemnify and hold harmless the state for any incidents occurring on the highway. Accordingly, the trial court dismissed the injured person's complaint. On review, the court affirmed the grant of summary judgment, but reversed as to attorneys fees. The court held: (1) the indemnification and hold harmless agreements were valid against the injured person, even though he did not sign the permit under Alaska Admin. Code tit. 17, § 30.050; (2) whether deliberately or not, the injured person's employer purported to act as the injured person's agent when its dispatcher signed the permit, and the injured person was bound to the terms of the permit, including the hold harmless language; (4) the "public duty" exception did not apply to invalidate an otherwise valid law; and (5) the contract was not one of adhesion; and (5) the state was entitled to attorneys fees under the indemnity clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that entered judgment dismissing the injured person's negligence action against the state for personal injuries and property damage arising from a single-vehicle accident that the injured person was involved in on the Dalton Highway. However, the court reversed, and remanded the case to the trial court for a determination of attorneys fees.

 

194 of 606 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ostrosky


Nos. 6336, 6373


Supreme Court of Alaska


667 P.2d 1184; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 452



July 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended July 21, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Victor D. Carlson, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Alaska sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which granted defendants' petition for relief from their convictions for illegal possession of commercially caught fish and illegal commercial fishing.


OVERVIEW: In its ruling, the superior court held that the transferability provisions of the Limited Entry Act (Act), Alaska Stat. §§ 16.43.010-16.43.380, violated Alaska Const. art. VIII, § 15, prohibiting exclusive rights of fishery, and Alaska Const. art. I, § 1, guaranteeing all persons equal rights and opportunities. Upon review the supreme court held that the Act did not violate those provisions of the Alaska Constitution, and reversed the judgment of the superior court granting post-conviction relief to defendants. Exclusion from participation as a gear license holder in a fishery was not more violative of the common use or the no exclusive right of fishery clauses because it was based on one's financial rather than physical standing, or on the laws of chance. Each of the objectives identified by the state as purposes of free transferability was discussed in the extensive legislative hearings preceding passage of the Act. Further, the objectives were legitimate and fairly and substantially furthered by free transferability. Therefore, the equal protection clause of the Alaska Constitution was not violated by the transfer provisions of the Act.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the superior court's granting of defendants' motion for post-conviction relief, and held that the Limited Entry Act did not violate the provisions of the Alaska Constitution prohibiting exclusive rights of fishery and guaranteeing all persons equal rights and opportunities.

 

195 of 606 DOCUMENTS


United States Jaycees v. Richardet


Nos. 5889, 5890


Supreme Court of Alaska


666 P.2d 1008; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 446



May 27, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Milton Souter, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff woman, as representative of a female class, filed an action against defendant organization, contending that the organization's by-laws excluding women from full membership violated Alaska Const. art. I, §§ 1 and 3, and Alaska Stat. § 18.80.230. The Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, rejected the constitutional claims, but held that the by-laws violated § 18.80.230. The parties filed cross-appeals.


OVERVIEW: The organization contended that the trial court erred in holding that the exclusion of women from full membership violated § 18.80.230. The court agreed, and reversed that portion of the order, holding that Alaska Stat. § 18.80.300(7) should not have been construed to encompass the organization. The court reasoned that the organization was not a "public accommodation" within the meaning of § 18.80.300(7). The court said that § 18.80.300(7) defined "public accommodation" in terms of a "place." Accordingly, the court construed § 18.80.300(7) as applying exclusively to places of public accommodation. The court said that because the organization was not a place, it did not fall within the prohibition of Alaska Stat. § 18.80.230. On cross-appeal, the woman challenged the trial court's holding that the organization's membership policies did not violate the Alaska Constitution. The court agreed with the trial court, holding that the nexus between the state and the challenged action of the private organization was insufficient to satisfy the "state action" requirement. Accordingly, the court affirmed the trial court's order, which held that the membership policies were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the trial court's order, which held that the organization's membership policies violated the public accommodations' statute. The court affirmed that portion of the order, which dismissed the woman's constitutional claims.

Department of Labor, Wage & Hour Div. v. University of Alaska


No. 5942


Supreme Court of Alaska


664 P.2d 575; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 428; 98 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,409



May 20, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, Fairbanks, Jay Hodges, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state department of labor challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Fourth Judicial District, which granted declaratory and injunctive relief by summary judgment against the department's wage and hour division after declaring that the department lacked subject matter jurisdiction to hear a purported wage claim brought against a university. The department was ordered not to prosecute the wage claim.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the superior court's grant of summary declaratory judgment, which dismissed the claim filed with the department, and enjoined the department from prosecuting the claim. The court held that the power to accept an assignment of and to investigate and prosecute a wage claim did not include the power to deal with a claim that an employer unconstitutionally, illegally, arbitrarily and capriciously failed to increase an employee's salary. Because the employee did not seek to recover wages due him, but rather claimed that he was wrongfully denied a reclassification and a salary increase, the court held that the department could not prosecute the employee's claim. The court held that because the jurisdiction of the department was questioned on persuasive grounds, an exception to the requirement that the university exhaust all of its administrative remedies existed. The court held that the burden on the university and the apparent doubt about the department's jurisdiction justified the superior court's grant of judicial relief without requiring further administrative proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's decision, which granted summary declaratory judgment, dismissed the claim filed with the department, and enjoined the department from prosecuting the claim of the employee.

Supreme Court of Alaska


663 P.2d 932; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 416

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended May 17, 1983.

198 of 606 DOCUMENTS


Chevron U.S.A., Inc. v. Le Resche


Nos. 6396, 6648


Supreme Court of Alaska


663 P.2d 923; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 415



April 29, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Mark Rowland, Judge Daniel A. Moore, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An appeal involved two cases. In the first, appellant oil companies challenged a decision of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which denied their summary judgment motion in an action against appellees, the Alaska Department of Natural Resources and various state officials, concerning the validity of several regulations. The other case involved the same issue, but the opposite result in superior court.


OVERVIEW: Each appeal involved a challenge to the validity of 11 Alaska Admin. Code §§ 96.210-96.240, regulations promulgated by the Alaska Department of Natural Resources. The regulations required persons who wished to explore for oil or gas on state lands to obtain a miscellaneous land use permit from the department. In exchange for the permit, explorers were required to submit certain data and information to the department. Two separate actions by oil companies sought the invalidation of §§ 96.210-96.240 and a permanent injunction against their enforcement. In one action, the court held the regulations valid, in the other, invalid. The oil companies appealed the first decision; the department appealed the second. On appeal, the court affirmed the first judgment and reversed the second. The court held that Alaska Stat. §§ 38.05.020(b)(1) and 38.05.180, considered together, implied authority to adopt the challenged regulations. The court also held that the regulations were procedurally valid, since there was reasonable notice that the proposed regulations might affect the petroleum industry's interests in the area of submission of test data.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court decision that denied summary judgment for the oil companies and held the regulations valid. The court reversed the trial court decision that granted summary judgment for the oil companies and held the regulations invalid.

Storrs v. State Medical Bd.


No. 6882


Supreme Court of Alaska


664 P.2d 547; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 435



April 29, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended May 4, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court, State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Brian Shortell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician challenged the judgment from the Superior Court, State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which affirmed a finding of incompetence by respondents, medical board and division of licensing, pursuant to Alaska Admin. Code tit. 12, § 40.970 (amended 1980, 1981). The superior court affirmed the revocation of the physician's license to practice medicine in accordance with Alaska Stat. § 08.64.330(b).


OVERVIEW: The physician contended that Alaska Admin. Code tit. 12, § 40.970 (amended 1980, 1981) was so ambiguous that it failed to give adequate notice of prohibited conduct. The court affirmed the judgment and found that the term "professional incompetence" was not so vague that a qualified practitioner would be deprived of fair notice of the minimum standard of competence required. The medical board was justified in meeting with the hearing officer pursuant to Alaska Stat. § 44.62.500(a) for two hours in a closed decision while the board considered the proposed decision. The physician argued that the hearing officer incorrectly applied the clear and convincing standard of proof in evaluating the evidence. The court concluded that the substantial evidence test was the correct standard to be used in the proceeding. However, the hearing officer filed almost thirty pages of detailed, accurate factual findings, and balanced, thoughtful conclusions, which were more than adequate to survive the substantial evidence test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment by the medical board and licensing division that revoked the physician's medical license. The court held that the record showed the proceedings to be thorough, comprehensive, and fair. The findings and conclusions were supported by substantial evidence, and the decision clearly had a reasonable basis.

Irby-Northface v. Commonwealth Elec. Co.


Nos. 7632, 7649


Supreme Court of Alaska


664 P.2d 557; 1983 Alas. LEXIS 436



April 29, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, Karl S. Johnstone, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lowest bidder sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the State of Alaska, Third Judicial District, Anchorage, which found for appellees, a second-lowest bidder and a third-lowest bidder, in their suit challenging a notice of intent by appellee Alaska Power Authority (APA) to award a contract to the lowest bidder. The third-lowest bidder cross-appealed the award of the contract to the second-lowest bidder.


OVERVIEW: The APA issued a notice of intent to award a contract for a construction project to the lowest bidder. The second-lowest bidder and the third-lowest bidder challenged the intended award. They contended that the lowest bidder did not qualify as an Alaska bidder under the Alaska bidder preference statute, Alaska Stat. § 37.05.230, because it was a joint venture that did not have an Alaska business license and did not maintain a place of business in Alaska for six months preceding its bid. They also argued that one of the lowest bidder's venturers would not qualify individually as an Alaska bidder. The superior court held that the lowest bidder was a nonresident bidder. On appeal, the court reversed. The court agreed with the APA's interpretation of § 37.05.230(5) as permitting a joint venture to qualify for preference if any of the venturers would qualify individually as an Alaska bidder. The court exercised its independent judgment and gave weight to the fact that the APA had consistently granted preference to joint ventures when one of the venturers qualified individually as an Alaska bidder. The lowest bidder qualified as an Alaska bidder where one of its venturers so qualified.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment.
